The Capable Agent and the Preponderating Factor
Sanusi presents the first proof for jabr, analyzing the capable agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the proof for determinism, specifically the division of the capable agent and the implications when preponderance depends on a cause.

Core Idea
Every preference for action over inaction requires a cause; if the cause is from outside, determinism follows.

The author begins by analyzing the condition of an agent who can perform an action. He distinguishes whether the agent can also omit the action. If the agent can omit, then the preference for acting over not acting must be explained. The argument then examines whether this preference has a cause or not, and what consequences each scenario entails. In this lesson, we will focus on the first branch: when the preference has a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the two main cases
Read the Arabic passage and identify the two initial divisions of the capable agent: one who can omit the action and one who cannot. Notice how the author frames the discussion.
The passage begins with 'الوجه الأول' to mark the first proof.
2
Trace the sub-branch of the cause
Focus on the scenario where the agent can omit. The preponderance either depends on a cause or not. Here we follow the cause-dependent branch. Note the two possibilities: cause from the agent or from another.
If the cause is from the agent, the same analysis recurs (regress). If from another, determinism is directly necessitated.
3
Understand the regress argument
Reflect on why a cause from the agent leads to a regress. The author says 'عاد التقسيم الأول فيه' meaning the same division applies again, leading to an infinite regress or ultimately to an external cause.
This shows that even if the cause originates with the agent, it still requires another cause, eventually leading to jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالوجه الأول : إن المتمكن من الفعل ، إما أن يكون متمكنا من تركه أولا يكون . فإن كان المتمكن من الفعل ، متمكنا من تركه . فرجحان جانب الفعل على جانب الترك ، إما أن يتوقف على مرجح ، أولا يتوقف . فإن توقف على مرجح ، فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، وإن كان من غيره ، فحينئذ يلزم الجبر ، وأما إن حصل ذلك الترجيح لا لمرجح ، أو يقال : إنه ترجح جانب الفعل على جانب الترك ، لا لمرجح أصلا . فهذا باطل لوجهين : الأول : إنه يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، وذلك يمنع من الاستدلال بحدوث المحدثات ، وبإمكان الممكنات على وجود الصانع . وإذا بطل القول بالصانع ، كان بطلان القول بالتكليف وبالنبوات لازما . الثاني : إن على هذا التقدير يكون رجحان جانب الفعل على جانب الترك : محض الاتفاق . وما يكون كذلك ، لم يكن الوقوع به ، أولى من الترك به . بل إن اتفق وقوعه ، فقد وقع . شاء ذلك الفاعل : ذلك الفعل ، أو أبي وإن لم يتفق وقوعه لم يقع ، شاء ذلك الفاعل : ذلك الفعل أو أبي . وهذا هو محض الجبر . فيثبت : أن رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر ، إن توقف على المرجح ، فالقول بالجبر لازم . وإن لم يتوقف ، فالقول بالجبر ألزم . فيثبت : أن القول بالجبر لازم على كل التقديرات. هذا إذا قلنا : إن المتمكن من الفعل متمكن أيضاً من الترك. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : المتمكن من الفعل غير متمكن من الترك ، فههنا الجبر أظهر وألزم . فيثبت : أن القول بالجبر لازم على كل الأقسام .
The first aspect: The one who is able to perform an action either is able to leave it or not. If the one able to perform an action is able to leave it, then the preponderance of the side of action over the side of non-action either depends on a preponderating factor (murajjih) or does not depend. If it depends on a preponderating factor, then that preponderating factor: if it is from the servant, then the first division recurs with respect to it; if it is from other than him, then determinism (jabr) necessarily follows. But if that preponderance occurs without a preponderating factor, or it is said that the side of action is preponderant over the side of non-action without any preponderating factor at all, then this is invalid for two reasons: First: It entails the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderating factor, and that prevents inferring the existence of the Maker from the occurrence of originated things and from the possibility of possible things. And if the doctrine of the Maker is invalid, then the invalidity of the doctrine of moral obligation and of prophethood is necessary. Second: On this assumption, the preponderance of the side of action over the side of non-action is pure coincidence. And whatever is like that, its occurrence is not more appropriate than its omission. Rather, if its occurrence happens to occur, it occurs whether the agent wills that action or not; and if its occurrence does not happen, it does not occur whether the agent wills that action or not. And this is pure determinism. So it is established that the preponderance of one of the two sides over the other, if it depends on a preponderating factor, then the doctrine of determinism is necessary; and if it does not depend, then the doctrine of determinism is even more necessary. So it is established that the doctrine of determinism is necessary in all cases. This is if we say that the one able to perform an action is also able to leave it. As for the second division, i.e., that it is said: the one able to perform an action is not able to leave it, here determinism is even more apparent and necessary. So it is established that the doctrine of determinism is necessary in all divisions.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof for the truth of jabr (determinism). The author considers a capable agent (mutamakkin min al-fi'l) and examines two possibilities: either the agent can omit the action or not. If the agent can omit it, then the preference for action over omission must have a cause (murajjih) or not. If it has a cause, that cause either comes from the agent (leading to regress) or from outside (which is jabr). If it has no cause, the preference is arbitrary, which is invalid for two reasons: (1) it undercuts arguments for the existence of God (since contingency would lack explanation), and (2) it reduces to pure coincidence, which is effectively jabr because the agent's will has no effect. Thus, jabr follows in both branches. The author then notes that if the agent cannot omit the action, jabr is even more obvious. The conclusion is that jabr is necessary in all cases.

Key Terms
متمكن
فعل
ترك
مرجح
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the preponderating factor comes from the agent, then determinism is avoided.
Correction: The author shows that even if the cause is from the agent, the same analysis applies to that cause, leading to an infinite regress. Ultimately, an external cause must be posited, which is determinism.

Checkpoint
According to the first part of the proof, what follows if the preponderating factor is from the agent?
A) Free will is established
B) The first division recurs
C) Determinism is directly necessitated
D) The action cannot occur

Key Takeaway
Whenever a capable agent prefers an action over inaction, the preponderance requires a cause; if the cause is from outside, determinism follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider one action you performed today that you consciously chose. Can you identify the preponderating factor behind it? If you trace it back, does it ultimately go beyond your control? If so, how does that affect your sense of moral responsibility before Allah?
?
Sanusi's proof concludes that determinism follows when the preponderating factor is external. Reflect on your own dua for guidance: do you believe Allah's response to your prayer is the external cause that determines your actions? If so, does that diminish your personal responsibility in seeking guidance?
?
The argument suggests an infinite regress if the cause of your action is your own will. How do you react to the idea that your will itself is not a final cause? Does this challenge your experience of making choices, and what does this tension reveal about your understanding of Allah's absolute will?

The No-Cause Branch: Why It Leads to Determinism
Sanusi refutes the no-cause alternative and concludes the first proof for jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the assumption that preference for action can occur without a preponderating factor is invalid and actually leads to determinism.

Core Idea
Without a cause, preference is arbitrary; this undermines proof of God and reduces to pure determinism.

After establishing that if preponderance depends on a cause, determinism follows, the author turns to the alternative: that preponderance occurs without any cause. He argues this is impossible for two compelling reasons. The first reason is theological: if contingency lacked explanation, the argument for God's existence would collapse. The second reason is logical: such preference would be pure coincidence, effectively making the agent powerless. In both cases, determinism is even more necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and locate the two reasons the author gives against the no-cause scenario. The first is introduced with 'الوجهين : الأول' and the second with 'الثاني'.
Each reason is a separate argument that shows the no-cause assumption leads to absurdity.
2
Understand the first objection: undermines proof of God
The author argues that if preference could occur without a cause, then the principle of sufficient reason is violated. This would block the inference from contingent things to a necessary Creator. He warns that this would also invalidate moral obligation and prophethood.
This reason shows the theological stakes: denying causality leads to atheism.
3
Understand the second objection: reduces to coincidence
The second reason: without a cause, the action's occurrence is pure coincidence. If so, the agent's will has no effect; the action happens or not regardless of the agent's desire. This is precisely determinism. The author then concludes that whether the preponderance has a cause or not, determinism is necessary.
The phrase 'وهذا هو محض الجبر' explicitly states this.
4
Reflect on the final conclusion
The author ends by considering the case where the agent cannot omit the action. He says determinism is even more apparent. Then he reiterates that determinism is necessary in all divisions.
This reinforces the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالوجه الأول : إن المتمكن من الفعل ، إما أن يكون متمكنا من تركه أولا يكون . فإن كان المتمكن من الفعل ، متمكنا من تركه . فرجحان جانب الفعل على جانب الترك ، إما أن يتوقف على مرجح ، أولا يتوقف . فإن توقف على مرجح ، فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، وإن كان من غيره ، فحينئذ يلزم الجبر ، وأما إن حصل ذلك الترجيح لا لمرجح ، أو يقال : إنه ترجح جانب الفعل على جانب الترك ، لا لمرجح أصلا . فهذا باطل لوجهين : الأول : إنه يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، وذلك يمنع من الاستدلال بحدوث المحدثات ، وبإمكان الممكنات على وجود الصانع . وإذا بطل القول بالصانع ، كان بطلان القول بالتكليف وبالنبوات لازما . الثاني : إن على هذا التقدير يكون رجحان جانب الفعل على جانب الترك : محض الاتفاق . وما يكون كذلك ، لم يكن الوقوع به ، أولى من الترك به . بل إن اتفق وقوعه ، فقد وقع . شاء ذلك الفاعل : ذلك الفعل ، أو أبي وإن لم يتفق وقوعه لم يقع ، شاء ذلك الفاعل : ذلك الفعل أو أبي . وهذا هو محض الجبر . فيثبت : أن رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر ، إن توقف على المرجح ، فالقول بالجبر لازم . وإن لم يتوقف ، فالقول بالجبر ألزم . فيثبت : أن القول بالجبر لازم على كل التقديرات. هذا إذا قلنا : إن المتمكن من الفعل متمكن أيضاً من الترك. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : المتمكن من الفعل غير متمكن من الترك ، فههنا الجبر أظهر وألزم . فيثبت : أن القول بالجبر لازم على كل الأقسام .
The first aspect: The one who is able to perform an action either is able to leave it or not. If the one able to perform an action is able to leave it, then the preponderance of the side of action over the side of non-action either depends on a preponderating factor (murajjih) or does not depend. If it depends on a preponderating factor, then that preponderating factor: if it is from the servant, then the first division recurs with respect to it; if it is from other than him, then determinism (jabr) necessarily follows. But if that preponderance occurs without a preponderating factor, or it is said that the side of action is preponderant over the side of non-action without any preponderating factor at all, then this is invalid for two reasons: First: It entails the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderating factor, and that prevents inferring the existence of the Maker from the occurrence of originated things and from the possibility of possible things. And if the doctrine of the Maker is invalid, then the invalidity of the doctrine of moral obligation and of prophethood is necessary. Second: On this assumption, the preponderance of the side of action over the side of non-action is pure coincidence. And whatever is like that, its occurrence is not more appropriate than its omission. Rather, if its occurrence happens to occur, it occurs whether the agent wills that action or not; and if its occurrence does not happen, it does not occur whether the agent wills that action or not. And this is pure determinism. So it is established that the preponderance of one of the two sides over the other, if it depends on a preponderating factor, then the doctrine of determinism is necessary; and if it does not depend, then the doctrine of determinism is even more necessary. So it is established that the doctrine of determinism is necessary in all cases. This is if we say that the one able to perform an action is also able to leave it. Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof for the truth of jabr (determinism). The author considers a capable agent (mutamakkin min al-fi'l) and examines two possibilities: either the agent can omit the action or not. If the agent can omit it, then the preference for action over omission must have a cause (murajjih) or not. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If preponderance has no cause, the agent is free because no external compulsion.
Correction: The author argues that no cause leads to pure coincidence, which means the agent's will is ineffective, resulting in determinism, not freedom.

Checkpoint
According to the second reason against the no-cause assumption, what does the action become?
A) A necessary act
B) Pure coincidence
C) A free choice
D) An impossible act

Key Takeaway
The no-cause assumption is invalid because it destroys the proof of God and reduces action to mere coincidence, which is determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you been acting as if your choices are just 'coincidence' – without a clear intention or cause behind them? How does Sanusi's argument that this leads to pure determinism challenge you to take responsibility for that area starting today?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by thinking 'it just happened' or 'I had no real choice'? This lesson shows that such thinking is actually determinism – not freedom. Will you tonight repent of one specific sin where you've used that excuse, and commit to seeing your actions as caused by your will and intention?
?
Sanusi says that denying a cause for preference destroys the proof of God. If you were to live as if your choices have no cause, how would that affect your worship and your sense of accountability to Allah? What will you do this week to remind yourself that every preference has a cause – and that cause ultimately points to the Maker?

First Shubhah: The Dilemma of Rational Moral Valuation
Introduces the first shubhah and its initial dilemma and argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first shubhah against obligation and prophethood, and the initial argument that benefit cannot return to God.

Core Idea
Neither affirming nor denying reason's moral judgment can save obligation from this shubhah.

This passage presents the first of several shubhahs (skeptical arguments) against the doctrine of divine obligation (taklif) and prophethood. The shubhah targets the very foundation of rational ethics: whether reason can independently judge actions as good or bad. The argument claims that on either answer, obligation is impossible. We will read the opening dilemma and the first part of their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: the shubhah sets up a dilemma then unpacks one branch.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off during the discussion of benefit.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two possibilities: either reason's judgment in moral valuation (tahseen wa taqbeeh) is considered or not. See how both are claimed to lead to the falsity of obligation and prophethood.
3
Trace the first argument
Under the first branch (if reason's judgment is considered), the shubhah argues that obligations either contain benefit or not. Then it argues that if they contain benefit, that benefit cannot return to God.
The argument lists three possible recipients of benefit: God, worshipper, or a third party. The passage covers only God's case.
4
Understand why benefit cannot be for God
The reason given is that God is transcendent (muta'ali) above benefit and harm, established by proof. Reflect on what this implies for obligation.
This is part of the shubhah, not the author's own view.
5
Anticipate the next steps
The argument pauses; consider what might be said about benefit returning to the worshipper or a third party. This incomplete argument will be completed in later passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تاير شبهات من يفكر التكليف لا بالبناء على مسألة الجبر. أعلم أن هذه الطائفة ذكروا الشبهات من وجوه: الشبهة الأولى: قالوا: إما أن يكون حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح معتبراً، وإما أن لا يكون. وعلى التقديرين، فالقول بالتكليف والنبوة باطل. أما بيان أن على تقدير إثبات تحسين العقل وتقبيحه كان القول بالتكليف باطلاً فمن وجوه: الحجة الأولى: قالوا: هذه التكاليف إما أن تكون مشتملة على فائدة ومصلحة وإما أن لا تكون، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بالتكليف. وإنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز القول بكونها مشتملة على الفائدة والمصلحة، فهو أن تلك الفائدة إما أن تكون عائدة إلى المعبود أو إلى العابد أو إلى ثالث يغايرهما. ولا يجوز أن تكون عائدة إلى المعبود لوجوه: الأول: إنه ثبت بالدليل: كونه متعالياً عن النفع والضر.
[The shubhahs of those who think about obligation not based on the issue of jabr?] Know that this group mentioned the shubhahs in several ways: The first shubhah: They said: Either the judgment of reason concerning tahseen and taqbeeh (moral valuation) is considered, or it is not. On both suppositions, the doctrine of obligation and prophethood is false. As for the explanation that on the supposition of affirming reason's moral valuation, the doctrine of obligation is false, it is from several aspects: The first argument: They said: These obligations either contain benefit and interest or they do not. Both divisions are false, so the doctrine of obligation is false. And we say: It is not permissible to say that they contain benefit and interest, because that benefit either returns to the worshipped, or to the worshipper, or to a third party distinct from them. And it is not permissible that it returns to the worshipped, for reasons: The first: It is established by proof that He is transcendent beyond benefit and harm.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage begins a new section presenting shubhahs from those who reject obligation without building on the jabr argument. The first shubhah poses a dilemma: either reason's judgment of good and bad (tahseen wa taqbeeh) is authoritative, or it is not. In both cases, the shubhah claims, obligation and prophethood are false. The author then elaborates on the first branch: if reason's judgment is accepted, the shubhah argues that obligation must be false. Their first argument is that obligations either contain benefit or they do not. If no benefit, they are pointless; if benefit, then that benefit must accrue to someone. The shubhah then argues it cannot be to God because He is transcendent and unaffected by benefit or harm. The text cuts off before completing the other possibilities (benefit to worshipper or third party).

Key Terms
تحسين وتقبيح
تكليف
نبوة
فائدة
مصلحة
متعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the shubhah's argument about benefit not returning to God is the author's own position.
Correction: This is part of a shubhah (skeptical argument) being presented for later refutation. The author does not endorse it.

Checkpoint
According to the first shubhah, why cannot obligations benefit God?
A) God does not need obedience.
B) It is established by proof that God is transcendent beyond benefit and harm.
C) Obligations are for human benefit only.
D) God already has everything.

Key Takeaway
The first shubhah against obligation uses a dilemma about rational ethics and then argues that obligations cannot benefit God, leaving the other cases unresolved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The shubhah argues that if reason can judge good and bad, then obligations become impossible. When you make a moral decision using your reason alone, do you feel that it is enough, or do you sense a need for divine guidance? How does this dilemma unsettle your confidence in your own ethical reasoning?
?
Consider a time you felt that your worship or obedience was 'benefiting' God. How does the passage's reminder that God is transcendent beyond benefit and harm challenge that feeling? Are you willing to let go of the idea that your acts add anything to God?
?
The shubhah presents a choice: either reason's judgment is authoritative or it is not, and both seem to lead to the falsehood of obligation. Have you ever felt trapped between two seemingly impossible options in your faith? What does it mean for you personally that this shubhah is presented as a challenge to be answered, not as the final word?

The First Doubt: Framework and Benefit to God
The author presents the overall doubt and the first part of the benefit argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first doubt against taklif and the three arguments why benefit cannot return to God.

Core Idea
The obligation cannot be for God's benefit because He is beyond need.

This passage introduces the first doubt against divine obligation (taklif) raised by opponents. It presents a dilemma: either the intellect's judgment of good and evil is considered or not. Focusing on the first horn, the argument examines whether obligations include benefit. The author then argues that benefit cannot possibly return to God, giving three reasons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the dilemma
Notice how the argument sets up a dilemma: either intellect is considered or not. On both, obligation is false. Then it focuses on the first horn and presents the benefit argument.
This is a classic kalam reductio.
2
Identify the three reasons against benefit to God
The passage lists three reasons why benefit cannot go to God. Find each one and understand the logic: transcendence, need implies imperfect power, and absurdity of God benefiting from weak actions.
The third reason uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion so far
The passage concludes that benefit to God is impossible. This sets up the next part regarding benefit to the worshipper.
The argument is incomplete at this stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى: قالوا: إما أن يكون حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح معتبراً، وإما أن لا يكون. وعلى التقديرين، فالقول بالتكليف والنبوة باطل. أما بيان أن على تقدير إثبات تحسين العقل وتقبيحه، كان القول بالتكليف باطلا فمن وجوه: الحجة الأولى: قالوا: هذه التكاليف، إما أن تكون مشتملة على فائدة ومصلحة، وإما أن لا تكون. والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بالتكليف. وإنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز القول بكونها مشتملة على الفائدة والمصلحة، فهو أن تلك الفائدة إما أن تكون عائدة إلى المعبود، أو إلى العابد، أو إلى ثالث يغايرهما. ولا يجوز أن تكون عائدة إلى المعبود، لوجوه: الأول: إنه ثبت بالدليل كونه متعالياً عن النفع والضر. والثاني: إن المحتاج لا يكون كامل القدرة، ومن كان كذلك لم يحصل الوثوق بوعده ووعيده. والثالث: إن البشر في غاية الضعف، وهذه العبادات أفعال قليلة وحركات ضعيفة، فلو كان الإله قد بلغ في الضعف إلى حيث ينتفع بهذه الحركات الخسيسة، فهو في الضعف والعجز في الغاية وذلك محال. وإنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز أن يقال إن تلك الفوائد عائدة إلى العابد لوجهين: الأول: إن جميع الفوائد محصورة في جلب المنافع ودفع المضار، والله تعالى قادر على تحصيلها بأسرها من غير واسطة هذه التكاليف، ولا تتفاوت حال القدرة على تحصيل هذه المطالب بسبب أن يأتي الإنسان بحركات معدودة. فإن كانت قدرته وحكمته تتفاوت بسبب هذه الأفعال الخسيسة الصادرة عن الإنسان، فهو في غاية الضعف. وإذا كان كذلك، كان توقيف إيصال تلك المنافع ودفع تلك المضار على هذه التكاليف عبثاً محضاً. والثاني: إن تلك المنافع الحاصلة من هذه الأفعال إما أن تحصل في الدنيا أو في الآخرة. والأول باطل لأنها في الدنيا محض التعب والكلفة والمشقة. وأما في الآخرة فبعيد أيضاً لأنه قادر على أن يدخلهم الجنة ويخلصهم من النيران من غير هذه الوسائط.
The first doubt: They said: Either the judgment of the intellect regarding good and evil is considered, or it is not. On both suppositions, the claim of obligation and prophecy is false. As for the demonstration that on the supposition of affirming the intellect's judgment of good and evil, the claim of obligation is false, it is from several aspects: The first proof: They said: These obligations, either they include benefit and interest, or they do not. Both divisions are false, so the claim of obligation is false. And we say: It is not permissible to say that they include benefit and interest, because that benefit either returns to the worshipped, or to the worshipper, or to a third entity distinct from them. It is not permissible that it returns to the worshipped, for reasons: First: It has been established by proof that He is exalted above benefit and harm. Second: The needy is not perfect in power, and whoever is such, his promise and threat are not trusted. Third: Humans are extremely weak, and these acts of worship are few actions and weak movements; so if the deity had reached such weakness that He benefits from these base movements, then He is at the ultimate limit of weakness and incapacity, and that is impossible. And we say: It is not permissible to say that those benefits return to the worshipper, for two reasons: First: All benefits are limited to bringing benefits and repelling harms, and Allah is able to achieve them all without the intermediary of these obligations, and the state of His power does not vary regarding achieving these goals by means of a human performing a few movements. For if His power and wisdom vary because of these base actions emanating from man, then He is in extreme weakness. And if that is the case, then making the delivery of those benefits and the repelling of those harms dependent on these obligations is sheer futility. Second: Those benefits resulting from these actions either occur in this world or in the Hereafter. The first is false because in this world they are pure fatigue, trouble, and hardship. As for the Hereafter, it is also far-fetched because He is able to cause them to enter Paradise and deliver them from the Fire without these intermediaries.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt (shubhah) against the concept of divine obligation (taklif) from the perspective of those who reject intellectualist ethics (likely the Ash'aris or even some philosophers, though the context suggests a critique of Mu'tazila). The argument proceeds as a dilemma: Either the intellect's judgment of good and evil is considered valid or not. In either case, the doctrines of obligation and prophethood are rendered false. The author then focuses on the first supposition—if intellect's judgment is considered—and gives a specific argument (al-hujja al-ūlā) focusing on the impossibility of benefit in obligation. The argument is structured as follows: Obligations must either include benefit or not. Both possibilities lead to absurdity, so obligation is false. The argument then tries to show that obligations cannot include benefit. If they did, the benefit would have to go to God, the worshipper, or a third party. Going to God is impossible for three reasons: (1) God is transcendent and above benefit and harm, proven by evidence. (2) Need implies deficiency in power, and a deficient being cannot inspire trust in promises and threats. (3) Humans are weak and their worship is minimal; if God were to benefit from such base actions, He would be supremely weak and incapable, which is impossible. Benefit to the worshipper is also impossible for two reasons: (1) All benefits are either bringing advantages or repelling harms, and God can do all that directly without the intermediary of these obligations. If God's power varied based on human actions, He would be weak, and then conditioning benefits on such actions would be purely futile (ʿabath). (2) The benefits of worship either occur in this world (but here they are pure hardship) or in the Hereafter—but God can directly grant Paradise and salvation without these intermediaries. Thus, no possible benefit, so obligation is baseless.

تكليف

مصلحة
معبود
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument denies that God can have any purpose in creation.
Correction: The argument is specifically about benefit (fāʾida) returning to God in obligations; it does not deny divine wisdom in creation.

Checkpoint
How many reasons does the author give for why benefit cannot return to the worshipped?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The first doubt argues that if obligation involved benefit, it cannot go to God because He is beyond need and imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes worship God hoping for a reward or fearing punishment, without considering that He is completely independent of your actions? How does this teaching challenge your intention?
?
If God does not need your worship, then why do you pray? Examine your heart: is your worship driven by genuine love and submission, or by a transactional expectation that God owes you something?
?
The argument shows that attributing need to God is absurd. Yet in your daily life, do you ever act as if God needs your defense or your reputation? How can you align your reverence with His absolute transcendence?

The First Doubt: Benefit to the Worshipper and Futility
The author presents two reasons why benefit cannot return to the worshipper, then concludes the benefit argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments against benefit returning to the worshipper and the conclusion that obligation is futile.

Core Idea
If benefit returned to the worshipper, God could give it directly, making obligation futile.

This passage continues the first doubt's benefit argument. Having shown that benefit cannot go to God, the author now examines whether it could go to the worshipper. He provides two reasons why this is also impossible, concluding that obligations cannot include any benefit and are therefore baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the two reasons against benefit to the worshipper
Find the two reasons: (1) God can directly give benefits without obligations, so conditioning them on worship is futile; (2) benefits of worship either are in this world (but worship is hardship) or in the Hereafter (but God can give Paradise directly).
Pay attention to the concept of futility (ʿabath).
2
Understand the logic of need and power
The first reason argues that if God's power varied based on human actions, He would be weak; but He is all-powerful, so making benefits dependent on worship is futile.
This ties into divine attributes.
3
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The passage ends by stating that God can directly grant Paradise and salvation without intermediaries, so obligations are unnecessary.
This completes the first doubt's first proof.

?

?

First Reason: God Can Achieve Benefits Without Worship
First argument against benefits returning to worshiper: God's direct power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against benefits returning to the worshiper: that God can attain all benefits directly, making their linkage to worship futile.

Core Idea
If God's power varied with human acts, He would be weak; linking benefits to worship is futile.

In this passage, the author presents the first of two reasons why it cannot be claimed that the benefits of religious obligations return to the worshiper. He argues that all benefits are either attaining good or warding off harm, and God can achieve all of them directly without the intermediary of worship. Therefore, making the delivery of benefits dependent on worship would be pure futility.

Guided Path
1
Read the first reason passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
Notice how the author uses a conditional statement to highlight the absurdity.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the premise that benefits are limited to achieving benefit and repelling harm, and that God is capable of all things without intermediaries.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the implication: if God's power depended on human actions, He would be weak. How does this support the conclusion of futility?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يقال : إن تلك الفوائد عائدة إلى العابد لوجهين : الأول : إن جميع الفوائد محصورة في جلب المنافع ، ودفع المضار ، والله تعالى قادر على تحصيلها بأسرها من غير واسطة هذه التكاليف . ولا تتفاوت حال القدرة على تحصيل هذه المطالب ، بسبب أن يأتي الإنسان بحركات معدودة . فإن كانت قدرته وحكمته تتفاوت بسبب هذه الأفعال الخسيسة الصادرة عن الإنسان، فهو في غاية الضعف . وإذا كان كذلك ، كان توقيف إيصال تلك المنافع ودفع تلك المضار - على هذه التكاليف - عبثاً محضاً. والثاني : إن تلك المنافع الحاصلة من هذه الأفعال إما أن تحصل في الدنيا أو في الآخرة . والأول باطل. لأنها في الدنيا محض التعب والكلفة والمشقة . وأما في الآخرة فبعيد أيضاً. لأنه قادر على أن يدخلهم الجنة ويخلصهم من النيران من غير هذه الوسائط .
And we said: it is not permissible to say that those benefits return to the worshiper, for two reasons: The first: that all benefits are confined to bringing benefits and repelling harms, and God Almighty is able to attain them all without the intermediary of these obligations. And the state of ability to attain these demands does not differ because the human performs counted movements. If His power and wisdom differed because of these base actions issuing from the human, then He is in extreme weakness. And if that is the case, then making the delivery of those benefits and repulsion of those harms dependent on these obligations is pure futility. The second: that those benefits resulting from these actions either occur in this world or in the Hereafter. The first is false, because in this world they are pure toil, burden, and hardship. As for the Hereafter, it is also farfetched, because He is able to admit them into Paradise and save them from the fires without these intermediaries.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents two reasons why it cannot be claimed that the benefits of religious obligations (taklif) accrue to the worshiper. The first reason: all benefits are either attaining good or warding off harm, and God can achieve all of them directly without these obligations. If God's power varied with human actions, He would be weak, making the linkage futile. The second reason: the benefits of these actions are either in this world or the next. In this world, they are only hardship; in the Hereafter, God can grant Paradise and save from Hell without these intermediaries. Thus, the benefits cannot be for the worshiper in any meaningful sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Second Reason: Benefits of Worship Not in This World or Hereafter
Second argument against benefits returning to worshiper: benefits not in this world or Hereafter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that benefits of worship cannot be in this world (since they are hardship) nor in the Hereafter (since God can grant Paradise without worship).

Core Idea
Worship is hardship in this world; in the Hereafter, God can grant rewards without intermediaries.

This passage provides the second reason why benefits of worship do not return to the worshiper. It argues that any benefits resulting from worship must be either in this world or the Hereafter. In this world, worship is pure toil and hardship, not benefit. In the Hereafter, God is able to admit people into Paradise and save them from Hell without these intermediaries. Thus, the benefits cannot be for the worshiper.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reason passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the division into two possible locations for benefits.
2
Analyze the rejection of this-worldly benefits
Understand why the author dismisses the idea that benefits occur in this world: because worship is pure toil and hardship, not benefit.
The key words are 'محض التعب والكلفة والمشقة' (pure toil, burden, and hardship).
3
Analyze the rejection of other-worldly benefits
Understand why the author dismisses the idea that benefits occur in the Hereafter: because God can directly grant Paradise and salvation without these intermediary acts of worship.
The phrase 'قادر على أن يدخلهم الجنة ويخلصهم من النيران من غير هذه الوسائط' is crucial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think worship yields benefits in this world (e.g., blessings) or in the Hereafter as rewards earned by works.
Correction: The author argues that worship in this world is hardship, not benefit; and in the Hereafter, God can give rewards without needing worship as a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second reason, why are benefits of worship not in this world?
A) Because God is weak.
B) Because worship is pure hardship.
C) Because worship is enjoyable.
D) Because benefits are only in the Hereafter.

Key Takeaway
The second reason: worship yields no benefit in this world (only hardship) and in the Hereafter God can grant Paradise without worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can grant Paradise without any of your acts of worship, what is the true reason you worship? Are you performing worship out of genuine love and obedience, or as a transaction to secure reward?
?
The lesson describes worship in this world as 'pure toil, burden, and hardship.' Does this description resonate with your experience of worship? If so, how does that affect your devotion?
?
If worship does not bring benefit in this world or the Hereafter, what does it mean for your daily practice? Will you continue or change your worship based on this realization?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Two Reasons Benefits Cannot Return to a Third Party
Two rational arguments against third-party beneficiary of obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments against the possibility that benefits of obligation accrue to anyone other than God or the worshiper.

Core Idea
Benefits cannot go to a third party; doing so would imply futility or injustice.

The author presents two concise but powerful arguments showing why the benefits of religious obligations cannot be directed to a third party. If you think obligations might benefit someone else, these reasons will clarify why that leads to absurd or unjust consequences. Let's examine each reason closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Let the argument structure become clear.
Notice the two reasons are introduced with 'لوجهين'.
2
Identify the two reasons
Locate the first reason about futility ('عبث') and the second reason about injustice ('ظلم'). Understand how each one attacks the idea of a third party beneficiary.
The first uses God's power, the second uses the moral concept of injustice.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how these two reasons together eliminate any scenario where obligations benefit someone other than God or the worshiper. This supports the broader argument about the purpose of obligations.
Contrast with any earlier assumptions you might have had.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الفوائد عائدة إلى ثالث غير العابد وغير المعبود وذلك لوجهين : الأول : إنه لا منفعة تحصل لذلك الثالث ، إلا والله تعالى قادر على تحصيلها بغير واسطة هذه التكاليف ، فيكون توسط هذه التكاليف : عبثاً . الثاني : إن حاصل هذا الكلام يرجع إلى أنه تعالى الحق الضرر والبلاء بأحد العبدين ، لأجل إيصال النفع إلى العبد الثاني . وهذا محض الظلم
And we said: it is not permissible that those benefits return to a third party other than the worshiper and the worshipped, for two reasons: First: that no benefit occurs for that third party except that God Almighty is able to obtain it without the intermediary of these obligations, so the intermediation of these obligations is futility. Second: that the outcome of this statement returns to the point that He, the Exalted, afflicts harm and calamity upon one of the two servants in order to deliver benefit to the second servant. And this is pure injustice.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents two reasons why the benefits of religious obligations (taklif) cannot be for a third party (neither God nor the worshiper). The first reason: any benefit that the third party receives could be given directly by God without obligations, so making the obligations intermediary is futile. The second reason: arguing that benefits go to a third party implies that God inflicts harm on one person to benefit another, which is pure injustice. Thus, the benefits cannot be for anyone other than God or the worshiper, and since previous arguments eliminated those possibilities, the entire notion of benefits from taklif is refuted.

Key Terms
ثالث
منفعة
عبث
الضرر والبلاء
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the benefits of worship (e.g., reward) can go to someone else, like a deceased person through offerings.
Correction: The passage argues that if benefits go to a third party, God could give them directly without the worshiper's action (futility) or it would imply God harming one to benefit another (injustice). Hence benefits cannot be for a third party.

Checkpoint
According to the second reason, what does the claim that benefits go to a third party imply about God?
A) God is generous.
B) God inflicts harm on one to benefit another, which is pure injustice.
C) God is all-powerful.
D) Obligations are unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The benefits of religious obligations cannot be for a third party, because that would either be futile (God could give directly) or unjust (harming one to benefit another).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you sometimes feel it is primarily for the benefit of others (e.g., your family, community, or society)? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? What would it mean for your sincerity if you viewed your worship as first and foremost for your own relationship with God? Will you commit to examining your intention in your next prayer?
?
Think of a particular act of worship that you find difficult or burdensome. In light of this lesson, consider: if that difficulty is not for God (He needs nothing) and not for a third party, then it must be for your own benefit. How does that change your perspective on that act? What specific change will you make in your attitude or practice starting today?

Rational Judgments

The First Rational Argument: Ta'dhim and Unnecessary Means
Sanusi defines ta'dhim and argues Allah can give joy without means

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument against the necessity of taklif based on the definition of ta'dhim and Allah's ability to grant joy directly.

Core Idea
If Allah can give joy directly, why impose means that cause hardship?

Sanusi begins by defining ta'dhim (glorification) as any act or omission that brings joy or pleasure. He then points out that Allah can give joy without any intermediary, making the entire structure of taklif rationally suspect. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of ta'dhim
Read how Sanusi defines ta'dhim as an act, saying, or omission that causes joy or pleasure. This sets up the argument.
2
Grasp the rational critique
Notice that Sanusi argues that since Allah can directly give joy, the means of obligation are unnecessary. Ask yourself why this would be a problem for rational justification of taklif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن التعظيم عبارة عن فعل أو قول أو ترك فعل أو ترك قول يقتضي حصول سرور في القلب أو لذة في البدن وقد بينا أنه لا سرور ولا لذة إلا والله تعالى قادر على إيصاله إلى العبد من غير هذه الوسائط . الثاني : إن استحقاق التعظيم قد يحصل بالأفعال السهلة فإن التكلم بكلمة الشهادة بعد سبق المعرفة عمل سهل وهو يوجب الثواب العظيم فلو كان المقصود من التكليف حصول هذا الاستحقاق لكان من الواجب في الحكمة أن نبالغ في تقوية أبداننا حتى يسهل العمل علينا فنصير مستحقين للثواب بسببه من غير حصول الكلفة والمشقة . الثالث : إن الحاصل بسبب هذا التكليف عند الطاعة حصول هذه الزيادة وهو هذا الاستحقاق العظيم وعند المعصية حصول العقاب الشديد الدائم والعقول السليمة قاضية بأن الفعل الذي تكون المنفعة الحاصلة زائدة فإنه تجب الحاجة إليه والفعل الذي تكون المضرة الحاصلة منه أعظم أنواع الضرر فإنه يجب تركه والاحتراز منه بأعظم الوجوه نفعا والله أعلم
The first: Indeed, glorification (al-ta'dhim) is an expression of an action, or a saying, or abandoning an action, or abandoning a saying, that necessitates the occurrence of joy in the heart or pleasure in the body. And we have explained that there is no joy nor pleasure except that Allah the Exalted is able to deliver it to the servant without these means. The second: Indeed, deserving of glorification may be attained by easy actions. For speaking the word of shahada after prior knowledge is an easy action, and it necessitates great reward. So if the purpose of obligation (al-taklif) was to attain this deservingness, then it would be necessary in wisdom that we exert ourselves in strengthening our bodies so that action becomes easy for us, so that we become deserving of reward by it without hardship and difficulty. The third: Indeed, what results from this obligation (al-taklif) in the case of obedience is the attainment of this increase, which is this great deservingness; and in the case of disobedience is the attainment of severe eternal punishment. And sound intellects judge that an action whose resulting benefit is greater requires doing it, and an action whose resulting harm is the greatest type of harm must be abandoned and guarded against in the greatest ways for benefit. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rational arguments (the first, second, third) against the goodness of taklif (divine obligation) within a framework that assumes rational judgment (tahsin al-aql wa taqbih). The first argument: ta'dhim (glorification) is defined as an act, saying, or omission that brings joy or pleasure; but Allah can directly give joy and pleasure without any intermediary, so the means of obligation are unnecessary. The second argument: deservingness of glorification can be attained by easy acts like saying the shahada after knowledge; if the goal were simply to attain deservingness, then the rational course would be to make all acts easy, not to impose hardship. The third argument: the outcome of obeying taklif is great reward, and of disobeying is eternal punishment; sound intellects judge that actions with great benefit must be done and actions with great harm must be avoided. The implication is that the extreme risk of punishment might outweigh any benefit, or that the very structure of reward and punishment makes obligation rationally problematic. The arguments are sequenced to show that taklif cannot be justified on rational grounds.

Key Terms
تعظيم
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi denies that Allah imposes obligations altogether.
Correction: No, this argument is a rational critique showing that taklif cannot be justified by reason alone; it does not deny that Allah may impose obligations as He wills.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's first argument, what is the definition of ta'dhim?
A) An act of worship that brings reward
B) An action, saying, or omission that necessitates joy or pleasure
C) A state of humility before Allah
D) A verbal declaration of faith

Key Takeaway
Ta'dhim is defined as an act causing joy, and since Allah can grant joy directly, the means of taklif are rationally unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah could grant you joy and pleasure without any effort from you, would you still choose to worship Him? What does your honest answer reveal about the purity of your intention?
?
The author defines ta'dhim as acts that cause joy. When you perform salah or dhikr, do you actually feel that joy? If not, what might be blocking it, and what will you do this week to seek that joy directly from Allah?
?
This argument risks making worship feel pointless. Has your heart ever whispered that 'why bother if Allah can give it without'? Confront that doubt tonight in sujud: ask Allah to show you the wisdom behind His commands even when you don't see the joy.

The Second Rational Argument: Easy Acts Can Deserve Great Reward
Sanusi argues that easy acts already earn reward, so hardship is unnecessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument against taklif: that easy acts like saying the shahada can earn great reward, so hardship is not necessary.

Core Idea
If easy acts already deserve great reward, why impose difficult obligations?

Sanusi's second argument focuses on the fact that extremely easy actions, like uttering the shahada after knowledge, can result in great reward. This suggests that if the purpose of taklif were to earn reward, then it would be rational to make all acts easy, not hard. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Read how Sanusi states that deservingness of glorification can be attained by easy actions. He gives the example of saying the shahada.
2
Follow the conditional argument
Notice the 'if... then' structure: if the goal of taklif is to attain deservingness, then wisdom would require strengthening bodies to make acts easy, not imposing hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first: Indeed, glorification (al-ta'dhim) is an expression of an action, or a saying, or abandoning an action, or abandoning a saying, that necessitates the occurrence of joy in the heart or pleasure in the body. And we have explained that there is no joy nor pleasure except that Allah the Exalted is able to deliver it to the servant without these means. The third: Indeed, what results from this obligation (al-taklif) in the case of obedience is the attainment of this increase, which is this great deservingness; and in the case of disobedience is the attainment of severe eternal punishment. And sound intellects judge that an action whose resulting benefit is greater requires doing it, and an action whose resulting harm is the greatest type of harm must be abandoned and guarded against in the greatest ways for benefit. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rational arguments (the first, second, third) against the goodness of taklif (divine obligation) within a framework that assumes rational judgment (tahsin al-aql wa taqbih). The first argument: ta'dhim (glorification) is defined as an act, saying, or omission that brings joy or pleasure; but Allah can directly give joy and pleasure without any intermediary, so the means of obligation are unnecessary. Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument says that all acts should be as easy as saying the shahada.
Correction: No, it is a conditional argument: if the goal of taklif is reward, then it should be made easy. But Sanusi does not claim that is the actual goal.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi give for an easy action that yields great reward?
A) Fasting in Ramadan
B) Speaking the shahada after knowledge
C) Giving charity in secret
D) Performing the pilgrimage

Key Takeaway
Since easy acts like shahada already earn great reward, imposing hardship in taklif is rationally unnecessary if the goal is reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that the shahada, an easy utterance, earns great reward. Yet how often do you lazily postpone easy acts of worship, like a simple dhikr? What does that reveal about your heart's connection to the reward? Today, commit to one easy act of worship you've been neglecting, and do it solely for Allah's pleasure, not just for reward.
?
If you could earn the same reward from an easy act as from a difficult one, would you still choose to struggle in obedience? Your honest answer shows whether you love the act itself or the ease. What hidden idol is your heart serving? Ask Allah to purify your intention.
?
The passage argues that hardship is not needed for reward. When you face a difficult obligation, do you resent the difficulty, or do you pause to ask: 'Ya Allah, what is Your real purpose in this trial?' Make a sincere intention to embrace the next hardship as a gift meant to draw you closer to Him, not merely to earn reward.

The Third Rational Argument: The Risk of Eternal Punishment
Sanusi contrasts reward and punishment and invokes rational judgment about benefit and harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument: that taklif carries the risk of eternal punishment, and sound intellects would avoid actions with such great harm.

Core Idea
If obeying leads to great reward but disobeying leads to eternal punishment, reason says avoid the risk.

Sanusi's third argument focuses on the outcomes of taklif: obedience brings great reward, but disobedience brings eternal punishment. Sound intellects judge that actions with great benefit are to be done and those with great harm are to be avoided. This lesson unpacks the rational calculus that makes taklif problematic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual outcome
Read that in obedience, one gains great deservingness; in disobedience, one incurs severe eternal punishment.
2
Apply the rational principle
Sound intellects judge that actions with great benefit should be done, and those with great harm should be avoided. Consider how this applies to taklif: the risk of eternal punishment may outweigh the benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi says we should avoid obeying Allah because of the risk of punishment.
Correction: No, he is pointing out the rational inconsistency: if the purpose is benefit, the risk of eternal harm seems irrational. He is critiquing the rationalist framework, not advocating disobedience.

What does Sanusi say about the outcome of disobedience under taklif?
A) It results in temporary punishment
B) It results in severe eternal punishment

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that disobedience leads to eternal punishment, would you change any habits today? What is holding you back from full obedience?
?
The argument shows that reason alone might lead to avoiding taklif. How does your faith overcome this rational risk? Are you obeying out of love or fear?
?
Have you ever considered that the risk of eternal punishment might make you avoid religious obligations altogether? What does this reveal about your reliance on God's mercy versus your own reasoning?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
Why Benefits of Obligation Cannot Return to a Third Party
Sanusi refutes that taklif benefits a third party with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's two rational arguments against the idea that the benefits of taklif (religious obligation) could return to a third party other than God and the worshipper.

Core Idea
Taklif cannot benefit a third party — it would be either futile or unjust.

Sanusi now addresses a possible objection: maybe the purpose of taklif is to benefit someone other than God or the worshipper. He provides two crisp rational arguments to show this cannot be the case. The first appeals to God's direct power, the second to the principle of justice. Let's read his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear Sanusi's own wording. Then read the full translation. Notice the structure: he states his thesis, then gives two numbered reasons.
The passage is compact; each reason is a single sentence.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two reasons marked by 'الأول' and 'الثاني'. For each, ask: what is the core argument? Write them in your own words.
The first uses the concept of futility (عبث), the second uses pure injustice (محض الظلم).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice how Sanusi concludes: 'So it is established... that it is not permissible to say that obligation is good for a reason of wisdom and benefit.' This shows the two reasons refute the entire claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الفوائد عائدة إلى ثالث غير العابد وغير المعبود وذلك لوجهين : الأول : إنه لا منفعة تحصل لذلك الثالث إلا والله تعالى قادر على تحصيلها بغير واسطة هذه التكاليف فيكون توسط هذه التكاليف عبثا . الثاني : إن حاصل هذا الكلام يرجع إلى أنه تعالى الحق الضرر والبلاء بأحد العبدين لأجل إيصال النفع إلى العبد الثاني وهذا محض الظلم وأيضا فليس إيصال الضرر إلى أحدهما لأجل حصول النفع للثاني أولى من العكس فثبت بهذا البيان أنه لا يجوز أن يقال التكليف إنما هو حسن لوجه حكمة ومصلحة
And we only say that it is not permissible for those benefits to return to a third party other than the worshiper and the worshipped, for two reasons: The first: There is no benefit that accrues to that third party except that Allah the Exalted is able to achieve it without the mediation of these obligations, so the mediation of these obligations would be futile. The second: Indeed, the gist of this statement returns to the point that He the Exalted affixed harm and affliction to one of the two servants in order to deliver benefit to the second servant, and this is pure injustice. And also: Delivering harm to one of them in order to obtain benefit for the second is not more preferable than the opposite. So it is established by this exposition that it is not permissible to say that obligation is good for a reason of wisdom and benefit.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage refutes the possibility that the benefits of taklif (obligation) could return to a third party (other than God and the servant). The author gives two succinct reasons. First: any benefit that a third party could receive from the obligation can be given directly by God without the mediation of obligation, so the obligation would be futile (عبث). Second: the very idea that God would harm one servant to benefit another is pure injustice (ظلم), and further, there is no reason to prefer harming one over the other; the choice is arbitrary. Thus, the claim that taklif is good for some wisdom or benefit is false.

Key Terms
فوائد
ثالث
عبث
ظلم
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God can use means like taklif to benefit a third party without issue.
Correction: Sanusi argues that if God can grant the benefit directly, the means become futile; if He cannot, then it implies injustice. Both are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given against benefits returning to a third party?
A) The third party does not exist
B) It involves pure injustice and arbitrariness
C) Benefits to a third party are impossible
D) The third party is not worthy of benefit

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's two arguments show that attributing taklif's benefits to a third party leads to either futility or injustice, both of which are impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself questioning why God imposes hardships on you, do you ever slip into thinking that there is no purpose or that it is unfair? How does Sanusi's argument that God's actions are neither futile nor unjust challenge that thought? What specific area of your life do you need to entrust to His wisdom today?
?
Consider a time you wished God would use you to benefit someone else through a trial you are facing. Can you see how this lesson protects you from expecting God to act unjustly? In what situation do you need to trust that God's wisdom is sufficient without needing to see the outcome?
?
If you were to truly internalize that every obligation God places on you is for your own good (not for a third party's benefit), how would it change your attitude towards the most difficult command you are currently failing to fulfill? What is one small step you can take today to obey with that certitude?

Refuting Taklif Without Benefit or Wisdom
Sanusi refutes the second division that taklif is good without any benefit or wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that, under the rationalist framework of tahsin and taqbih, the idea of divine obligation without benefit or wisdom is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
If reason is the judge, then unwarranted harm is always evil.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses the second branch of the rationalist argument about obligation. Having earlier refuted the first branch (that benefit is necessary), he now shows that even if one claims obligation can be good without benefit, it fails because under rationalist premises, inflicting harm without purpose is inherently evil. This completes the reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors.
Notice the phrase 'على تقدير' (on the assumption) which sets up the conditional.
2
Identify the second division being refuted
The second division claims that obligation can be good even if it brings no benefit or wisdom. Underline the key terms 'فائدة' and 'حكمة'.
3
Understand the rationalist assumption
Sanusi is working within the framework of rational approval and disapproval (تحسين العقل وتقبيحه). On that assumption, what is the rational judgment of inflicting harm without purpose?
The text says it is 'قبيح في العقل' (rationally evil).
4
Trace the conclusion
Since both divisions (with benefit and without) are false, the overall idea of obligation under rationalist premises collapses. See how Sanusi ends: 'فوجب أن يكون القول بالتكليف باطلا'.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This is not an absolute rejection of divine obligation, but a critique of rationalist ethics. The author is showing an internal contradiction in the rationalist position.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أنه حسن ذلك من الله تعالى من غير فائدة ولا حكمة فهذا أيضا باطل لأنا في هذا القسم إنما نتكلم على تقدير أن يكون القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبرا ومعلوم أن على هذا التقدير فإلحاق المضار والآلام والمتاعب بالغير من غير حكمة وفائدة قبيح في العقل فكان هذا القسم باطلا فيثبت أن على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبرا لو حصل التكليف لحصل إما لفائدة أو لا لفائدة وثبت فساد القسمين فوجب أن يكون القول بالتكليف باطلا على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبرا
As for the second division, which is that it is good from Allah the Exalted without any benefit or wisdom, this is also false. For in this division we only speak on the assumption that the doctrine of rational approval and disapproval (tahsin al-aql wa taqbih) is considered. And it is known that on this assumption, inflicting harms, pains, and difficulties on others without wisdom and benefit is rationally evil (qabih). So this division is false. So it is established that on the assumption that rational approval and disapproval is considered, if obligation were to occur, it would occur either for a benefit or not for a benefit, and the falsity of both divisions has been proven. So necessarily, the doctrine of obligation would be false on the assumption that rational approval and disapproval is considered.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage refutes the second division of the argument: that taklif (obligation) could be good even if it brings no benefit or wisdom. The author notes that this discussion is premised on accepting rational approval and disapproval (تحسين العقل وتقبيحه). Under that premise, inflicting harm and difficulty on others without any purpose is rationally evil (قبيح). Therefore, this division is false. Combined with the earlier refutation of the division that postulates benefit, both possibilities are falsified. Thus, under the rationalist framework, the very concept of taklif becomes rationally indefensible.

Key Terms
القسم الثاني
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
قبيح
فائدة
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Sanusi is absolutely denying that God can impose obligations without any purpose, or that he rejects divine obligation altogether.
Correction: This is not an absolute denial that God imposes obligations, but a reductio ad absurdum of the rationalist position. Sanusi is showing that if one accepts rational approval and disapproval as a standard, then the concept of obligation without benefit or wisdom becomes rationally indefensible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about inflicting harm without wisdom or benefit under the rationalist assumption?
A) It is permissible
B) It is rationally evil (qabih)
C) It is obligatory
D) It is neutral

Key Takeaway
Under rationalist premises, a taklif that brings no benefit or wisdom is rationally impossible, because inflicting harm without purpose is inherently evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself asking 'why did God allow this hardship in my life?' expecting a clear benefit? This lesson shows that demanding benefit from God's actions leads to logical absurdity. Will you trust God's wisdom even when you see no benefit?
?
When you read the Quran and find commands that seem to bring no immediate worldly benefit, do you sometimes waver in obedience? How does this lesson call you to obey without needing to see the reason?
?
If you truly believe that God's actions are not subject to human standards of benefit, how should that change your complaint when difficulties come? Will you surrender your judgment to His, starting today?

Second Proof: Four Divisions of Taklif Purposes
Sanusi presents a second argument with four divisions and refutes the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against rational approval and disapproval, which divides the possible purposes of taklif into four categories and refutes the first two.

Core Idea
Four possible purposes of taklif exhaust all likelihoods, and two are already proven invalid.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a second argument structured as a division of all possible purposes for divine obligation (taklif). He identifies four exhaustive possibilities: benefit returning to God, to the servant, to a third party, or no benefit at all. He then begins refuting these, addressing the first two. The argument is cut off but still instructive for understanding the dialectical method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the structure of the argument. Notice the initial division and the refutations.
The text cuts off mid-sentence; the refutations of the third and fourth divisions are missing.
2
Identify the four divisions
The proof begins by dividing purposes into four mutually exclusive categories: benefit to God, to the servant, to a third party, or no benefit at all.
These four exhaust all logical possibilities.
3
Follow the refutation of the first division
The first division (benefit to God) is dismissed as false, referencing an earlier argument (al-wajh al-awwal).
This is brief; the earlier argument is not included here.
4
Analyze the refutation of the second division
The second division (benefit to the servant) is refuted by pointing out a contradiction: if the purpose is the servant's benefit, then threatening punishment for not achieving it is self-defeating. The passage includes a dialogue where the servant points out the contradiction.
The argument is that the command becomes irrational.
5
Recognize the incomplete status
Note that the refutation of the third and fourth divisions is missing. Consider how the argument likely concludes: all four are false, so taklif would be evil under the opponent's assumption.
The text is an excerpt; the complete argument is found elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان أنه مع القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه وجب أن يكون التكليف قبيحا هو أن نقول إما أن يقال إن الله تعالى في هذه التكاليف فائدة ومنفعة وإما أن يقال إنه منزه عن جميع المنافع والفوائد العائدة إليه وإنما كلفه بهذه الأفعال لأجل الفوائد العائدة إلى ذلك العبد أو لأجل الفوائد العائدة إلى ذلك الثالث أو لا لشيء من الفوائد والأقسام الأربعة باطلة أما القسم الأول وهو أن يكون المقصود من هذه التكاليف عود الفوائد إلى الله تعالى فهو باطل على ما بيناه في الوجه الأول وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يكون المقتضى لحسن هذه التكاليف عود المنافع والمصالح إلى العباد فهذا باطل لأن معنى إيجابها ترتب العقاب على تركها فيصير المعنى كأن الله تعالى يقول للعبد أيها العبد حصل لنفسك المصلحة الفلانية وإن لم تحصلها لنفسك فأنا أعذيك أبد الآباد فيقول العبد يا إله العالمين هذا الحكم متناقض لأنه إذا كان لا مقصود لك من
The second proof, in explaining that with the acceptance of rational approval and disapproval, obligation must be evil, is that we say: Either it is said that Allah the Exalted has benefit and advantage in these obligations, or it is said that He is transcendent above all benefits and advantages returning to Him, and that He only obligated these acts for the benefits returning to that servant, or for the benefits returning to that third party, or for no benefit whatsoever. And the four divisions are false. As for the first division, which is that the purpose of these obligations is the return of benefits to Allah the Exalted, it is false as we explained in the first point. As for the second division, which is that the cause of the goodness of these obligations is the return of benefits and interests to the servants, this is false because the meaning of imposing them is the attachment of punishment upon abandoning them. So the meaning becomes as if Allah the Exalted says to the servant: 'O servant, obtain for yourself such-and-such interest, and if you do not obtain it for yourself, then I will punish you forever.' So the servant says: 'O God of the worlds, this judgment is contradictory. Because if you have no purpose from...'
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This is a second proof structured as a division (تقسيم) into four exhaustive possibilities for the purpose of taklif: (1) benefits return to God, (2) benefits return to the servant, (3) benefits return to a third party, (4) no benefit at all. The author declares all four false. He refutes the first by referencing an earlier argument (الوجه الأول). He refutes the second by pointing out a contradiction: if the purpose is the servant's benefit, then threatening punishment for not achieving that benefit is contradictory—the servant would argue that the command is self-defeating. The text cuts off during the refutation of the second division, and the third and fourth are not provided. The argument is incomplete in this window.

Misconception: The second proof is an attack on the very concept of religious obligation.
Correction: Sanusi is not denying that Allah imposes obligations; rather, he is arguing that if one accepts rational approval and disapproval, then taklif would be deemed evil. The argument is dialectical, assuming the opponent's premise.
