First Proof: Taklif Cannot Be Justified Under Moral Rationalism
Rāzī refutes two possible justifications for divine obligation under rational ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the assumption of rational moral judgment (taḥsīn al-'aql wa taqbīḥuh) leads to the inconsistency of divine obligation (taklīf).

Core Idea
If obligation is rationally good, it must either benefit someone or not; both options fail.

The author now presents the first proof against divine obligation under the framework of rational ethics. If we accept that reason alone can determine good and evil, then we must examine whether obligation can be justified. This passage shows that any attempt to justify obligation leads to contradiction, establishing that taklīf cannot be maintained under this ethical system.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Recognize that this proof is conditional: it only applies if we assume that reason can judge good and evil (taḥsīn al-'aql wa taqbīḥuh). Without that assumption, the argument does not hold.
The author is not rejecting obligation per se, but showing inconsistency within a specific ethical framework.
2
Follow the two divisions
Notice the author splits the possible justifications for obligation into two: either it is for a benefit (ḥikma/maṣlaḥa) or it is without any benefit. Read how each is refuted.
This division is exhaustive: every possible justification falls into one of these two.
3
See how both are refuted
The first division is refuted because harming one to benefit another is not rationally preferable to the opposite. The second is refuted because inflicting harm without reason is rationally evil (qabīḥ). Therefore, both lead to contradiction.
The conclusion: under rational moral judgment, obligation must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا : فليس إيصال الضرر إلى أحدهما ، لأجل حصول النفع للثاني ، أولى من العكس . فثبت بهذا البيان: أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : التكليف إنما هو حسن لوجه حكمة ومصلحة .
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنه حسن ذلك من الله تعالى من غير فائدة ولا حكمة . فهذا أيضاً باطل لأنا في هذا القسم إنما نتكلم على تقدير أن يكون القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً. ومعلوم أن على هذا التقدير فإلحاق المضار والآلام والمتاعب بالغير من غير حكمة وفائدة قبيح في العقل . فكان هذا القسم باطلاً . فيثبت أن على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً . لو حصل التكليف . لحصل ، إما لفائدة أو لا لفائدة ، وثبت فساد القسمين ، فوجب أن يكون القول بالتكليف باطلا ، على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً .
And also: Conveying harm to one for the sake of benefiting the other is not preferable to the opposite. So it is established by this clarification: it is not permissible to say that obligation (al-taklīf) is good only for a reason of wisdom (ḥikma) and benefit (maṣlaḥa). As for the second division: that it is good from God, Exalted, without any benefit or wisdom. This too is false, because in this division we are only speaking on the assumption that the doctrine of rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-'aql wa taqbīḥuh) is valid. It is known that on this assumption, inflicting harms, pains, and hardships on others without wisdom and benefit is rationally evil (qabīḥ fī al-'aql). Thus this division is false. So it is established that on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is valid, if obligation were to occur, it would occur either with a benefit or without a benefit, and both divisions are refuted, so it must be that the doctrine of obligation is false on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is valid.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the possibility of divine obligation (taklīf) if one accepts the principle of rational moral judgment (taḥsīn al-'aql wa taqbīḥuh). The author begins with a subsidiary point: harming one person to benefit another is not rationally preferable to the opposite; no reason compels that choice. From this, he concludes that one cannot say that obligation is good for a reason of wisdom and benefit, because that would imply a preference that is not rationally justified. Then he considers the second division: that obligation is good from God without any benefit or wisdom. On the assumption of rational moral judgment, this is also false, because inflicting harm without reason is rationally evil. Thus, if obligation exists, it must either be for a benefit or not; both possibilities are refuted, so obligation must be false under the assumption of rational moral judgment.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
تكليف
حكمة
مصلحة
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof rejects divine obligation altogether, regardless of ethical framework.
Correction: The proof only shows inconsistency under rationalist ethics; it does not apply if moral value is determined by divine command.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is it false to say obligation is good for a reason of wisdom and benefit?
A) Because harming one for another is never rational.
B) Because it is not rationally preferable to harm one for the benefit of another.
C) Because wisdom and benefit are not relevant to divine acts.
D) Because obligation is always harmful.

Key Takeaway
Under rational moral judgment, divine obligation is impossible because it would either serve a purpose (which is not rationally preferable) or serve no purpose (which is rationally evil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's commands must make sense to your own reasoning? After reading this proof, do you see a risk in making your reason the judge of divine actions? What would it mean for your faith if you felt that an obligation of God seemed 'unreasonable'?
?
This passage shows that rationalist ethics leads to a dead end for obligation. Do you rely on your intellect more than revelation when deciding right and wrong? How does this affect your understanding of worship? Will you commit to seeking guidance primarily from the revealed sources rather than your own reasoning?
?
Rāzī argues that inflicting harm without reason is rationally evil. Think of a hardship or obligation in your life that you have resented. Can you now see it as something that must have a reason from God, even if you do not know it? Will you surrender your judgment and trust that His wisdom surpasses yours?

The Four Divisions of Benefit and the First Refutation
Listing four divisions and refuting first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof's structure, which divides possible reasons for divine obligation into four categories, and the refutation of the first category (benefit to God).

Core Idea
If obligation benefits God, it contradicts His perfection; if it benefits others, punishment contradicts the goal.

In this second proof, the author systematically examines every possible reason why God might impose obligations. He finds all four options self-contradictory. We begin with the first division: the idea that God derives benefit from our worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four divisions
Read the passage and note the four possible purposes for obligation listed by the author.
The divisions are: benefit to God, benefit to the servant, benefit to a third party, or no benefit.
2
Focus on the first refutation
The author immediately rejects benefit to God. Why? Recall the previous proof about God's perfection.
The author says it is false 'based on what we explained in the first proof'.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Understand that this refutation sets the stage for examining the remaining divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان أنه مع القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، وجب أن يكون التكليف قبيحاً : هو أن نقول : إما أن يقال : إن الله تعالى في هذه التكاليف فائدة ومنفعة وإما أن يقال : إنه منزه عن جميع المنافع والفوائد العائدة إليه . وإنما كلفه بهذه الأفعال لأجل الفوائد العائدة إلى ذلك العبد ، أو لأجل الفوائد العائدة إلى ذلك الثالث. أو لا لشيء من الفوائد . والأقسام الأربعة باطلة
أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يكون المقصود من هذه التكاليف : عود الفوائد إلى الله تعالى، فهو باطل على ما بيناه في الوجه الأول
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يكون المقتضى لحسن هذه التكاليف عود المنافع والمصالح إلى العباد ، فهذا باطل ، لأن معنى إيجابها : ترتب العقاب على تركها ، فيصير المعنى كأن الله تعالى يقول للعبد: أيها العبد حصل لنفسك المصلحة الفلانية ، وإن لم تحصلها لنفسك ، فأنا أعذيك أبد الآباد . فيقول العبد : يا إله العالمين : هذا الحكم متناقض . لأنه إذا كان لا مقصود لك من هذا التكليف إلا حصول منافع مخصوصة إلى، كان كل المقصود رعاية أحوالي فتعذيبي على تركها يناقض رعاية أحوالي فكان الجمع بينهما متناقضاً . ومثاله : أن يقول السيد لعبده : اجتهد في هذا اليوم في كسب درهم لنفسك ، فقصر العبد في ذلك ، فأخذه السيد ، وقرض أعضاءه بمقاريض من النار . فيقول العبد : أيها السيد هل كنت في ذلك الدرهم لنفسك ؟ أو كنت فارغاً عن جميع المطامع العائدة إليك ، وإنما أمرتني بكسب ذلك الدرهم لمصالح نفسي فقط ؟ فإن كان الأول ، كان هذا التعذيب حسناً ، لأني سعيت في تفويت مطلوبك . وأما إن كان الحق هو الثاني ، كان هذا الفعل باطلا . لأن العبد يقول : إنما أمرتني بكسب ذلك الدرهم لنفسي ، ولتكون منافعه عائدة إلي ، لا إليك . فلما قصرت في تحصيله، فأنا ما قصرت إلا في تحصيل المنفعة لنفسي . وتعذيب الإنسان لأجل أنه قصر في تحصيل مصالح نفسه ، قبيح في العقول . لأن رعاية مصالحه ، إن لم تكن واجبة الرعاية ، كان تكليفه لتحصيل ذلك الدرهم لنفسه : غير واجب. وإن كانت واجبة الرعاية ، فأهم المهمات له : إزالة العقاب . فكان إيصال العقاب إليه ، لأجل أنه قصر في حق نفسه : فعلا متناقضاً
وبهذا يظهر الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب فإن السيد إذا أمر عبده بعمل ، فقصر فيه ، فإنه يستحق الملامة والتغذيب. وذلك لأن السيد إذا أمره بذلك العمل ، ليستفيد منه نوعاً من أنواع المنافع ، فلما قصر العبد فيه . فقد فات على السيد تلك المنافع ، فحسن منه تعذيب ذلك العبد . أما هذا في حق الله محال . فكان تعذيب العبد على ترك التكاليف والأعمال : قبيحاً . فظهر الفرق بين الشاهد وبين الغائب
وأما القسم الثالث والرابع : فهما باطلان بالوجوه المذكورة في الحجة الأولى .
The second proof in demonstrating that with the doctrine of rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-'aql wa taqbīḥuh), obligation (al-taklīf) must be evil (qabīḥ): it is to say: either it is said that God, Exalted, has benefit and advantage in these obligations, or it is said that He is exalted above all benefits and advantages returning to Him. But He obligated these acts for the benefits returning to that servant, or for the benefits returning to that third party, or for none of the benefits. And the four divisions are false. As for the first division: that the purpose of these obligations is the return of benefits to God, Exalted, it is false based on what we explained in the first proof. As for the second division: that the basis for the goodness of these obligations is the return of benefits and interests to the servants, this is false, because the meaning of making them obligatory is the attachment of punishment to their omission, so the meaning becomes as if God, Exalted, says to the servant: 'O servant, obtain for yourself such and such interest, and if you do not obtain it for yourself, I will punish you forever.' The servant says: 'O God of the worlds: this judgment is contradictory. Because if you have no purpose in this obligation except the attainment of specific benefits for me, then all purpose is the care of my conditions, and punishing me for omitting it contradicts the care of my conditions, so the combination of them is contradictory.' Its example: a master says to his slave: 'Exert yourself today in earning a dirham for yourself,' and the slave is negligent in that, so the master takes him and cuts his limbs with scissors of fire. The slave says: 'O master, did you have an interest in that dirham for yourself? Or were you free from all desires returning to you, and you only commanded me to earn that dirham for my own interests only?' If the first, then this punishment is good, because I strove to frustrate your desire. But if the truth is the second, this act is false. Because the slave says: 'You only commanded me to earn that dirham for myself, and so that its benefits return to me, not to you. Since I fell short in attaining it, I only fell short in attaining benefit for myself. And punishing a person because he fell short in attaining his own interests is rationally evil. Because the care of his interests, if it is not obligatory to care for, then his obligation to earn that dirham for himself is not obligatory. And if it is obligatory to care for, the most important of important matters for him is the removal of punishment. So conveying punishment to him because he fell short in his own right is a contradictory act.' And by this, the difference between the witnessed (al-shāhid) and the absent (al-ghā'ib) becomes clear. For if a master commands his slave to do a work and he falls short, he deserves blame and punishment. That is because when the master commands him to do that work to benefit from some kind of benefit, when the slave falls short, those benefits are lost to the master, so it is good for him to punish that slave. As for this in the case of God, it is impossible. So punishing the slave for omitting obligations and acts is evil. Thus the difference between the witnessed and the absent becomes clear. As for the third and fourth divisions, they are false by the proofs mentioned in the first proof.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against divine obligation under rationalist ethics. The author structures the argument into a fourfold division of possible benefits that could justify obligation: (1) benefit to God, (2) benefit to the servant, (3) benefit to a third party, or (4) no benefit at all. He refutes each. 

First, benefit to God is rejected by reference to the earlier proof (al-wajh al-awwal). 

Second, benefit to the servant is refuted in detail. The author argues that obligation implies punishment for non-performance. If the only purpose is the servant's own benefit, then punishing him for failing to benefit himself is contradictory, because the punishment itself harms him, undermining the claimed care for his welfare. He illustrates this with a vivid analogy: a master commands his slave to earn a dirham for himself; the slave fails, and the master tortures him with fire-scissors. The slave then questions whether the master had a personal interest or was selflessly concerned only for the slave. If the master had personal interest, punishment is reasonable (since the slave thwarted the master's goal). But if the master's sole motive was the slave's benefit, punishing the slave for failing to benefit himself is absurd—it contradicts the very care for the slave. The author elaborates: if caring for one's own interests is not obligatory, then commanding them is not obligatory; if it is obligatory, then the most important thing is to avoid punishment, so imposing punishment for failing to care for oneself is contradictory.

Third and fourth divisions (benefit to a third party or no benefit) are dismissed by reference to the first proof.

Finally, the author distinguishes the 'witnessed' (shāhid, i.e., human masters) from the 'absent' (ghā'ib, i.e., God). In human cases, punishment is justified because the master has a personal interest. In the divine case, since God has no need, punishment for mandating self-benefit is evil. Thus the second proof concludes that obligation is impossible under rationalist ethics.

Key Terms
الحجة الثانية
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
تكليف
فائدة
قسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God could be benefited by worship, similar to how humans benefit from service.
Correction: The author argues that God is perfect and has no needs, so He cannot derive benefit from obligations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the first division (benefit to God) false?
A) Because God already has everything.
B) Because it contradicts the first proof.
C) Because the servant never benefits.
D) Because obligation cannot have a purpose.

Key Takeaway
The first potential reason for obligation—benefit to God—is impossible because God is perfect and self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your acts of worship are a favor to Allah? This passage makes clear that God gains nothing from your obedience. How does that realization reshape your intention when you pray or give charity?
?
If you truly believed that every command from Allah is for your own benefit—and that He is completely self-sufficient—would your heart feel more eager or more reluctant to obey? Be honest: what holds you back from acting on that belief?
?
The fourth division—that obligation might have no benefit at all—is rejected. Yet sometimes we act as if our deeds are meaningless. Reflect on a time you felt your worship was empty. How does the argument that obligation is for your benefit confront that feeling and call you to change?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
8 min
Refutation of Benefit to the Servant
Refutation of benefit to servant with detailed analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed refutation of the second division—that obligation exists for the servant's benefit—and why it leads to an internal contradiction.

Core Idea
Punishing someone for failing to benefit themselves is contradictory and rationally evil.

The second division seems more plausible: God obligates us for our own good. But the author shows that obligation includes punishment, which undermines any supposed care for our welfare. He illustrates this with a striking analogy of a master and slave.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation carefully
Read the passage and follow the logic: obligation implies punishment for omission. If the purpose is the servant's benefit, punishing him for not benefiting himself contradicts that purpose.
2
Analyze the analogy
The author uses a master who commands his slave to earn a dirham for himself. The slave fails, and the master tortures him. Consider the slave's objection.
The analogy is meant to show the absurdity of punishing someone for neglecting their own interest.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
The author concludes that punishing someone for failing to secure their own benefit is 'rationally evil' (qabīḥ). Why?
Because the punishment itself harms the person, defeating the claimed purpose.

What is the main argument against the second division?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short in acts of worship, do you ever imagine God as a harsh master ready to punish you for failing to benefit yourself? How does this lesson's analogy of the master and slave reshape your understanding of God's justice and mercy?
?
The slave's question reveals that if the command was for his own benefit, punishment is contradictory. Where in your life have you demanded that God reward you for your obedience as if He owes you, while forgetting that His commands are not for His own benefit but for yours?
?
The argument proves that punishing someone for neglecting their own interests is rationally evil. Do you ever punish yourself—through guilt, self-blame, or despair—for failing to live up to what you think God expects? How does this lesson free you from that cycle and point you to a deeper purpose in obligation?

Rational Judgments

The Watched-Absent Distinction and Final Refutations
Shahid-gha'ib distinction and dismissal of third and fourth divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between human masters (witnessed) and God (absent) regarding punishment, and how it refutes the remaining divisions (benefit to third party or no benefit).

Core Idea
Unlike human masters who act for self-interest, God has no needs, making punishment for self-benefit obligations unjustifiable.

The author now draws a sharp contrast between the human context (shāhid) and the divine (ghā'ib). What makes punishment rational for a human master is his personal stake; for God, that stake is absent. This ties together the refutation of all divisions, especially the third and fourth.

Guided Path
1
Read the shahid-gha'ib argument
Read the passage and understand why punishment is rational for a human master but not for God.
2
Apply the distinction
See how this distinction renders the third and fourth divisions false by the same logic used in the first proof.
3
Reflect on the overall second proof
Consider how this completes the refutation of all four categories.

أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يكون المقصود من هذه التكاليف : عود الفوائد إلى الله تعالى، فهو باطل على ما بيناه في الوجه الأول
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يكون المقتضى لحسن هذه التكاليف عود المنافع والمصالح إلى العباد ، فهذا باطل ، لأن معنى إيجابها : ترتب العقاب على تركها ، فيصير المعنى كأن الله تعالى يقول للعبد: أيها العبد حصل لنفسك المصلحة الفلانية ، وإن لم تحصلها لنفسك ، فأنا أعذيك أبد الآباد . فيقول العبد : يا إله العالمين : هذا الحكم متناقض . لأنه إذا كان لا مقصود لك من هذا التكليف إلا حصول منافع مخصوصة إلى، كان كل المقصود رعاية أحوالي فتعذيبي على تركها يناقض رعاية أحوالي فكان الجمع بينهما متناقضاً . ومثاله : أن يقول السيد لعبده : اجتهد في هذا اليوم في كسب درهم لنفسك ، فقصر العبد في ذلك ، فأخذه السيد ، وقرض أعضاءه بمقاريض من النار . فيقول العبد : أيها السيد هل كنت في ذلك الدرهم لنفسك ؟ أو كنت فارغاً عن جميع المطامع العائدة إليك ، وإنما أمرتني بكسب ذلك الدرهم لمصالح نفسي فقط ؟ فإن كان الأول ، كان هذا التعذيب حسناً ، لأني سعيت في تفويت مطلوبك . وأما إن كان الحق هو الثاني ، كان هذا الفعل باطلا . لأن العبد يقول : إنما أمرتني بكسب ذلك الدرهم لنفسي ، ولتكون منافعه عائدة إلي ، لا إليك . فلما قصرت في تحصيله، فأنا ما قصرت إلا في تحصيل المنفعة لنفسي . وتعذيب الإنسان لأجل أنه قصر في تحصيل مصالح نفسه ، قبيح في العقول . لأن رعاية مصالحه ، إن لم تكن واجبة الرعاية ، كان تكليفه لتحصيل ذلك الدرهم لنفسه : غير واجب. وإن كانت واجبة الرعاية ، فأهم المهمات له : إزالة العقاب . فكان إيصال العقاب إليه ، لأجل أنه قصر في حق نفسه : فعلا متناقضاً
وبهذا يظهر الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب فإن السيد إذا أمر عبده بعمل ، فقصر فيه ، فإنه يستحق الملامة والتغذيب. وذلك لأن السيد إذا أمره بذلك العمل ، ليستفيد منه نوعاً من أنواع المنافع ، فلما قصر العبد فيه . فقد فات على السيد تلك المنافع ، فحسن منه تعذيب ذلك العبد . أما هذا في حق الله محال . فكان تعذيب العبد على ترك التكاليف والأعمال : قبيحاً . فظهر الفرق بين الشاهد وبين الغائب
وأما القسم الثالث والرابع : فهما باطلان بالوجوه المذكورة في الحجة الأولى .
The second proof in demonstrating that with the doctrine of rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-'aql wa taqbīḥuh), obligation (al-taklīf) must be evil (qabīḥ): it is to say: either it is said that God, Exalted, has benefit and advantage in these obligations, or it is said that He is exalted above all benefits and advantages returning to Him. But He obligated these acts for the benefits returning to that servant, or for the benefits returning to that third party, or for none of the benefits. And the four divisions are false. As for the first division: that the purpose of these obligations is the return of benefits to God, Exalted, it is false based on what we explained in the first proof. As for the second division: that the basis for the goodness of these obligations is the return of benefits and interests to the servants, this is false, because the meaning of making them obligatory is the attachment of punishment to their omission, so the meaning becomes as if God, Exalted, says to the servant: 'O servant, obtain for yourself such and such interest, and if you do not obtain it for yourself, I will punish you forever.' The servant says: 'O God of the worlds: this judgment is contradictory. Because if you have no purpose in this obligation except the attainment of specific benefits for me, then all purpose is the care of my conditions, and punishing me for omitting it contradicts the care of my conditions, so the combination of them is contradictory.' Its example: a master says to his slave: 'Exert yourself today in earning a dirham for yourself,' and the slave is negligent in that, so the master takes him and cuts his limbs with scissors of fire. The slave says: 'O master, did you have an interest in that dirham for yourself? Or were you free from all desires returning to you, and you only commanded me to earn that dirham for my own interests only?' If the first, then this punishment is good, because I strove to frustrate your desire. But if the truth is the second, this act is false. Because the slave says: 'You only commanded me to earn that dirham for myself, and so that its benefits return to me, not to you. Since I fell short in attaining it, I only fell short in attaining benefit for myself. And punishing a person because he fell short in attaining his own interests is rationally evil. Because the care of his interests, if it is not obligatory to care for, then his obligation to earn that dirham for himself is not obligatory. And if it is obligatory to care for, the most important of important matters for him is the removal of punishment. So conveying punishment to him because he fell short in his own right is a contradictory act.' And by this, the difference between the witnessed (al-shāhid) and the absent (al-ghā'ib) becomes clear. For if a master commands his slave to do a work and he falls short, he deserves blame and punishment. That is because when the master commands him to do that work to benefit from some kind of benefit, when the slave falls short, those benefits are lost to the master, so it is good for him to punish that slave. As for this in the case of God, it is impossible. So punishing the slave for omitting obligations and acts is evil. Thus the difference between the witnessed and the absent becomes clear. As for the third and fourth divisions, they are false by the proofs mentioned in the first proof.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against divine obligation under rationalist ethics. The author structures the argument into a fourfold division of possible benefits that could justify obligation: (1) benefit to God, (2) benefit to the servant, (3) benefit to a third party, or (4) no benefit at all. He refutes each. 

First, benefit to God is rejected by reference to the earlier proof (al-wajh al-awwal). 

Second, benefit to the servant is refuted in detail. The author argues that obligation implies punishment for non-performance. If the only purpose is the servant's own benefit, then punishing him for failing to benefit himself is contradictory, because the punishment itself harms him, undermining the claimed care for his welfare. He illustrates this with a vivid analogy: a master commands his slave to earn a dirham for himself; the slave fails, and the master tortures him with fire-scissors. The slave then questions whether the master had a personal interest or was selflessly concerned only for the slave. If the master had personal interest, punishment is reasonable (since the slave thwarted the master's goal). But if the master's sole motive was the slave's benefit, punishing the slave for failing to benefit himself is absurd—it contradicts the very care for the slave. The author elaborates: if caring for one's own interests is not obligatory, then commanding them is not obligatory; if it is obligatory, then the most important thing is to avoid punishment, so imposing punishment for failing to care for oneself is contradictory.

Third and fourth divisions (benefit to a third party or no benefit) are dismissed by reference to the first proof.

Finally, the author distinguishes the 'witnessed' (shāhid, i.e., human masters) from the 'absent' (ghā'ib, i.e., God). In human cases, punishment is justified because the master has a personal interest. In the divine case, since God has no need, punishment for mandating self-benefit is evil. Thus the second proof concludes that obligation is impossible under rationalist ethics.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
قبيح
عقاب
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is punishing a servant for disobeying a command considered good (ḥasan) in the human context according to the passage?
A) Because the servant deserves it.
B) Because the master loses benefit from the servant's action.
C) Because it teaches the servant a lesson.
D) Because God commands it.

Key Takeaway
The lack of divine need makes punishment for self-benefit obligations morally impossible, completing the second proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short in your prayers, do you feel you have failed God or failed yourself? How does this lesson's distinction between the shahid and the gha'ib force you to rethink that guilt?
?
If God has no need of your obedience, then why does your disobedience matter? What does this lesson reveal about the real reason you should be obedient?
?
Think of a time you felt proud of a good deed. Did you secretly think God owed you something for it? How does the refutation of the third and fourth divisions expose that subtle arrogance in your heart?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
The Master-Slave Analogy: Punishment for Self-Benefit Is Contradictory
Analogy showing that punishment for failing to benefit oneself is contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why punishing a person for failing to benefit themselves is logically contradictory, as illustrated by the master-slave analogy.

Core Idea
If a command benefits only the servant, punishment for failure is absurd.

This lesson examines a powerful analogy used in kalam to argue against the moral justification of divine punishment. The analogy presents a master who commands his slave to earn a dirham for the slave's own benefit, then punishes the slave when he fails. Through this scenario, we see the logical contradiction that arises if God's commands are truly for our benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the full analogy from the passage, noting the setup: master commands slave to earn a dirham for himself, slave fails, master punishes.
Pay attention to the dialogue between master and slave.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The slave asks: did you command for your benefit or mine? Identify the two possibilities and their outcomes.
3
Understand the contradiction
The second scenario (command for slave's benefit) leads to a contradiction: punishment undermines the care for the slave's own interests.
This is the key argument against divine punishment if God's commands are for our good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا التكليف إلا حصول منافع مخصوصة إلى كل المقصود رعاية أحوالي فتعذيبي على تركها يناقض رعاية أحوالي فكان الجمع بينهما متناقضاً. ومثاله: أن يقول السيد لعبده: اجتهد في هذا اليوم في كسب درهم لنفسك، فقصر العبد في ذلك، فأخذه السيد، وقرض أعضاءه بمقاريض من النار. فيقول العبد: أيها السيد هل كنت في ذلك الدرهم لنفسك؟ أو كنت فارغاً عن جميع المطامع العائدة إليك، وإنما أمرتني بكسب ذلك الدرهم لمصالح نفسي فقط؟ فإن كان الأول، كان هذا التعذيب حسناً، لأني سعيت في تفويت مطلوبك. وأما إن كان الحق هو الثاني، كان هذا الفعل باطلاً. لأن العبد يقول: إنما أمرتني بكسب ذلك الدرهم لنفسي، ولتكون منافعه عائدة إلي، لا إليك. فلما قصرت في تحصيله، فأنا ما قصرت إلا في تحصيل المنفعة لنفسي. وتعذيب الإنسان لأجل أنه قصر في تحصيل مصالح نفسه، قبيح في العقول. لأن رعاية مصالحه، إن لم تكن واجبة الرعاية، كان تكليفه لتحصيل ذلك الدرهم لنفسه: غير واجب. وإن كانت واجبة الرعاية، فأهم المهمات له: إزالة العقاب. فكان إيصال العقاب إليه، لأجل أنه قصر في حق نفسه: فعلاً متناقضاً.

وبهذا يظهر الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب فإن السيد إذا أمر عبده بعمل، فقصر فيه، فإنه يستحق الملامة والتعذيب. وذلك لأن السيد إذا أمره بذلك العمل، ليستفيد منه نوعاً من أنواع المنافع، فلما قصر العبد فيه، فقد فات على السيد تلك المنافع، فحسن منه تعذيب ذلك العبد. أما هذا في حق الله محال. فكان تعذيب العبد على ترك التكاليف والأعمال: قبيحاً. فظهر الفرق بين الشاهد وبين الغائب.

الأولى.

وأما القسم الثالث والرابع: فهما باطلان بالوجوه المذكورة في الحجة
This obligation (taklīf) except [for] obtaining specific benefits to… all the purpose is the care of my states, so punishing me for abandoning them contradicts the care of my states, so combining them is contradictory. Its example: that the master says to his slave: strive today to earn a dirham for yourself, and the slave fell short in that, so the master took him and cut his limbs with scissors of fire. The slave says: O master, were you in that dirham for yourself? Or were you free from all desires returning to you, and you only commanded me to earn that dirham for my own interests? If the first, then this punishment is good, because I strove to thwart your desire. But if the truth is the second, this act is invalid. Because the slave says: You only commanded me to earn that dirham for myself, and for its benefits to return to me, not to you. So when I fell short in obtaining it, I only fell short in obtaining benefit for myself. And punishing a person because he fell short in obtaining his own interests is ugly (qabīḥ) in the intellects. Because the care of his interests, if it is not obligatory to care for, then his being obligated (taklīf) to obtain that dirham for himself is not obligatory. And if it is obligatory to care for, then the most important of important matters for him is removal of punishment. So bringing punishment to him because he fell short in his own right is a contradictory act.

And by this the difference between the visible (al-shāhid) and the invisible (al-ghā'ib) appears. For when a master commands his slave to do a work and he falls short, he deserves blame and punishment. That is because when the master commands him to do that work to obtain some kind of benefit, and when the slave falls short, those benefits are lost to the master, so punishing that slave is good (ḥasan). But this is impossible regarding God. So punishing the slave for abandoning obligations and works is ugly (qabīḥ). Thus the difference between the visible and the invisible appears.

The First.

And as for the third and fourth divisions: they are invalid by the arguments mentioned in the argument (al-ḥujja).
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument against the moral goodness of religious obligations (taklīf) by using an analogy and then a crucial distinction between the visible and invisible realms. The author first sets up a scenario: a master commands his slave to earn a dirham for himself. The slave fails, and the master tortures him. The slave then asks whether the master commanded it for his own benefit or for the slave's benefit. If for the master, punishment is just because the slave thwarted his master's desire. But if for the slave's own benefit, punishment is absurd because the slave only harmed himself. The author argues that this is self-contradictory: caring for the slave's states and then punishing him for not benefiting himself cannot be reconciled. The argument then extends to a general principle distinguishing the visible world (shāhid) from the invisible world (ghā'ib). In the visible world, when a master commands a servant for the master's own benefit and the servant fails, punishment is reasonable. But with God, who needs nothing from us, commanding us for our own benefit and then punishing us for failing to benefit ourselves is morally repugnant. The passage concludes by noting that the third and fourth divisions (presumably of a larger classification of arguments) are invalid based on the same reasoning already given in earlier arguments.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklīf)
حسن (ḥusn)
قبح (qubh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Punishment for failing to obey God's commands is always just because the commands are for our own benefit.
Correction: If commands are truly for our own benefit, punishing us for failing to benefit ourselves is contradictory, as the analogy shows.

Checkpoint
In the analogy, if the master commanded the slave to earn a dirham for the slave's own benefit, why is punishing the slave for failing contradictory?
A) Because the master lost nothing and the slave only harmed himself.
B) Because the slave intended to earn the dirham but was prevented.
C) Because the master's command was unclear.
D) Because the slave deserves punishment for any shortcoming.

Key Takeaway
The master-slave analogy shows that punishing someone for failing to benefit themselves is logically contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short in an act of worship that is clearly for your own benefit (like prayer or charity), do you realize that you are only harming yourself? How does this analogy affect your view of Allah's punishment for such neglect?
?
If you truly believed that every command of Allah is for your own good—not for His benefit—would your obedience become more eager and less resentful? What single command do you struggle to obey because you doubt it benefits you?
?
The slave argued that punishment for self-benefit failure is contradictory. Do you live as though you believe that? When you sin, do you feel the ugliness (qubh) of harming yourself, or do you simply fear punishment? How can you train your soul to see sin as self-harm?

Rational Judgments

The Distinction Between Visible and Invisible Worlds in Moral Reasoning
Distinction between shahid and ghaib applied to divine command and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between the visible world (shahid) and the invisible world (ghaib) leads to the conclusion that God punishing us for failing to benefit ourselves is morally repugnant.

Core Idea
In the visible world, punishment makes sense because the master loses; with God, it doesn't.

Having established the internal contradiction in the analogy, the author now extends the argument to a general principle: what is true in the visible world (shahid) does not apply to the invisible world (ghaib) when it comes to divine commandments. This lesson explores that distinction and its conclusion that God's punishment would be unjust if His commands are for our own good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the difference between shahid and ghaib
Read from 'And by this the difference between the visible and the invisible appears' to the end of the passage. Note how the logic of the visible world works.
2
Analyze the logic of the visible world
In the visible world, a master punishes a slave for failing a command because the master benefits from the command. Punishment is good (hasan) because the master's benefit was lost.
3
Apply the distinction to God
Since God has no need and commands solely for our benefit, punishing us for failing to benefit ourselves would be ugly (qabīḥ). The distinction shows why we cannot analogize from human masters to God.
The passage concludes that the third and fourth divisions are invalid by this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: What is morally good or bad in human interactions directly applies to God without change.
Correction: The distinction between shahid and ghaib shows that the rules of punishment change because God is not benefited or harmed by our actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is punishing a slave in the visible world considered good (hasan)?
A) Because the master suffers a loss from the slave's failure.
B) Because the slave deserves punishment for any disobedience.
C) Because the master has absolute authority.
D) Because it deters others from failing.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between shahid and ghaib shows that punishment for failing God's commands is morally repugnant because God, unlike human masters, does not benefit from our actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you neglect a religious duty (like prayer or fasting), do you ever feel that God is 'owed' something and that He might punish you for failing to pay your debt? How does this passage challenge that feeling by showing that God never benefits from your obedience in the first place?
?
If you truly internalized that God's commands are for your own benefit and not His, would you still obey Him out of fear of punishment? Or would you obey out of gratitude and self-interest? What change in your daily practice would that shift require?
?
Think of a time you felt resentful toward God for a trial you faced. Did you subconsciously think God was punishing you for a failure? Based on this lesson, how does that assumption misunderstand God's nature? Are you ready to let go of that resentment and trust that His actions are always for your good, even when you cannot see it?

The Third Argument: Past Blessings Do Not Justify Taklif
Third argument against taklif with three proofs refuting past blessings as justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the idea that past blessings make religious obligations good, using three rational proofs.

Core Idea
Past blessings do not justify imposing difficult obligations on God's servants.

This passage presents the third argument against the moral goodness of taklif. The author first considers two possible justifications for taklif being good: past blessings or future reward. He then systematically refutes the first justification with three rational proofs, appealing to moral intuition, the nature of need, and God's supreme generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the clear division into two possibilities and then three proofs.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author divides the possible grounds for taklif being good into past blessings and future reward. He declares both invalid. Focus on the first division.
The passage then provides three proofs against past blessings.
3
Examine each of the three proofs
Read each proof carefully. The first uses moral intuition, the second examines the nature of need and blessing, the third argues from generosity.
The proofs are numbered: 'first', 'second', 'third'.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why these proofs challenge the assumption that blessings impose obligations. How do they relate to God's attributes?
The argument assumes the intellect can judge good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: لو حسن التكليف، لحسن إما لأجل النعم السالفة أو لأجل النعم اللاحقة، وهو الثواب الذي يحصل بعد ذلك، أو لا لواحد من هذين القسمين. والكل باطل.

أما بطلان القسم الأول: فالدليل عليه من وجوه:

الأول: وهو أن كل من أوصل نعمة قليلة إلى إنسان ضعيف، ثم إنه يكلف ذلك الضعيف بالأفعال الشاقة، فإن كل أحد يذمه، ويقول: إنه أعطاه شيئاً قليلاً ثم إنه يعذبه عليه، ويكلفه بتلك التكاليف الشاقة.

ونحن في هذا القسم إنما نتكلم على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه: معتبراً، بل نقول: ههنا حصل في هذه الصورة شيء يوجب الزيادة في القبح، وذلك لأن إله العالم غني عن جميع العالمين، فتكليف العبد بهذه أنه لا فائدة له في شيء منها والعبد مضار يكون في الأفعال الشاقة غاية القبح.

الثاني: إن كل نعمة أوصلها إلى العبد فقد خلق في العبد قبل تلك النعمة الاحتياج إليها، والشهوة لها. وذلك ضرر. فإذا أعطى ذلك الشيء فحينئذ يتقابل الضرر السالف، بالنعمة الحاصلة. وذلك لا يوجب النعمة. هذا إذا أعطى كل ما أحوج إليها، وقد حصل عنده من الحاجة بحر، وقطرة من المحتاج إليه. ومثل هذه المعاملة لا توجب الشكر.

الثالث: إن من أنعم على ضعيف بنعمة، ثم يتركه مع نفسه، ولا يكلفه عملاً شاقاً في مقابلة تلك النعمة السالفة كان ذلك أدخل في المدح والثناء، والجود والكرم، مما إذا أتبع ذلك الإنعام بالتكاليف الشاقة. وأكرم الأكرمين هو الله رب العالمين. فكيف يليق بجوده وكرمه، أن يعامل العبد
The third argument: If obligation (taklīf) were good, it would be good either because of past blessings (al-ni‘am al-sālifa) or because of future blessings (al-ni‘am al-lāḥiqa), which is the reward (thawāb) that occurs after that, or not for one of these two divisions. And all are invalid.

As for the invalidity of the first division: the proof of it has aspects:

First: That everyone who conveys a small blessing to a weak person, then he obliges that weak person with difficult actions, then everyone blames him, and says: He gave him a small thing, then he punishes him for it, and obliges him with those difficult obligations.

And we in this division only speak on the assumption that the intellect's judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-‘aql wa taqbīḥuhu) is considered. Rather we say: Here in this image something occurs that necessitates an increase in ugliness (qubḥ), and that is because the God of the world is independent of all worlds, so the obligation of the servant with these—there is no benefit in any of it—and the servant is harmed, being in difficult actions; it is extreme ugliness.

Second: That every blessing He conveys to the servant, He has created in the servant before that blessing a need for it and a desire for it. And that is harm. So when He gives that thing, then the previous harm is offset by the obtained blessing. And that does not necessitate the blessing. This is if He gave everything He made him need, while he has an ocean of need and a drop of the needed. Such treatment does not necessitate gratitude.

Third: That whoever bestows a blessing upon a weak person, then leaves him to himself, and does not oblige him with a difficult work in exchange for that past blessing, that is more deserving of praise and commendation, and generosity and nobility, than if he followed that bestowal with difficult obligations. And the Most Generous of the generous is God, Lord of the worlds. So how does it befit His generosity and nobility that He treat the servant [so]?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the moral goodness of religious obligations (taklīf). The author first divides the possible grounds for taklif being good: either because of past blessings (i.e., God's previous gifts to us) or because of future reward (thawāb). He then declares both to be invalid and proceeds to refute the first division with three distinct proofs.

1. The first proof uses a moral intuition: if someone gives a small gift to a weak person and then imposes heavy tasks on him, everyone will condemn that behavior. This ugliness is increased when we consider that God is absolutely self-sufficient; He gains nothing from our obligations, so imposing hardship without benefit is pure ugliness.

2. The second proof focuses on the nature of need. Before every blessing, God created in us a need and desire for it, which itself is a harm (ḍarar). The blessing only offsets that prior harm, so it does not constitute a net favor that warrants imposing obligations. The scale is unbalanced: we have an ocean of need and only a drop of what is needed.

3. The third proof argues that the nobler act is to bestow a blessing without demanding anything in return. Since God is the most generous, it would be inconsistent with His generosity to follow His gifts with heavy obligations. The passage ends with a rhetorical question driving home this point.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklīf)
نعم سالفة (ni‘am sālifa)
ثواب (thawāb)
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه (taḥsīn al-‘aql wa taqbīḥuhu)
قبح (qubḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Past blessings from God make it good for Him to impose difficult obligations.
Correction: The author argues that blessings only offset the harm of created need, do not create a surplus, and that generosity is shown by giving without demanding return.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what are the two possible grounds for taklif being good?
A) Past blessings and future reward
B) God's power and mercy
C) Human reason and revelation
D) Justice and wisdom

Key Takeaway
The third argument refutes the idea that past blessings make taklif good by showing that blessings are offset by prior need, that imposing heavy tasks after small gifts is morally condemned, and that true generosity gives without demanding return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing from Allah, do you feel an unspoken pressure to 'earn' it through extra worship? How does this argument challenge that tendency, and what would it mean to accept blessings as pure gift without demanding repayment?
?
Consider a time you felt burdened by religious obligations. Did you ever think, 'I have so much, I must do more'? How does the second proof—that your need for the blessing was created by Him and offsets it—change your view of your 'debt' to Allah?
?
The third proof says it is nobler to give without asking for anything in return. Are there areas in your life where you give (charity, time, help) with hidden expectations of return? How does God's perfect generosity expose your own conditional giving, and what one concrete step can you take to give more freely?

Third Proof, First Division: Past Benefits Do Not Justify Obligation
Refutes the claim that obligation is good for past benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why past blessings cannot justify moral obligation according to the three rational arguments presented.

Core Idea
Past blessings do not make obligation good; they actually increase blame.

This lesson continues the third proof against the goodness of obligation. The author now examines the first possible justification: that obligation could be good because of past blessings. He presents three clear arguments showing why this reasoning fails, using rational analogies and principles of generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the proof against obligation for past blessings.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the three arguments
Identify the three distinct arguments the author lists: the blame analogy, the prior need cancellation, and the generosity principle.
3
Analyze the first argument
Consider the analogy of a person giving a small gift and then imposing heavy duties. Notice how everyone would blame such behavior, and how this reflects on God's independence.
The phrase 'and the slave is harmful' means the slave does not benefit.
4
Analyze the second argument
Reflect on how God creates need before granting a blessing, so the prior need cancels out the blessing. The blessing does not warrant gratitude because it only satisfies a created need.
5
Reflect on the third argument
Consider the generosity principle: leaving the slave without obligation after a blessing is more generous than imposing obligation. Since God is most generous, He would not choose the inferior way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو حسن التكليف ، لحسن إما لأجل النعم السالفة أو لأجل النعم اللاحقة ، وهو الثواب الذي يحصل بعد ذلك ، أو لا لواحد من هذين القسمين . والكل باطل .
أما بطلان القسم الأول : فالدليل عليه من وجوه :
الأول : وهو أن كل من أوصل نعمة قليلة إلى إنسان ضعيف ، ثم إنه يكلف ذلك الضعيف بالأفعال الشاقة ، فإن كل أحد يذمه ، ويقول : إنه أعطاه شيئاً قليلاً ثم إنه يعذبه عليه ، ويكلفه بتلك التكاليف الشاقة .
ونحن في هذا القسم إنما نتكلم على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه : معتبراً ، بل نقول : ههنا حصل في هذه الصورة شيء يوجب الزيادة في القبح ، وذلك لأن إله العالم غني عن جميع العالمين ، فتكليف العبد بهذه أنه لا فائدة له في شيء منها والعبد مضار يكون في الأفعال الشاقة غاية القبح
الثاني : إن كل نعمة أوصلها إلى العبد فقد خلق في العبد قبل تلك النعمة الاحتياج إليها ، والشهوة لها . وذلك ضرر . فإذا أعطى ذلك الشيء فحينئذ يتقابل الضرر السالف ، بالنعمة الحاصلة . وذلك لا يوجب النعمة . هذا إذا أعطى كل ما أحوج إليها ، وقد حصل عنده من الحاجة بحر ، وقطرة من المحتاج إليه . ومثل هذه المعاملة لا توجب الشكر
الثالث : إن من أنعم على ضعيف بنعمة ، ثم يتركه مع نفسه ، ولا يكلفه عملاً شاقاً في مقابلة تلك النعمة السالفة كان ذلك أدخل في المدح والثناء ، والجود والكرم ، مما إذا أتبع ذلك الإنعام بالتكاليف الشاقة . وأكرم الأكرمين هو الله رب العالمين. فكيف يليق بجوده وكرمه ، أن يعامل العبد بالطريق الأدون الأنقص ؟
The third proof: If obligation were good, it would be good either for the sake of past blessings or for the sake of future blessings, and that is the reward that occurs after that, or for neither of these two divisions. All are false.
As for the falsity of the first division: the proof for it is from several aspects:
The first: that whoever conveys a small blessing to a weak person, then obliges that weak person with difficult acts, then everyone would blame him and say: he gave him a small thing and then punishes him for it and obliges him with those difficult obligations.
And we in this section only speak on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered. Rather we say: here in this situation something has occurred that necessitates an increase in evil, and that is because the God of the world is independent of all the worlds. So obliging the slave with these—that there is no benefit for Him in any of them—and the slave is harmful—is utmost evil in the difficult acts.
The second: Every blessing He conveys to the slave—He created in the slave prior to that blessing the need for it and desire for it. That is harm. When He gives that thing, then the past harm is balanced against the obtained blessing, and that does not constitute a blessing. This is if He gives all that He made him need, while there has occurred from him a sea of need and a drop of what is needed. Such treatment does not necessitate gratitude.
The third: Whoever bestows a blessing on a weak person, then leaves him alone and does not oblige him with difficult work in return for that past blessing, that is more deserving of praise and commendation, and generosity and nobility, than if he followed that bestowal with difficult obligations. And the most generous of the generous is God, Lord of the worlds. So how is it befitting His generosity and nobility to treat the slave in the lower, inferior way?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is the first division of the third proof, which argues that moral obligation (taklif) cannot be considered good (hasan) either for past blessings or future blessings. Here the author refutes the possibility that obligation is justified by past blessings. He gives three arguments:
1. The first argument uses a rational analogy: If a person gives a small gift then imposes heavy duties, everyone would blame him. Since God is independent and gets no benefit, such obligation increases blame. The phrase 'and the slave is harmful' suggests the slave derives no real benefit either.
2. The second argument notes that God creates in the slave the need and desire for a blessing before granting it, so the prior need is a harm that cancels out the blessing. The benefit is not a pure gift since it only satisfies a need He Himself created.
3. The third argument says that leaving the slave without obligation after a blessing is more generous than imposing duties. Since God is the most generous, He would not choose the inferior way. These three lines of reasoning aim to show that past blessings cannot make obligation good.

Key Terms
التكليف
النعم السالفة
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
غاية القبح
الضرر
الشكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies God's generosity.
Correction: The argument uses human standards of generosity to show that imposing obligation after a blessing is less generous than leaving without obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument of the third proof, why is obligation for past blessings blameworthy?
A) Because God gains benefit from it.
B) Because it is like giving a small gift then imposing heavy duties, which is blameworthy.
C) Because obligations are always harmful.
D) Because past blessings are not real.

Key Takeaway
Past blessings cannot justify obligation because they lead to blame, cancel out the blessing, and contradict divine generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a blessing from Allah that you often use as an excuse to ask for more from Him. If that blessing came after a need He created in you, are you truly grateful or just seeing it as a small drop compared to your sea of needs?
?
Is there a relationship in your life where you are the giver, and you are silently burdening the other with expectations because of your past generosity? This lesson calls generosity without strings attached as higher. Will you release that person today?

Refuting Future Benefits as Justification for Obligation
Refutes the claim that obligation is good for future benefits via two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars argue that the claim 'obligation aims at future benefits' is rationally impossible, based on two key counterarguments.

Core Idea
What is called 'benefit' often becomes punishment—obligation cannot be justified by an outcome it fails to produce.

In this passage, the author tackles a common theological argument: that divine obligation is good because it leads to future rewards. He offers two devastating objections, grounded in observable reality and rational ethics. These objections show that the very structure of obligation, as experienced by many, contradicts the claim of intended benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
2
Identify the two arguments
Notice the phrase 'من وجهين' (from two aspects). The first argument is based on observed outcome; the second is a hypothetical scenario.
The second argument is more elaborate, listing worldly factors that lead to sin.
3
Analyze the first argument
Consider: If obligation often results in punishment for disbelievers and sinners, how can it be said to aim at their benefit? The contradiction is clear.
This uses empirical observation.
4
Analyze the second argument
Imagine a master who burdens a weak slave with many needs and temptations, then claims he intended good. Reason would judge that as harm, not benefit. This mirrors God's relation to humans?
The argument relies on rational judgement (tahsin wa taqbeeh).
5
Connect to rational ethics
The passage concludes that these two aspects negate the permissibility of obligation, based on the principle that reason can judge good and evil. Reflect on how this principle is used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : هو أن يقال : إنه إنما كلفه لأجل المنافع المستقبلة فنقول : هذا أيضاً ممتنع . وبيانه من وجهين :
الأول : وهو : أنه لما علم من أحوال الكفار والفساق أنهم لا يتوصلون بهذا التكليف إلا لاستحقاق العذاب الشديد ، والآلام العظيمة . كان القول بأنه إنما كلفهم للفوز بالمنافع : كلام متناقض .
الثاني : وهو أنه لو أراد بهم ذلك ، لوجب أن يفرغهم من متاعب الدنيا وآلامها ، وأن يظهر لهم الدلائل الواضحة ، في أن الحق هو ذلك . ولما لم يفعل ذلك ، بل أحوجهم إلى الأشياء الكثيرة ، وعلم أن تلك الحاجات تحملهم على المعاصي ، ثم سلط عليهم الشهوات والشبهات، وسلط عليهم شياطين الإنس والجن . ومن فعل بالعبد الضعيف الذي يقل عقله ، وتضعف قدرته هذه المعاملة ، ثم يزعم : أنه أراد به الخير، والفوز بالرحمة ، كانت العقول تنادي عليه : بأنه ما قصد إلا الإساءة إليه والإيذاء . والكلام في هذه المباحث مفرع على تحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، فثبت : أن هذين الوجهين ينافيان القول بجواز التكليف
As for the second division: it is said that He only obligated him for the sake of future benefits. We say: this is also impossible. Its explanation is from two aspects:
The first: that since it is known from the state of the disbelievers and sinners that they do not attain by this obligation except deserving severe punishment and great pains, the statement that He only obligated them for winning benefits is contradictory speech.
The second: that if He intended that for them, He would have to free them from the troubles of the world and its pains, and show them clear proofs that the truth is that. And since He did not do that, but rather made them in need of many things, and knew that those needs lead them to sins, then He empowered against them desires and doubts, and empowered against them the devils of humans and jinn. And whoever treats the weak slave of little intellect and weak power in this way, then claims that He intended good for him and winning mercy, minds would declare against him that he intended nothing but harm and injury. And the discussion in these investigations is based on rational judgment of good and evil. So it is established that these two aspects negate the saying of the permissibility of obligation.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is the second division of the third proof, which refutes the justification of obligation based on future benefits. The author gives two arguments:
1. The first argument observes that in reality, many disbelievers and sinners only incur punishment through obligation, so claiming that obligation aims at their benefit is contradictory.
2. The second argument is more elaborate: If God truly wanted good for them, He would have removed worldly hardships, provided clear guidance, and prevented the factors that lead to sin—such as needs, desires, doubts, and devils. Since He did not, and instead created these obstacles, it is unreasonable to claim He intended good. Reason itself would judge that such treatment is harmful, not beneficial. The author concludes that both arguments, based on rational ethics (tahsin wa taqbeeh), disprove the permissibility of obligation.

العذاب الشديد
المعاصي
الشهوات
الشبهات
شياطين الإنس والجن
جواز التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument denies God's wisdom or mercy altogether.
Correction: The argument only refutes a specific justification for obligation (future benefits), not God's overall wisdom.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the claim that obligation aims at future benefits contradictory?
A) Because future benefits are uncertain.
B) Because many people incur punishment through obligation, not benefit.
C) Because obligation is inherently evil.
D) Because God does not care about benefits.

Key Takeaway
The two arguments demonstrate that obligation cannot be justified by future benefits, because in reality it often leads to punishment, and if benefit were intended, the conditions of obligation would be different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Can you still accept Allah's decree without resentment, knowing this argument is only hypothetical and not about Allah's actual justice?

Fourth Proof: God's Power to Create in Paradise Refutes Benefit Thesis
Argues from God's power to show obligation is not for benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof uses God's omnipotence to argue that obligation (taklif) cannot be for the sake of providing benefits, because God could have granted those benefits directly in paradise.

Core Idea
If God could create all in paradise, obligation is not needed for benefit.

This lesson presents the fourth proof against the view that God's commands are for human benefit. The author argues from God's absolute power: if God wanted good for people, He would have created them directly in paradise. Since He did not, obligation cannot be for their benefit. This argument challenges the very purpose of religious obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a premise, a conditional, an observation, and a conclusion.
The text is cut off at the end; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the statement about God's power to create in paradise, and the conditional 'if He intended good...'. Then note the actual state of the world filled with desires, doubts, and devils.
3
Understand the conclusion and the counterargument
The argument concludes that God did not intend good, so obligation cannot be for benefit. Then it refutes the idea that obligation has no purpose by calling it absurd (عبث), which is rationally evil.
This assumes rational ethics (tahsin wa taqbeeh).

الحجة الرابعة : إنه تعالى كان قادراً على أن يخلق الكل في الجنة ، وأن يوصلهم إلى الخيرات والدرجات ، وأن يحميهم عن منازل الآفات والمخافات فلو أراد بهم خيراً لخلقهم على هذه الصفة ولما لم يفعل ذلك ، بل سلط عليهم الشهوات والشبهات ، وملأ العالم من الشياطين . علمنا أنه ما أراد بهم خيراً . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يقال : إنه كلفهم لأجل التعريض للمصالح . أما القول الأول : وهو أنه كلفهم لا لغرض ومصلحة ، فهذا عبث ، والعبث قبيح في العقل ، ونحن إنما نتكلم الآن على تسليم أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً . فيشت بهذه البيانات : أن على تقدير أن يكون تحسين
The fourth proof: God Most High was able to create everyone in paradise, and to convey them to goods and degrees, and to protect them from the abodes of afflictions and fears. If He intended good for them, He would have created them in this condition. And when He did not do that, but rather empowered against them desires and doubts, and filled the world with devils, we know that He did not intend good for them. And if the matter is so, it is impossible to say that He obligated them for the sake of exposure to benefits. As for the first statement—that He obligated them without purpose or interest—this is absurd, and absurdity is evil in reason. And we only speak now on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered. Thus it is established by these proofs that on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered...
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof against the goodness of obligation (taklif). The author argues from God's omnipotence: if God wanted good for people, He could have simply created them directly in paradise, free from harm. But instead He created them in a world filled with desires, doubts, and devils—factors that lead to sin and punishment. Therefore, God did not intend good for them through obligation. The author then considers a possible counter: maybe obligation has no purpose at all, but that would be absurd (عبث), and absurdity is rationally evil. Since the discussion assumes rational ethics (tahsin wa taqbeeh), the conclusion is that obligation cannot be justified. The text is cut off, but the argument aims to show that the only remaining possibility (obligation for exposure to benefits) is defeated because God could have granted benefits directly.

Two Arguments Refuting Obligation for Future Benefits
The author provides two arguments refuting the claim that obligation is for future benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments that refute the claim that divine obligation is for future benefits, and how they rely on rational judgment of good and evil.

Core Idea
Obligation cannot be for future benefit; it leads to punishment and God's actions contradict that intent.

This passage continues the discussion on the nature of divine obligation (taklif). After having dealt with one division of arguments, the author now addresses the second division: the claim that obligation is decreed for the sake of future benefits. He presents two 'faces' or arguments that demonstrate the impossibility of this claim, drawing on empirical observation and rational consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The passage begins by stating the opposing view: that obligation is for future benefits. Note that the author immediately declares this impossible.
This sets up the two arguments to follow.
2
Examine the first argument (face)
The first argument uses observed consequences: disbelievers and sinners only attain severe punishment through obligation, so claiming benefit is contradictory.
This is an empirical observation.
3
Examine the second argument (face)
The second argument uses a counterfactual: if God intended benefit, He would have freed them from worldly troubles and provided clear proofs. Instead, He subjected them to desires, doubts, and devils.
This is a logical inconsistency in the opponent's position.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The passage links these arguments to the rational judgment of good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih). This foundation shows that reason can discern the contradiction.
This concept is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : هو أن يقال : إنه إنما كلفه لأجل المنافع المستقبلة فنقول : هذا أيضاً ممتنع . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : وهو : أنه لما علم من أحوال الكفار والفساق أنهم لا يتوصلون بهذا التكليف إلا لاستحقاق العذاب الشديد ، والآلام العظيمة . كان القول بأنه إنما كلفهم للفوز بالمنافع : كلام متناقض . الثاني : وهو أنه لو أراد بهم ذلك ، لوجب أن يفرغهم من متاعب الدنيا وآلامها ، وأن يظهر لهم الدلائل الواضحة ، في أن الحق هو ذلك . ولما لم يفعل ذلك ، بل أحوجهم إلى الأشياء الكثيرة ، وعلم أن تلك الحاجات تحملهم على المعاصي ، ثم سلط عليهم الشهوات والشبهات، وسلط عليهم شياطين الإنس والجن . ومن فعل بالعبد الضعيف الذي يقل عقله ، وتضعف قدرته هذه المعاملة ، ثم يزعم : أنه أراد به الخير، والفوز بالرحمة ، كانت العقول تنادي عليه : بأنه ما قصد إلا الإساءة إليه والإيذاء . والكلام في هذه المباحث مفرع على تحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، فثبت : أن هذين الوجهين ينافيان القول بجواز التكليف
As for the second division: it is that it is said: He only obligated him for the sake of future benefits. We say: this is also impossible. And its explanation is from two faces. The first: it is that since it is known from the states of the disbelievers and wicked that they only attain through this obligation is deserving severe punishment and great pains, the statement that He only obligated them for attaining benefits is contradictory speech. The second: it is that if He intended that for them, He would have freed them from the troubles of the world and its pains, and would have shown them clear evidences so that the truth is that. But when He did not do that, rather He made them need many things, and knew that those needs lead them to sins, then He subjected them to desires and doubts, and subjected them to the devils of humans and jinn. And whoever does to the weak servant whose intellect is little and whose ability is weak this treatment, then claims that He intended good for him and attaining mercy, minds would pronounce against him that he intended nothing but harm and injury to him. And the discussion in these investigations is branched upon the rational judgment of good and evil. So it is established that these two faces contradict the statement claiming the permissibility of obligation.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the claim that divine obligation (taklif) is for the sake of future benefits. The author classifies this as the second division of arguments and immediately states that it is impossible. He then provides two sub-arguments (two faces). The first face is based on empirical observation: from the condition of disbelievers and sinners, we see that obligation only leads them to deserve severe punishment and great pains. Therefore, claiming that obligation aims at their benefit is self-contradictory. The second face is a hypothetical argument: if God truly intended benefit for them, He would have removed the difficulties of the world and provided clear proofs of the truth. But instead, He made them dependent on many things, knowing that these needs lead to sin, and He subjected them to desires, doubts, and devils. The author argues that such treatment, followed by a claim of intending good, would be universally condemned as mere harm. The passage notes that this entire discussion rests on the assumption that reason can judge good and evil (tahsin al-aql wa taqbihihi). The conclusion is that these two faces negate the permissibility of obligation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine obligation always aims at the benefit of the obligated person.
Correction: The passage shows that obligation often leads to punishment for disbelievers, and God's actions (subjecting them to trials) contradict any claim of intended benefit.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The two faces demonstrate that the claim of obligation for future benefits is self-contradictory based on observed reality and logical consistency.

Two Positions on Rational Judgment and the Invalidation of Obligation
The author examines two positions on rational judgment and concludes obligation is invalid in both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that the notion of divine obligation is self-defeating, whether or not rational judgment of good and evil is accepted.

Core Idea
Whether we accept rational judgment or not, divine obligation collapses.

The author now delivers the knockout blow. Having examined the basis of rational judgment, he shows that on either view—whether we accept that reason can judge God's actions or not—the very notion of obligation (taklif) and prophethood becomes impossible. This passage is a powerful argument that the entire edifice of religious duty rests on a logical dead end.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: first assumption, second assumption, conclusion.
The argument is compact; read carefully.
2
Identify the two assumptions
Notice that the author considers two positions: (1) rational judgment is considered in God's actions, and (2) rational judgment is not considered. Identify the phrase for each.
3
Trace the consequences
For the first assumption, the author argues that obligation without purpose is futility, which is evil. For the second, he lists even more absurd consequences. Write them down.
The consequences are meant to show impossibility.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that obligation and prophethood are false on both assumptions, so they are absolutely false. Reflect on the strength of this reductio ad absurdum.
This is a classic kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القول الأول : وهو أنه كلفهم لا لغرض ومصلحة ، فهذا عبث ، والعبث قبيح في العقل ، ونحن إنما نتكلم الآن على تسليم أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً . فيشت بهذه البيانات : أن على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً في أفعال الله تعالى وفي أحكامه ، وجب الجزم بفساد التكليف ، وبفساد بعثة الأنبياء والرسل . وأما القول الثاني : وهو أن يكون الحق هو أن تحسين العقل وتقبيحه باطل، وغير معتبر في أفعال الله تعالى وفي أحكامه . فنقول : فعل هذا التقدير ، كان القول بفساد التكليف والبعثة : أظهر . لأن على هذا التقدير ، لا يمتنع إظهار المعجز ، على يد الكاذب، ولا يمتنع إرسال الرسل بالفحش والكذب وشتم الله وشتم الملائكة، ولا يمتنع إرسال الرسل إلى الجمادات ، ولا يحصل الوثوق بوعد الله تعالى ، ولا بوعيده . وكل ذلك يوجب القول بفساد التكليف والبعثة . فثبت : أنه لو صح القول بالتكليف وبعثة الأنبياء والرسل ، لصح أما على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً ، وإما على تقدير أن لا يكون ذلك معتبراً ، وثبت أنه باطل على التقديرين فكان ذلك أيضاً باطل.
As for the first statement: that He obligated them not for a purpose or interest, this is futility, and futility is rationally evil. And we are only speaking now upon the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered. So these clarifications show that on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered in the actions and rulings of God, exalted, the corruption of obligation and the corruption of sending prophets and messengers must be affirmed. And as for the second statement: that the truth is that rational judgment of good and evil is false and not considered in the actions and rulings of God, exalted. We say: on this assumption, the saying of the corruption of obligation and sending is more obvious. Because on this assumption, it is not impossible to show a miracle at the hand of a liar, nor is it impossible to send messengers with obscenity, lies, reviling God and reviling the angels, nor is it impossible to send messengers to inanimate objects, nor is trust obtained in God's promise nor in His threat. And all that necessitates the saying of the corruption of obligation and sending. So it is established: that if the statement of obligation and sending of prophets and messengers were correct, it would be correct either on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered, or on the assumption that it is not considered. And it is established that it is false on both assumptions, so that is also false.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage concludes the overall argument by considering two possible positions regarding rational judgment of good and evil (tahsin al-aql wa taqbihihi). The first position assumes that rational judgment is valid and considered in God's actions and rulings. On this assumption, the author argues that obligation (taklif) not motivated by purpose or interest is futility (abath), which is rationally evil. Therefore, obligation and sending prophets must be deemed corrupt. The second position denies that rational judgment applies to God. On this assumption, the corruption is even more obvious: without rational standards, miracles could be shown by liars, prophets could be sent with blasphemy, and no trust could be placed in God's promises or threats. Such consequences necessitate the corruption of obligation and prophethood. The final conclusion is that on both assumptions, the institution of obligation and prophethood is proven false. Thus, whether one accepts rational judgment or not, the argument stands.

Correction: The argument does not deny God's power; it demonstrates that the notion of obligation and prophethood cannot be consistently maintained on either view of rational judgment.
