The Dilemma of Rational Good and Evil and Prophethood
The author presents a dilemma: both affirming and denying rational good and evil lead to the corruption of obligation and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dilemmatic argument against prophethood is constructed using the two positions on rational good and evil.

Core Idea
Both affirming and denying rational good and evil corrupt obligation and prophethood.

In this passage, the author presents a sophistical argument that attempts to invalidate the very concept of prophethood by posing a dilemma concerning rational good and evil. The argument claims that whether you affirm or deny that reason can judge good and evil in God's actions, the result is that obligation and sending prophets become impossible. This lesson unpacks the two horns of this dilemma and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first horn
The passage first considers the case where rational good and evil are considered valid in God's actions. According to the argument, this leads to the corruption of obligation and prophethood.
The precise reasoning is not elaborated, but the conclusion is stated as necessary.
2
Understand the second horn
Then it considers the opposite case where rational good and evil are not considered. This makes the corruption even more evident, as miracles could be given to liars and messengers sent to do evil, etc.
This horn is described as 'more apparent' in its corruption.
3
See the shared conclusion
Both horns lead to the same conclusion: obligation and prophethood are false. The argument claims that since both possibilities fail, the very claim is false.
The author is presenting this as a sophistical objection, not his own view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً في أفعال الله تعالى وفي أحكامه ، وجب الجزم بفساد التكليف ، وبفساد بعثة الأنبياء والرسل . وأما القول الثاني : وهو أن يكون الحق هو أن تحسين العقل وتقبيحه باطل، وغير معتبر في أفعال الله تعالى وفي أحكامه . فنقول : على هذا التقدير ، كان القول بفساد التكليف والبعثة : أظهر . لأن على هذا التقدير ، لا يمتنع إظهار المعجز ، على يد الكاذب، ولا يمتنع إرسال الرسل بالفحش والكذب وشتم الله وشتم الملائكة ، ولا يمتنع إرسال الرسل إلى الجمادات ، ولا يحصل الوثوق بوعد الله تعالى ، ولا بوعيده . وكل ذلك يوجب القول بفساد التكليف والبعثة . فثبت : أنه لو صح القول بالتكليف وبعثة الأنبياء والرسل ، لصح إما على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً ، وإما على تقدير أن لا يكون ذلك معتبراً ، وثبت أنه باطل على التقديرين فكان ذلك أيضاً باطلاً.
If reason and its judging of good and evil are considered in the actions of Allah Almighty and in His rulings, then it is necessary to affirm the corruption of obligation and the corruption of sending prophets and messengers. As for the second statement: that the truth is that reason's judging of good and evil is false and not considered in the actions of Allah Almighty and in His rulings. We say: on this supposition, the statement of the corruption of obligation and sending is more apparent. Because on this supposition, it is not impossible to show a miracle at the hand of a liar, nor is it impossible to send messengers with obscenity and lies and reviling Allah and reviling the angels, nor is it impossible to send messengers to inanimate objects, nor is trust attained in Allah's promise nor in His threat. And all that necessitates saying the corruption of obligation and sending. So it is established: if the statement of obligation and sending of prophets and messengers were valid, it would be valid either on the supposition that reason's judging of good and evil is considered, or on the supposition that it is not considered, and it is established that it is false on both suppositions, so that too is false.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma argument attributed to the sophists (or an opponent) designed to refute the validity of prophethood. The argument begins by considering two possible positions regarding rational good and evil (tahsin al-‘aql wa taqbīh): either it is valid and binding on Allah's actions, or it is invalid. If it is valid, then reason's judgments would constrain Allah, leading to the corruption of obligation (taklīf) and the sending of prophets—though the precise reasoning is not elaborated here, the conclusion is stated as necessary. If it is invalid, then the corruption is even more evident: miracles could be performed by liars, messengers could be sent to commit heinous acts, and no trust in Allah's promises or threats would remain. Thus both horns lead to the same conclusion: the claim of obligation and prophethood is false. The author upholds this argument as part of his presentation of the opponents' view, not as his own position.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
التكليف
البعثة
المعجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author endorses this argument as his own position.
Correction: The author is presenting this as a sophistical objection to prophethood, which he later refutes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The dilemma argument attempts to show that both affirming and denying rational good and evil in God's actions leads to the impossibility of prophethood.

The First Proof: Actions Divided into Three Kinds
The author states the sophists' claim and begins refutation by dividing actions into three kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophists’ argument that rational judgment obviates the need for prophethood, and the first stage of the rebuttal: the division of actions into three categories regarding reason’s judgment.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot judge all actions; some require prophetic guidance.

In this passage, the author begins refuting the sophists who claim that human reason is sufficient to know all obligations, thereby rendering prophethood unnecessary. He first presents their argument in a compressed form, then offers a proof that actions fall into three kinds: those reason deems good, those it deems evil, and those it suspends judgment on. This division shows that reason is not comprehensive, setting the stage for the necessity of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the sophists’ argument
Identify their claim that belief in prophethood is either combined with rational judgment or not, and both are false according to them.
This is a classic dialectical move.
2
Grasp the threefold division
Notice how the author distinguishes actions based on reason’s judgment: good, evil, and suspended.
The third category is key.
3
Examine the suspended category
See how the author analyzes the third kind: if abandoning it is possible, it becomes obligatory to avoid potential harm.
This shows reason’s limitation.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The text cuts off, but the direction is clear: reason alone cannot decide all actions, so prophets are needed.
Be aware of the OCR issues.

الفصل الرابع في تعمیر شبهات المتكوين الشبهات بالبناء على أن العقل كاف في معرفة التكليف وخلكيوجب سقوط القمل بالبعثة والرسالة. تقرير كلام هذه الطائفة : أن يقال : القول بصحة البعثة . إما أن يحصل مع القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، أو مع إبطال هذا الأصل. والقسمان باطلان، فكان القول بالبعثة : باطلاً. أما بيان فساد القسم الأول . فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الأفعال على ثلاثة أقسام : قسم قضى العقل فيه بالحسن فكان فعله صواباً ، وحكم العقل بكونه واجب القبول . وقسم ثان قضى العقل فيه بالقبح والمنع . فكان حكمه أيضاً واجب القبول. وقسم ثالث توقف العقل فيه . فلم يحكم فيه لا بحسن ولا يقبح . فنقول : هذا القسم على قمين ، لأنه إما أن يكون تركه ممكناً أو ممتنعاً . فإن كان تركه ممكناً على سبيل السهولة واليسر ، كان تركه واجباً . لأن الانفكاك عنه ممكن ، ولم يعلم أنه حسن أو قبيح، فكان الإقدام عليه إقداماً على فعل ، لا حاجة إليه . مع الضرر قائم فيه ، والعقل يقضي في مثل هذه الصورة بوجوب الترك والاحتراز ، لأن الخوف لازم له ، فالإقدام عليه التزام لضرر الخوف من غير حاجة [ ولا ان احتمال ]
Chapter Four: On Refuting the Sophists' Arguments Built on the Claim that Reason is Sufficient for Knowing Obligation and That It Necessitates the Abandonment of the Need for Sending Prophets and Messengers. Statement of this group's words: It is said: The statement of the validity of sending is either combined with the statement of reason's judging good and evil, or with the invalidation of this principle. And both divisions are false, so the statement of sending is false. As for the demonstration of the corruption of the first division, proofs indicate it: The first: Actions are of three kinds: a kind in which reason judged good, so its doing is correct, and reason ruled that it is obligatory to accept. A second kind in which reason judged evil and prohibition, so its ruling is also obligatory to accept. A third kind in which reason suspended judgment, so it did not rule either good or evil. We say: this kind is on two possibilities, because either its abandonment is possible or impossible. If its abandonment is possible easily and conveniently, then its abandonment is obligatory. Because separation from it is possible, and it is not known whether it is good or evil, so embarking upon it is embarking upon an act which is not needed, while harm exists in it, and reason judges in such a case the obligation of abandonment and caution, because fear is necessary for it, so embarking upon it is an undertaking of the harm of fear without need [and not ...]
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter aimed at refuting the sophists' claim that reason is sufficient for knowing obligation and therefore prophethood is unnecessary. The author first states their argument: the claim of valid prophethood is either combined with affirming rational good and evil or with rejecting it; both are false, so prophethood is false. Then the author begins to rebut the first division (affirming rational judgment). He presents the first proof: actions are divided into three categories. The first category is actions reason deems good; the second is those reason deems evil; the third is those reason suspends judgment on. For the third category, reason has no ruling. The argument then examines whether abandoning such actions is possible. If it is possible easily, then abandoning them is obligatory because one cannot be sure they are not harmful, and reason says to avoid potential harm. This shows that reason does not suffice to guide all actions, thus undermining the sophists' claim. The text cuts off before completing the proof.

البعثة

توقف العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason can judge all actions as good or evil, so humans do not need prophets.
Correction: The passage shows that reason cannot judge a whole category of actions (suspended), and even there, reason itself leads to avoiding them, indicating the need for prophetic guidance to fill the gap.

Checkpoint

A) Doing them is obligatory.
B) Abandoning them is obligatory.
C) They are neutral.
D) They are forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The division of actions into three kinds demonstrates that reason alone cannot determine the moral status of all actions, thereby undermining the sophists' claim that prophethood is unnecessary.

Rational Judgments

The Rational Judgment of All Actions
Presents the mutakallimun's argument that reason judges all actions, making prophethood unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that reason can judge all actions, thereby rendering prophethood unnecessary.

Core Idea
Reason alone can determine the moral status of every action, making prophethood redundant.

This passage presents a challenging argument from a group of mutakallimun who claim that since reason can judge all actions as good, evil, or permissible, there is no need for prophets. While this is not the author's own position, understanding it is crucial to grasp the refutation that follows. The argument proceeds by dividing all actions into three categories and showing that reason reaches a definitive judgment in each.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Framing
The argument begins by setting up the claim that belief in prophethood (baʿtha) either coexists with rational good/evil or rejects it; both alternatives are false, so prophethood is false. This is the logical structure for the ensuing proof.
Note that this is the opponent's view, not the author's.
2
Examine the Three Divisions of Actions
Actions are divided into three categories based on reason's judgment: those reason judges good, those it judges evil, and those it suspends judgment on. Only the third requires further analysis.
The first two are straightforward: reason commands acceptance or avoidance.
3
Analyze the Subcases of the Third Division
For actions reason does not initially judge, it further distinguishes whether avoidance is possible or not. If possible, reason obligates avoidance; if impossible, reason permits the action.
The argument uses the principle of avoiding unnecessary harm.
4
See How Reason's Universal Judgment Leads to the Conclusion
Since reason provides a judgment for every action (good, evil, obligatory avoidance, or permissible), it covers all moral knowledge. Therefore, prophethood would serve no additional purpose.
The conclusion is that prophethood is unnecessary if reason is sufficient.
5
Recognize This as an Opposing View
Remember that the author is presenting this argument to later refute it. Appreciate its logical structure without assuming it is correct.
This is a common dialectical technique in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير كلام هذه الطائفة : أن يقال : القول بصحة البعثة . إما أن يحصل مع القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، أو مع إبطال هذا الأصل والقسمان باطلان، فكان القول بالبعثة : باطلاً أما بيان فساد القسم الأول . فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الأفعال على ثلاثة أقسام : قسم قضى العقل فيه بالحسن فكان فعله صواباً ، وحكم العقل بكونه واجب القبول . وقسم ثان قضى العقل فيه بالقبح والمنع . فكان حكمه أيضاً واجب القبول وقسم ثالث توقف العقل فيه . فلم يحكم فيه لا بحسن ولا يقبح . فنقول : هذا القسم على قمين ، لأنه إما أن يكون تركه ممكناً أو ممتنعاً . فإن كان تركه ممكناً على سبيل السهولة واليسر ، كان تركه واجباً . لأن الانفكاك عنه ممكن ، ولم يعلم أنه حسن أو قبيح، فكان الإقدام عليه إقداماً على فعل ، لا حاجة إليه . مع الضرر قائم فيه ، والعقل يقضي في مثل هذه الصورة بوجوب الترك والاحتراز ، لأن الخوف لازم له ، فالإقدام عليه التزام لضرر الخوف من غير حاجة ولا ضرورة ، والتزام الضرر من غير الحاجة قبيح عند العقل ، فكان حكم العقل حاصلاً في هذا القسم بوجوب الترك وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن لا يكون تركه ممكناً، فههنا حكم العقل حاصل فيه بالجواز . لأن العقل علم أن إله العالم حكيم رحيم وأنه لا يكلف عباده فوق قدرتهم وطاقتهم ، فإذا كان ذلك الفعل مما لا يقدر العبد على الانفكاك عنه ، فلو أمره الله تعالى بالانفكاك عنه ، لكان قد كلفه ما لا طاقة له به وذلك قبيح عند العقل . ونحن إنما نتكلم الآن على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً . فيثبت : أن حكم العقل حاصل في هذا القسم أيضاً . فقد ظهر بهذا البحث : أن حكم العقل حاصل في جميع أقسام الأفعال . وإذا كان العقل كافياً في معرفة الله تعالى وما يجب وما يجوز ، ويحرم . لم يكن في البعثة فائدة .
Exposition of the speech of this group: It is said: The claim of the soundness of [prophetic] mission (baʿtha) either occurs with the claim of rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥih), or with the abolition of this principle. Both divisions are false, so the claim of mission is false. As for the demonstration of the corruption of the first division, it is indicated by several aspects: First: Actions are of three divisions: a division in which reason judged good (ḥasan), so its doing was correct, and reason judged it to be obligatory to accept. A second division in which reason judged evil (qabīḥ) and prohibition, so its judgment also is obligatory to accept. A third division in which reason suspended judgment; it did not judge it as good or evil. We say: This division is of two kinds, because either its avoidance is possible or impossible. If its avoidance is possible with ease and facility, then its avoidance is obligatory. Because dissociation from it is possible, and it is not known to be good or evil, so undertaking it is undertaking an action with no need for it, while harm is present in it, and reason in such a case judges the obligation of avoidance and caution, because fear attaches to it. So undertaking it is an assumption of the harm of fear without need or necessity, and assumption of harm without need is evil (qabīḥ) according to reason. Thus the judgment of reason is obtained in this division as the obligation of avoidance. As for the second division, which is that its avoidance is not possible, here the judgment of reason is obtained as permissibility (jawāz). Because reason knows that the God of the world is Wise and Merciful and that He does not burden His servants beyond their ability and capacity. So if that act is such that the servant cannot dissociate from it, then if God Almighty commanded him to dissociate from it, He would have burdened him with what he has no capacity for, and that is evil according to reason. And we only speak now on the assumption that rational judgment of good and evil is considered. So it is established that the judgment of reason is obtained in this division as well. So it has become clear by this investigation that the judgment of reason is obtained in all divisions of actions. And if reason is sufficient for knowledge of God Almighty and what is obligatory, permissible, and prohibited, then there is no benefit in [prophetic] mission (baʿtha).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of a group (the mutakallimūn) who claim that reason alone is sufficient for knowledge of obligation, thereby rendering the sending of prophets unnecessary. The author is setting up the argument to refute it. The passage begins by stating that the belief in the validity of prophethood (baʿtha) either occurs alongside belief in rational good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥih) or with the rejection of this principle. Both alternatives are false, so the claim of prophethood is false. Then the author expounds the first division (the claim that rational good and evil is accepted) and gives the first proof: Actions are divided into three categories: (1) those reason judges good, so doing them is correct and accepting them is obligatory; (2) those reason judges evil and prohibited, so avoiding them is obligatory; (3) those reason suspends judgment on, neither good nor evil. For this third category, reason further distinguishes two subcases: a) if avoidance is possible easily, then avoidance is obligatory because there is no benefit and harm is possible; b) if avoidance is not possible (i.e., the action is unavoidable), then reason judges it permissible because God is wise and merciful and does not impose impossible obligations. Thus reason provides a judgment in all cases. The conclusion is that if reason suffices for knowledge of God and His commands, there is no benefit in sending prophets. This is a complete deductive argument aimed at showing that the opponent's own premises lead to the futility of prophethood.

Key Terms
البعثة
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
أقسام الأفعال
حكم العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument represents the author's own position.
Correction: The author is presenting the argument of the mutakallimun in order to refute it later. The author does not endorse it.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are inherently evil.
B) Because undertaking them entails pointless risk of harm with no benefit.
C) Because God explicitly forbids them.
D) Because prophets have forbidden them.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that reason's judgments cover all actions, eliminating any need for prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage argues that reason alone can supposedly cover all moral decisions. Think of a time you chose what you thought was 'reasonable' but later realized it conflicted with divine guidance. How did that feel? Are you willing to submit your reasoning to revelation even when it doesn't make sense to you?
?
If reason truly sufficed for knowing God and right conduct, your prayer, Quran reading, and seeking knowledge would be pointless. What does that truth reveal about your actual dependence on divine guidance? How would your daily habits change if you truly believed that reason is not enough?

Four Objections to the Sufficiency of Reason and the Start of the Response
Four objections to sufficiency of reason with start of reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational objections raised against the sufficiency of reason for knowing God, and the initial response refuting the first objection.

Core Idea
The opponent's strongest arguments for prophethood reveal limits of reason that require divine guidance.

This passage presents four objections to the claim that reason suffices, showing why prophethood is still needed. The author then begins to refute the first objection, arguing that reason is independent in knowing what is praiseworthy. Understanding these objections is crucial for grasping the theological debate on prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text lists four points, each introduced by a number.
2
Identify each objection
The passage presents four objections. Find each one and understand its main idea. The first is about reinforcement, the second about hidden benefits, the third about intellectual deficiency, and the fourth about divine aids (Mu'tazili view).
The fourth objection is explicitly attributed to the Mu'tazila.
3
Analyze the author's response to the first objection
The author begins the reply by calling the first objection weak, because the intellect is independent in knowing what is praiseworthy. Note that the text cuts off, so the full rebuttal is not given here.
This is a machine-draft; the original text may continue on the next page.

فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال تحسن بعثة الأنبياء والرسل ؟ لوجوه : الأول : تأكيد ما في العقول الثاني : إنه قد يحصل في بعض الأشياء منافع ومصالح ، لا يمكن الوقوف عليها ، بمجرد العقول، فتحسن بعثة الأنبياء والرسل، ليدلوا عليها ، ويعرفوا الخلق ما فيها من المنافع والمصالح الثالث : إن عقول الخلق ناقصة قاصرة عن معرفة الله تعالى ومعرفة كيفية طاعاته . فكانت الحكمة في بعثة الأنبياء والرسل عليهم السلام : إرشاداً للخلق ، إلى معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، ومعرفة كيفية طاعاته . الرابع : إنما أمر الله تعالى بهذه العبادات الشرعية ، لتكون الطافاً في الواجبات العقلية . وهذا قول المعتزلة . قلنا : أما الأول : فضعيف . لأنه لما كان العقل مستقلاً بمعرفة وجوه الح
For if they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the sending of prophets and messengers is good? Due to several aspects: First: Reinforcing what is in the intellects. Second: Indeed, there may occur in some things benefits and interests that cannot be known by mere intellects, so the sending of prophets and messengers is good, so that they may point to them and make the creation know the benefits and interests they contain. Third: Indeed, the intellects of the creation are deficient and incapable of knowing God Almighty and knowing the manner of obeying Him. So the wisdom in sending the prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, is guidance for the creation to knowledge of the essence of God Almighty and His attributes, and knowledge of the manner of obeying Him. Fourth: Indeed, God Almighty commanded these religious acts of worship to be aids (alṭāf) in the rational obligations. And this is the saying of the Muʿtazila. We said: As for the first, it is weak. Because since the intellect is independent in knowing the aspects of praise [sentence incomplete].
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents four objections raised against the previous argument that reason suffices and makes prophethood unnecessary. The opponent asks: why can we not say that sending prophets is good? They list four reasons: (1) Prophets reinforce what is already in the intellects. (2) There are benefits and interests that cannot be known by reason alone, so prophets are needed to reveal them. (3) Human intellects are deficient in knowing God's essence, attributes, and how to worship Him, so prophets provide guidance. (4) Religious rituals serve as divine aids (alṭāf) for fulfilling rational obligations—this is attributed to the Muʿtazila. After listing these, the author begins the response, saying that the first objection is weak because the intellect is independent in knowing the aspects of praise (but the text cuts off; the full rebuttal is not present in this window). The passage functions to set up the opponent's strongest points before the author refutes them.

Rational Judgment Covers All Actions, No Need for Prophets
Concludes that rational judgment covers all actions, making prophetic mission superfluous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational judgment applies to all divisions of actions, leading to the conclusion that reason alone suffices for moral knowledge and thus prophets are not needed for that purpose.

Core Idea
Reason alone is enough to know God and moral duties – prophets are not needed for that.

In this passage, the author completes his argument that reason's judgment of good and evil covers every type of action. He shows that for actions one cannot avoid, reason rules them as permissible, and for harmful ones, as obligatory to abandon. From this, he draws the powerful conclusion that if reason can cover all actions, then there is no need for prophets to teach morality. This sets up a major objection in the larger debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage begins with the third division of actions.
2
Identify the two divisions discussed
The passage addresses the third division (actions whose omission is harmful and unavoidable) and the second division (actions that cannot be abandoned by the servant). Find these two parts in the text.
The first division was covered earlier and is not repeated here.
3
Understand the rational judgment for each division
For the third division, reason judges it as obligatory to abandon (وجوب الترك). For the second division, reason judges it as permissible (جواز) because God does not charge beyond capacity.
The author assumes that reason's approval and disapproval are valid (تحسين وتقبيح).
4
See how the conclusion follows
From the coverage of these divisions, the author concludes that rational judgment applies to all divisions of actions. Then he infers that if reason is sufficient for knowing God and moral categories, then sending a messenger has no benefit.
This conclusion directly challenges the need for prophethood in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرورة ، والتزام الضرر من غير الحاجة (۱) قبيح عند العقل ، فكان حكم العقل حاصلاً في هذا القسم بوجوب الترك . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن لا يكون تركه ممكناً، فههنا حكم العقل حاصل فيه بالجواز . لأن العقل علم أن إله العالم حكيم رحيم وأنه لا يكلف عباده فوق قدرتهم وطاقتهم ، فإذا كان ذلك الفعل مما لا يقدر العبد على الانفكاك عنه ، فلو أمره الله تعالى بالانفكاك عنه ، لكان قد كلفه ما لا طاقة له به وذلك قبيح عند العقل . ونحن إنما نتكلم الآن على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه معتبراً . فيثبت : أن حكم العقل حاصل في هذا القسم أيضاً . فقد ظهر بهذا البحث : أن حكم العقل حاصل في جميع أقسام الأفعال . وإذا كان العقل كافياً في معرفة الله تعالى وما يجب وما يجوز ، ويحرم . لم يكن في البعثة فائدة .
Harm and incurring harm without need (1) are ugly according to reason, so the judgment of reason was obtained in this section as obligation of abandoning. As for the second section, which is that its abandonment is not possible, here the judgment of reason is obtained as permissibility, because reason knows that the God of the world is wise and merciful and that He does not charge His servants above their power and capacity. So if that act is such that the servant cannot free himself from it, then if God commanded him to free himself from it, He would have charged him with what he has no capacity for, and that is ugly according to reason. We now speak on the assumption that reason's approval and disapproval are considered. So it is established that the judgment of reason is also obtained in this section. So by this investigation it has become clear that the judgment of reason is obtained in all divisions of actions. And if reason is sufficient for knowing God Most High and what is obligatory, permissible, and forbidden, then there is no benefit in sending a messenger.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on the three divisions of actions. The author first addresses the third division (actions whose omission is harmful and unavoidable), stating that reason judges them as obligatory to avoid. Then he addresses the second division (actions that cannot be abandoned by one's own power), where reason judges them as permissible because God does not impose beyond capacity. He emphasizes that this analysis assumes that reason's judgments of good and evil are valid. The conclusion is that rational judgment applies to all divisions of actions. Hence, since reason alone can know God, obligations, and prohibitions, there is no need for a prophetic mission. This sets up the objection that follows.

جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reason alone cannot establish all moral duties, especially for actions that cannot be avoided.
Correction: The author argues that reason judges such actions as permissible due to God's mercy, and for harmful actions as obligatory to avoid. Thus reason covers all divisions.

Key Takeaway
Reason's judgment covers all actions, so prophets are not needed for moral knowledge — a powerful objection in the debate on prophethood.

?
The author asserts that if reason is enough, there is no benefit in sending a messenger. Do you live as if reason is enough, or do you genuinely seek prophetic guidance in your daily life? What would change if you took this argument seriously?

Reason Sufficient for Knowledge of God
Author divides knowledge and proves reason suffices for divine matters via circularity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reason alone can provide knowledge of God's essence, attributes, acts, and judgments, and why basing divine knowledge on prophethood would lead to circularity.

Core Idea
Reason suffices for divine knowledge; reversing dependency creates a vicious circle.

The author begins by dividing all knowledge into two categories: things absent from the senses (divine matters) and things present to the senses (worldly matters). He then argues that for the first category, intellects are sufficient. The key argument is that knowledge of prophethood depends on knowledge of God; if we instead tried to base knowledge of God on prophethood, we would fall into circular reasoning. This establishes reason's independence and foundational role.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of knowledge
Read the opening lines where the author divides knowledge into two categories: things absent from the senses and things present to the senses. Note that the first category includes divine matters.
2
Grasp the claim about divine matters
The author asserts that intellects are sufficient for knowing God's essence, attributes, acts, and judgments. Consider what this implies about reason's capability.
3
Follow the circularity argument
The proof is that prophethood knowledge branches from divine knowledge. If we reverse this and base divine knowledge on prophethood, a vicious circle (dawr) occurs, which is invalid. This shows that reason must be able to attain divine knowledge independently.
Circular reasoning is a logical fallacy where the conclusion is assumed in the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان أن العقل كاف في معرفة المهمات : هو أن نقول : المطلوب إما معرفة الأشياء الغائبة عن الحواس، أو معرفة الأشياء الحاضرة عند الحواس . أما الأول فنقول : العقول كافية في معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وأفعاله وأحكامه . والدليل عليه : أن معرفة نبوة الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - متفرعة على معرفة الإلهيات ، فلو فرعنا معرفة الإلهيات على النبوات ، وقع الدور . وأنه باطل . وأما معرفة مهمات المعاش ومصالح الدنيا فإنها غير موقوفة على بعثة الأنبياء والرسل . لأنا نرى من لا يؤمن بالله ورسوله واليوم الآخر ، يسعى في تحصيل هذه المصالح على أحسن الوجه . فعلمنا : أن التصرف فيها لا يتوقف على بعثة الأنبياء ، ولأن العقول الوقادة ، والخواطر الغواصة ، وافية بتحصيل هذه المطالب ، وأما كيفية العبادات، فهي أيضاً معلومة بالعقول . لأن كل عمل يكون الإخلاص الله تعالى فيه أكمل وأتم ، كان إلى القبول أقرب ، وكل عمل يشوبه غرض [ من الأغراض العاجلة ] فإنه لا يكون في محل القبول . وأيضاً : لما شهد العقل : بأن الدنيا فانية ، وبأن الآخرة باقية ، حكم صريح العقل : بأنه يجب السعي في تقليل حب الدنيا، وتقوية حب الآخرة . فهذه الأصول هي المطالب الأصلية للخلق ، والعقول وافية فيها بأسرها . فعلمنا : أن العقول وافية بمعرفة جميع مهمات الدنيا والآخرة والدين .
The second proof concerning the explanation that reason is sufficient for the knowledge of important matters: it is that we say: what is sought is either knowledge of things absent from the senses, or knowledge of things present to the senses. As for the first, we say: intellects are sufficient for knowledge of the essence of God, His attributes, His acts, and His judgments. And the proof for it is: that knowledge of the prophethood of the prophets, peace be upon them, branches from knowledge of divine matters; for if we were to branch knowledge of divine matters from prophethood, a circularity would occur, and it is invalid. As for knowledge of important matters of livelihood and worldly interests, they are not dependent on the commissioning of prophets and messengers, because we see those who do not believe in God, His messenger, or the Last Day striving to obtain these interests in the best manner. Thus we know that disposal therein does not depend on the commissioning of prophets, and because penetrating intellects and diving thoughts are sufficient for obtaining these demands. As for the modality of acts of worship, they too are known by intellects, because every deed in which sincere devotion to God is more complete and perfect is closer to acceptance, and every deed tainted by an immediate purpose is not in a place of acceptance. Also, when reason testifies that this world is perishable and the Hereafter is lasting, the manifest judgment of reason is that one must strive to reduce love of this world and strengthen love of the Hereafter. So these principles are the original demands of creation, and intellects suffice for them all. Thus we know that intellects suffice for knowledge of all important matters of this world, the Hereafter, and religion.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (al-hujjah al-thaniyah) demonstrating the sufficiency of reason (al-`aql) for knowledge of all important matters (al-muhimmat). The author structures the proof by dividing the objects of knowledge into two categories: (1) things absent from the senses (al-gha'ib), and (2) things present to the senses (al-hadir). For the first category, the author asserts that intellects are sufficient for knowledge of God's essence (dhat), attributes (sifat), acts (af`al), and judgments (ahkam). The argument is that knowledge of prophethood is dependent on knowledge of divine matters; if we were to reverse this dependency, a vicious circle (al-dawr) would result, which is invalid. For the second category—worldly interests and modalities of worship—the author argues that reason alone suffices. Evidence includes observing that even disbelievers achieve worldly benefits well, and that reason can deduce principles of worship (such as sincerity) and the primacy of the Hereafter. Therefore, intellects are sufficient for all essential demands (al-matalib al-asliyyah) of human beings.

Reason Sufficient for Worldly Matters and Worship
Author proves reason suffices for worldly interests and worship through examples and rational principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reason alone can guide worldly interests and the modality of worship, as demonstrated by the author's arguments.

Core Idea
Reason suffices for worldly benefits and moral worship; even disbelievers excel in worldly affairs.

Having established reason's sufficiency for divine matters, the author turns to the second category: things present to the senses. He argues that reason is sufficient for important matters of livelihood and worldly interests, pointing to the fact that even non-believers manage these well. Moreover, the modality of worship can be deduced by reason, which recognizes the value of sincerity and the priority of the afterlife over this world.

Guided Path
1
Reason for worldly matters
Read the argument that worldly interests are not dependent on prophethood. Note the evidence: disbelievers achieve them well. This shows reason is sufficient.
2
Reason for worship modality
The author argues that acts of worship are known by reason because sincerity leads to acceptance, and deeds tainted by worldly motives are not accepted. Consider how reason deduces this principle.
3
Reason's judgment about afterlife priority
Reason testifies that this world is perishable and the afterlife is lasting, so one should reduce love of this world and strengthen love of the hereafter. This is a rational moral principle.

The Sufficiency of Reason for Prophethood
The author presents the third proof that reason suffices for knowing prophethood and must be the ultimate judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from Al-Matalib al-'Aliyah that rational knowledge of God is a prerequisite for knowing prophethood, and that reason's judgment remains decisive even when considered alongside revelation.

Core Idea
Reason must be the root; prophethood is the branch that cannot contradict it.

This passage presents the third formal proof that knowledge of prophethood depends on prior rational knowledge of God. The author argues that if reason can grasp the highest matters (God's essence, attributes, acts, and judgments), it is also sufficient for lower worldly and religious concerns. Moreover, when prophethood's commands are examined, they must either align with reason or conflict; in either case, reason's authority stands. This proof is foundational for establishing the proper relationship between reason and revelation in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the flow: the need for rational knowledge, the sufficiency argument, the root-branch analogy, and the conditional remark.
Pay attention to the bracketed sections; they indicate manuscript variants.
2
Identify the logical structure of the proof
The proof moves through several steps: (1) prophethood knowledge requires prior rational knowledge of God; (2) if reason suffices for high matters, it suffices for low matters; (3) prophethood branches from reason; (4) if prophets agree with reason, reason is enough; if they conflict, reason (root) is preferred; (5) this holds if rational good/bad is admitted; if not, the case against prophethood is even stronger.
This is a cumulative argument with conditional qualifications.
3
Analyze the root-branch analogy
The author uses 'root' (asl) for reason and 'branch' (far') for prophethood. When they conflict, the root must be preferred. Consider why this analogy is compelling within the author's framework.
This analogy assumes the validity of rational judgment of good and bad (tahsin wa taqbih).
4
Consider the conditional ending
The proof ends by noting that if rational judgment of good and bad is not admitted, the corruption of prophethood is even more apparent. Reflect on how this strengthens the overall argument.
This shows the author is aware of alternative positions and responds from within his own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن العلم بنبوة الأنبياء ، ووصول التكاليف من الله تعالى إلى الخلق ، لا يتم إلا بعد أن يُعرف بالعقل ، معرفة الله تعالى بذاته ، وصفاته ، وأفعاله ، وأحكامه . وإذا كان العقل وافياً بهذه المطالب العالية الشريفة ، كان أيضاً وافياً بمعرفة مصالح الدنيا والآخرة . على ما بيناه في الطريقة الثانية. وكان أيضاً : وافياً بكيفية العبادات . وإذا كان العقل بهذه المطالب ، كان أيضاً وافياً بمعرفة [ مصالح الدنيا ، لأن الوافي بمعرفة ] المهمات العالية الشريفة ، أولى أن يكون وافيا بمعرفة المهمات النازلة الحسيمة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : ظهر أن معرفة نبوة الأنبياء ، مفرعة على قبول حكم العقل في جميع هذه المطالب العالية وعند هذا نقول : حكم الأنبياء والرسل ، إن كان على وفق حكم العقل ، ففي حكم العقل غنية . وإذا كان على خلافه كان الفرع معارضا للأصل ، وعند وقوع التعارض بين الأصل والفرع ، كان ترجيح الأصل على الفرع أولى من ضده. فثبت : أن حكم العقل يجب أن يكون راجعاً على كل التقديرات .. وهذا الكلام على تقدير أن يكون تحسين العقل وتقبيحه [ معتبراً في معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وأفعاله ، وإذا قلنا : إن تحسين العقل وتقبيحه غير معتبر ، فالقول ] بفساد البعثة والنبوة الزم وأظهر ، على ما بيناه في الطريقة المتقدمة [ والله أعلم ] .
The third proof: knowledge of the prophethood of the prophets, and the arrival of obligations from God, may He be exalted, to creation, is not completed except after God, may He be exalted, is known by reason—knowledge of His essence, attributes, acts, and judgments. And if reason is sufficient for these high and noble demands, it is also sufficient for knowledge of the interests of this world and the Hereafter, as we explained in the second method. And it is also sufficient for the modality of acts of worship. And if reason is sufficient for these demands, it is also sufficient for knowledge of [the interests of this world, because that which is sufficient for knowledge of] the high and noble important matters is more likely to be sufficient for knowledge of the low and mean important matters. And if this is established, we say: it has become clear that knowledge of the prophethood of the prophets branches from acceptance of the judgment of reason in all these high demands. And at this point we say: the judgment of the prophets and messengers, if it is in accordance with the judgment of reason, then in the judgment of reason there is sufficiency. And if it is contrary to it, then the branch opposes the root, and when opposition occurs between root and branch, preferring the root over the branch is more appropriate than its opposite. So it is established that the judgment of reason must be decisive in all cases. And this speech is on the assumption that the rational judgment of good and bad is [considered in knowing the essence of God, His attributes, and His acts; and if we say that the rational judgment of good and bad is not considered, then the statement] of the corruption of the mission and prophethood is more necessary and more apparent, as we explained in the preceding method. [And God knows best].
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (al-hujjah al-thalithah) further establishing the sufficiency of reason. The author argues that knowledge of prophethood and divine obligations cannot be attained without prior rational knowledge of God's essence, attributes, acts, and judgments. Since reason is sufficient for these higher matters, it is also sufficient for lower worldly and religious interests. The proof draws an analogy: what suffices for higher matters more fittingly suffices for lower ones. Then the author examines the relationship between reason and revelation. If prophets' commands align with reason, reason is sufficient; if they conflict, the branch (prophethood) opposes the root (reason), and the root must be preferred. Thus reason's judgment is decisive in all cases. This reasoning holds, the author notes, if rational judgment of good and bad (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) is admitted in knowing God. If it is not admitted, the corruption of prophethood becomes even more apparent, as previously explained.

Key Terms

عقل
تحسين وتقبيح
أصل
فرع
مطالب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author completely subordinates revelation to reason without qualification.

Checkpoint

A) Both should be followed
B) The judgment of reason is preferred
C) The judgment of prophets is preferred
D) The conflict is impossible

Key Takeaway
The third proof establishes that reason is the root and prophethood is the branch, so reason's judgment must be decisive in all cases, provided rational good and bad are admitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever encountered a religious teaching that seemed to contradict your reason? How does this proof challenge you to resolve that tension in a way that honors both reason and revelation?
?
If reason is sufficient for knowing God and His judgments, why do you still need prophethood? What does that necessity reveal about your own intellectual and spiritual dependence on both?
?
The author says that when the branch opposes the root, the root must be preferred. In your life, are there areas where you allow tradition, habit, or authority to override your rational conviction about God? What step will you take today to align your practice with reason's verdict?

The Eight Conditions for Vision and the Ash'ari Position
Razi enumerates the eight conditions for vision and compares sectarian views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight conditions necessary for vision according to rationalist theologians and how Ash'ari breaks this causal link.

Core Idea
For Ash'ari, God is not bound by the natural conditions of vision.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a classic debate in Islamic theology: are the physical conditions for vision sufficient to cause sight? The philosophers and Mu'tazila say yes; Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari says no, because God's will is the sole cause of all events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow. Notice the enumeration of eight conditions and the contrasting theological views.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where Razi contrasts the deterministic view of the philosophers and Mu'tazila with Ash'ari's occasionalism.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on Ash'ari's examples: mountains we don't see, a blind person seeing a gnat. What does this say about causality?
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this fits into Ash'ari's occasionalism — God directly creates all events, and natural causes are not binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون الإبصار واجباً . وتلك الشرائط الثمانية هي هذه : أحدها: أن تكون الحدقة سليمة عن الآفات والعيوب وثانيها : أن يكون الشيء بحيث يصح إبصاره . وثالثها : أن لا يكون المرئي في غاية القرب من الحدقة ورابعها : أن لا يكون في غاية البعد . وخامسها : أن لا يكون في غاية اللطافة وسادسها : عدم السائر والحاجب وسابعها : أن لا يكون في غاية الصغر . وثامنها : " أن يكون مقابلا للرائي ، أو في حكم المقابل له فعند حصول هذه الشرائط ، أطبقت الفلاسفة والمعتزلة على أن الابصار يكون واجباً . وأما عند اختلالها [ أو اختلال (۱) ] بعضها [ فإن الإبصار (۲) ] يكون ممتنعاً . وأما أبو الحسن الأشعري فمذهبه : أن عند حصول هذه الأشياء يجوز أن لا يحصل الإبصار ، وعند عدمها يجوز أن يحصل . فعلى هذا لا يمتنع أن يحضر عندنا جبال شاهقة ، وأصوات عالية ، ونحن لا نبصرها ، ولا نسمعها ، ولا يمتنع أيضاً أن يبصر الأعمى الذي يكون بالمشرق : بقة (۳) بالمغرب . فهذا مذهبه :
Vision is necessary. And those eight conditions are these: First: that the pupil be free from defects and imperfections. Second: that the thing be such that it is possible to see it. Third: that the visible object not be extremely close to the pupil. Fourth: that it not be extremely far. Fifth: that it not be extremely subtle. Sixth: absence of an obstruction and a barrier. Seventh: that it not be extremely small. Eighth: that it be facing the viewer, or in the ruling of being facing. When these conditions are present, the philosophers and the Mu'tazila agreed that vision is necessary. And when they are disrupted, or some of them, vision becomes impossible. As for Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, his doctrine is that when these things are present, it is possible that vision does not occur, and when they are absent, it is possible that it occurs. Thus, it is not impossible that immense mountains and loud sounds be present before us and we do not see them nor hear them, and it is also not impossible that a blind person in the east see a gnat in the west. This is his doctrine.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage enumerates the eight conditions for vision according to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, who hold that when these conditions are fully met, vision must occur, and when they are lacking, vision is impossible. The author then contrasts this with the doctrine of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who maintains that even when these conditions are present, vision may not occur, and conversely, vision may occur even in their absence. This is because for Ash'ari, all causal relationships are ultimately contingent upon God's direct creation. The author illustrates this with two striking examples: it is possible for us to fail to see mountains or hear loud sounds, and possible for a blind person in the east to see a gnat in the west, if God so wills.

Key Terms
شرائط
إبصار
فلاسفة
معتزلة
أبو الحسن الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'ari denies the empirical reality of the conditions for vision.
Correction: Ash'ari affirms that the conditions can be present, but he denies that they necessitate vision. God is not bound by them.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers and Mu'tazila, when is vision necessary?
A) When all eight conditions are present
B) When most conditions are present
C) Only when God wills
D) Never

Key Takeaway
For Ash'ari, the eight conditions do not necessitate vision; God's will alone determines all perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God can override every natural cause in your life, or do you secretly rely on the 'conditions' being right before you trust in His power? What would it look like to live as if God could make you see a gnat in the west while blind—what impossibility are you currently denying Him?
?
When you face a 'mountain' before you—an obstacle or ambition—do you see only the material conditions (distance, resources, ability), or do you remember that God can remove your blindness to it or cause you to see what is impossible? How would you act differently if you really believed that God is not bound by your conditions?
?
If a blind person can see a gnat at a distance by God's will, what does that mean for the things you consider impossible in your own life—healing a broken relationship, providing for a need, changing a hardened heart? Are you limiting God by the very conditions you think are necessary, and what will you do today to break that limitation?

The Deniers' First Proof: Intuitive Knowledge vs. Miracles
Deniers argue miracles contradict intuitive knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the deniers' argument that miracles are impossible because they would require theoretical reasoning to overturn intuitive certainty.

Core Idea
Miracles are impossible because they would contradict necessary intuitive knowledge.

This passage presents the first argument of those who deny the possibility of miracles (al-mani‘un). The deniers claim that all knowledge is either intuitive (badihiyya) or acquired (kashiyya), and that acquired knowledge cannot contradict intuitive knowledge because it is derived from it. Miracles, if they required overturning an intuitive certainty, would therefore be impossible. Let's explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of knowledge
The deniers begin by dividing knowledge into two types: intuitive and acquired. Note that acquired knowledge depends on intuitive knowledge.
This classification is fundamental to the argument.
2
Define intuitive knowledge
They define intuitive knowledge as certain knowledge that arises in the soul without prior inference and cannot be doubted. This sets the standard for what cannot be contradicted.
The example of the young man will illustrate this.
3
Apply the argument to a specific case
Consider a young man. We intuitively know he must have gone through earlier stages (fetus, infant, child). If someone claims he appeared instantly, our immediate certainty of the impossibility shows this is intuitive. Therefore, to allow such a miracle would require theoretical reasoning to overturn intuition, which is invalid.
This leads to the conclusion that miracles are false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المانعون من انخراق العادات بوجهين : الأول : أن قالوا : العلوم قسمان : بديهية وكسبية ، والكسبيات مفرعة على البديهيات ، وإذا كان كذلك كان كل كسبي يوجب القدح في البديهي ، كان كالفرع القادح في الأصل ، وكل ما كان كذلك كان فاسداً فعلمنا : أن العلوم الكسبية يمتنع كونها قادحة في العلوم البديهية ، ثم لما أردنا أن نبحث عن حقيقة العلوم البديهية ، لم نجد لها معنى إلا العلم الحاصل في النفس ابتداء ، على سبيل الجزم ، من غير أن يقدر الإنسان على تشكيك نفسه فيه ، وكل ما كان كذلك كان علماً بديهياً . إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول : إنا إذا رأينا إنساناً شاباً قطعنا بأنه كان جنينا في رحم أمه ، ثم بعد الانفصال من رحم أمه ، كان طفلاً ، ثم صار شاباً . ولو أن قائلاً قال : إنه ما كان كذلك ، بل إنه حدث الآن شاباً ، من غير هذه المقدمات والوابق قطعنا : بأنه كاذب في هذا القول ، وجزمنا بأن الذي يقوله : باطل وبهتان . فلما كان هذا الجزم حاصلاً ابتداء ، من غير أن يستفاد ذلك الجزم من دليل متقدم ، ومن قياس سابق ، علمنا : أنه جزم بديهي ، وعلم أولي ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو قلنا : إن حدوث إنسان شاب ، ابتداء من غير تلك المقدمات والسوابق ممكن لزم أن يحصل مع هذا التجويز ذلك القطع والجزم . لكنا بينا أن ذلك القطع والجزم بديهي فثبت أن الحكم بهذا التجويز حكم نظري ، يوجب القدح في البديهي . وقد بينا : أن كل ما كان كذلك ، فإنه باطل ، فوجب أن يكون الحكم بهذا التجويز باطلا . فيثبت بهذا الطريق : أن القول بانخراق العادات عن مجاريها : قول باطل .
And those who prevent the breach of customs argued with two aspects: The first: They said: Knowledge is of two divisions: intuitive (badihiyya) and acquired (kashiyya). Acquired sciences are derived from intuitive sciences. If so, then every acquired [science] that leads to impugning the intuitive would be like a branch impugning the root, and everything like that is invalid. So we know that acquired sciences cannot impugn intuitive sciences. Then when we wanted to investigate the reality of intuitive sciences, we found no meaning for them except knowledge that occurs in the soul initially, with certainty, without a person being able to doubt himself about it. And everything like that is intuitive knowledge. If you know this premise, we say: When we see a young man, we are certain that he was a fetus in his mother's womb, then after separation from his mother's womb, he was a child, then he became a young man. If someone said that he was not like that, but rather he appeared now as a young man without these prior stages, we would be certain that he is lying in this statement, and we are certain that what he says is false and slander. Since this certainty arises initially, without being derived from a prior proof or previous syllogism, we know that it is intuitive certainty and primary knowledge. If this is established, we say: If we say that the occurrence of a young man initially without those prior stages is possible, then that certainty would have to exist along with this permission. But we have shown that that certainty is intuitive. So it is established that the judgment of this permission is a theoretical judgment that necessitates impugning the intuitive. And we have shown that everything like that is invalid. So the judgment of this permission must be invalid. Thus it is established by this way that the statement about the breach of customs from their courses is a false statement.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of those who deny miracles (al-mani‘un). It is a logical argument based on the classification of knowledge. The deniers begin by dividing knowledge into two types: badihiyya (intuitive, self-evident) and kashiyya (acquired, rational). Acquired knowledge is derived from intuitive knowledge, so it cannot contradict it. If an acquired belief contradicts an intuitive certainty, it is invalid because it would be like a branch attacking its root. They then define intuitive knowledge: it is knowledge that arises in the soul initially with full certainty, and one cannot doubt it. For example, when we see a young man, we know intuitively that he must have gone through stages of fetus, infant, child, etc. If someone claims he appeared instantly as a young adult, we know with immediate certainty that this is false. This certainty is not based on inference; it is direct and intuitive. Therefore, to claim that such a miracle (instant creation of a young man) is possible would require us to accept a theoretical judgment that contradicts this intuitive certainty. Since acquired knowledge cannot impugn intuitive knowledge, the judgment permitting such a miracle is invalid. Hence, the belief in miracles (breaches of custom) is false according to this argument.

كسبية
جزم
قدح
نظري
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that God cannot perform any miracles.
Correction: The argument only targets miracles that contradict intuitive certainties about natural causation (like instant generation of a young man). It does not necessarily negate all miracles, but the deniers intend to deny all breaches of custom.

Checkpoint
According to the deniers, why is it invalid to consider a miracle like a young man appearing instantly?
A) Because it violates the laws of physics
B) Because it contradicts intuitive certainty about human development
C) Because the philosophers say so
D) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it

Key Takeaway
The deniers' first proof argues that miracles are impossible because they would require theoretical reasoning to overturn necessary intuitive certainties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers argue that intuitive certainties cannot be overturned by theoretical reasoning. Is there any belief about Allah or His creation that you hold with such intuitive certainty that you would reject a miracle that contradicts it? Would your conviction withstand even if the miracle were clearly from Allah?
?
When you read that the deniers are certain that a young man must have been a fetus and child, do you share that same certainty? Or have you ever allowed philosophical doubts to shake even your most basic convictions about causality? What would it take for you to doubt the development of a young man?
?
The deniers' argument makes miracles epistemically impossible. But if Allah is the Creator of both intuitive knowledge and the world, could He not create a situation where intuitive knowledge itself is different? Reflect: Do you trust your own reason more than Allah's power? Would you be willing to submit to a miracle that your mind says is impossible?

Axiomatic Knowledge Cannot Be Invalidated by Acquired Sciences
The author defines axiomatic knowledge and proves that acquired sciences cannot invalidate it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of axiomatic knowledge and why acquired sciences cannot contradict it.

Core Idea
Axiomatic knowledge is self-evident; no theory can overturn it.

Before delving into complex theological debates, we must establish what counts as undeniable knowledge. What if someone claimed that our most basic certainties could be overturned by theoretical reasoning? This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defends the inviolability of axiomatic truths, using a vivid example to show that even the possibility of contradicting them leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's initial claim
Read the first part of the Arabic passage: 'البديهي؛ كان كالفرع القادح في الأصل...' and its translation. Notice how the author sets up the principle that a theoretical objection cannot invalidate an axiomatic truth.
This establishes the overall thesis.
2
Understand the definition of axiomatic knowledge
Read the definition: 'ما هو العلم الحاصل في النفس ابتداء، على سبيل الجزم...' Understand that axiomatic knowledge is a certainty that arises without proof and cannot be doubted by the person.
This definition is crucial for the argument.
3
Work through the example of the young man
Consider the example given: when we see a young man, we know with certainty he passed through fetal and childhood stages. If someone claims he appeared suddenly, we judge it false. Ask yourself: Is this certainty based on a proof or is it immediate?
The example illustrates a case of axiomatic knowledge.
4
Follow the logical argument against possibility
Trace the reasoning: if we deem the sudden appearance possible, that judgment contradicts the axiomatic certainty. But since axiomatic knowledge is more fundamental, such possibility must be false. Reflect on how this refutes the idea of arbitrary violations of natural laws.
This argument shows why habit-breaking events cannot be casually accepted without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البديهي؛ كان كالفرع القادح في الأصل، وكل ما كان كذلك كان فاسداً فعلمنا: أن العلوم الكسبية يمتنع كونها قادحة في العلوم البديهية، ثم لما أردنا أن نبحث عن حقيقة العلوم البديهية، لم نجد لها معنى إلا العلم الحاصل في النفس ابتداء، على سبيل الجزم، من غير أن يقدر الإنسان على تشكيك نفسه فيه، وكل ما كان كذلك كان علماً بديهياً. إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول: إنا إذا رأينا إنساناً شاباً قطعنا بأنه كان جنينا في رحم أمه، ثم بعد الانفصال من رحم أمه، كان طفلاً، ثم صار شاباً. ولو أن قائلاً قال: إنه ما كان كذلك، بل إنه حدث الآن شاباً، من غير هذه المقدمات والوابق قطعنا: بأنه كاذب في هذا القول، وجزمنا بأن الذي يقوله: باطل وبهتان. فلما كان هذا الجزم حاصلاً ابتداء، من غير أن يستفاد ذلك الجزم من دليل متقدم، ومن قياس سابق، علمنا: أنه جزم بديهي، وعلم أولي، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو قلنا: إن حدوث إنسان شاب، ابتداء من غير تلك المقدمات والسوابق ممكن لزم أن يحصل مع هذا التجويز ذلك القطع والجزم. لكنا بينا أن ذلك القطع والجزم بديهي فثبت أن الحكم بهذا التجويز حكم نظري، يوجب القدح في البديهي. وقد بينا: أن كل ما كان كذلك، فإنه باطل، فوجب أن يكون الحكم بهذا التجويز باطلا. فيثبت بهذا الطريق: أن القول بانخراق العادات عن مجاريها: قول باطل.
The axiomatic: it is like a branch invalidating the root, and everything like that is corrupt. So we know: that acquired sciences cannot be invalidators of axiomatic sciences. Then when we wanted to investigate the reality of axiomatic sciences, we did not find any meaning for it except knowledge occurring in the soul at the outset, with certainty, without a person being able to cause doubt in himself about it, and everything like that is axiomatic knowledge. If you understand this premise, we say: When we see a young man, we are certain that he was a fetus in his mother's womb, then after separation from his mother's womb, he was a child, then became a youth. And if someone said: He was not like that, rather he came into being now as a youth, without these prior stages, we would be certain: that he is lying in this statement, and we are convinced that what he says is false and slander. And we have shown: that everything like that is false, so it must be that the judgment of this deeming possible is false. So by this way it is established: that the statement of breaking habits from their courses is a false statement.

Commentary
This passage defines axiomatic knowledge and proves that acquired (theoretical) sciences cannot contradict it. The author begins by stating that if a branch (acquired science) invalidates the root (axiomatic science), it is corrupt. So we know that acquired sciences cannot invalidate axiomatic ones. Then he defines axiomatic knowledge: knowledge occurring in the soul at the outset, with certainty, such that a person cannot doubt it. He gives the example of a young man: we are certain that he was a fetus, then a child, then a youth. If someone claims he appeared suddenly as a youth without prior stages, we know he is lying, and this certainty is axiomatic, not from a proof. Then he argues: if we deem the sudden youth possible, that judgment would contradict the axiomatic certainty, but theoretical judgments cannot invalidate axiomatic ones, so that possibility is false. Thus, the claim that habits can be broken (miracles contrary to the natural order) is false.

Free Agent Acts Without Purpose
Concluding argument on free agency and purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of the world's temporal origination requires affirming that the free agent (God) acts without any purpose or preferrer.

Core Idea
For the proof of creation to work, God must act without identifiable purpose.

This passage presents a crucial logical consequence of the argument for the world's temporal origination (huduth al-alam). After discussing the ideas of benefit and postponement, the author concludes that the proof stands only if we accept that the divine free agent acts without any purpose or reason. This may seem surprising, but it prevents an infinite regress and secures the argument. Here we'll see how this conclusion is reached and what it implies for miracles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author has already discussed two divisions (benefit and postponement) and shown they are invalid. Read the passage to see how this leads to the conclusion.
2
Identify the key phrase
Find the statement: 'the free agent does the act without any purpose and without any preferrer.' Note that this is presented as necessary for the proof of huduth.
The Arabic phrase is: إن الفاعل المختار يفعل الفعل لا لغرض ولا المرجح أصلا
3
Grasp the implication
Once the free agent acts without purpose, we cannot be certain about the purpose of miracles (e.g., authentication).
4
Reflect on the logical chain
Without purpose, the act can be at any time, supporting temporal origination. This prevents an infinite regress of purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصلحة، عاد الكلام في تخصيص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض . ويلزم التسلل، وهو باطل . ولما بطل هذان القسمان، ثبت : أن القول بحدوث العالم، لا يتم لنا إلا إذا قلنا : إن الفاعل المختار يفعل الفعل لا لغرض ولا المرجح أصلا . وإذا ثبت هذا، فحينئذ يمتنع القطع بأنه تعالى إنما خلق هذا المعجز، في هذا الوقت لغرض التصديق .
Benefit: the discourse returns to specifying that time for that purpose. And postponement follows, which is invalid. When these two divisions are invalidated, it is established that the statement of the world's temporal origination is only complete for us if we say: the free agent does the act without any purpose and without any preferrer at all. And when this is established, then it is impossible to be certain that He, exalted, only created this miracle at this time for the purpose of authentication.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument. The author states that after discussing benefit (maslaha) and postponement (tasalsul), two divisions have been proven false. From this, it is established that the claim of the world's temporal origination (huduth al-‘alam) is only valid if we affirm that the free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār) acts without any purpose (gharaḍ) and without any preferrer (murjiḥ). Once this is established, it becomes impossible to be certain that God only created this miracle at this specific time for the purpose of authentication (taṣdīq). In other words, the logical consequence of the proof for huduth is that the divine actor is not motivated by purposes that we can identify, so we cannot claim definite knowledge about the purpose behind a miracle.

Key Terms

الغرض
المرجح
التسلل
حدوث العالم
المعجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying purpose in God's actions makes Him arbitrary.
Correction: The denial of purpose here is limited to the context of the proof of temporal origination; it does not negate divine wisdom but prevents an infinite regress in causal explanation.

Checkpoint

A) That he acts for a specific purpose
B) That he acts without any purpose or preferrer
C) That he acts only with a preferrer
D) That he acts with both purpose and preferrer

Key Takeaway
The proof of the world's temporal origination requires that the divine free agent act without any purpose or preferrer, which in turn prevents us from claiming definite knowledge about the purpose of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to hold that purpose with a measure of uncertainty, trusting God's wisdom even when you cannot assign a specific reason to His actions?
?
Think of a trial in your life where you have confidently said, 'I know why God allowed this.' According to this lesson, can you be certain of that purpose? What would change in your heart if you admitted that you might not know God's reason, yet still submit to His action?
?
This argument says that for the proof of creation to hold, we must stop seeking a 'why' behind God's act of creation. Where in your spiritual life are you still demanding a 'why' from God? What would it mean to let go of that demand and simply accept that He acts without need of justification?
