The Objection from Concomitance and Its Summary Refutation
Objection and summary refutation of the causality-from-concomitance argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere concomitance does not establish causality and how the author uses examples from God's knowledge, equality of cause and effect, and common factor to undermine this objection.

Core Idea
Concomitance alone cannot prove causality; causal connection requires more.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the miracle argument: the fact that a miracle occurs at the same time as a prophetic claim does not prove that the miracle affirms the claim, because mere concomitance (dawaran) does not imply causality. Then he begins a refutation by outlining three arguments from a summary perspective. These arguments will show that even perfect concomitance can exist without any causal relationship, using examples from theology and natural phenomena. Let's examine each argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated: the occurrence of an act with a request does not indicate causality. Notice the claim that concomitance does not prove causality even apparently.
2
Examine the first argument (God's knowledge)
The author uses the example of God's knowledge: it is concurrent with all known things, but neither causes the other. This shows that perfect concomitance can exist without causality.
Think about why this refutes the objection: if God's knowledge and the known are concomitant but not cause and effect, then the miracle and the claim could also be concomitant without causation.
3
Examine the second argument (equality and generality)
The author considers two cases: cause and effect equal in scope, or effect more general. If equal, they are concomitant but causality is not reciprocal. If effect is more general, the claimed cause is just one possible cause. Thus, mere concomitance does not isolate a specific cause.
Example: sunrise and daytime are equal and concomitant, but daytime is not the cause of sunrise. Fever can have multiple causes, so a single concomitant factor is not necessarily the cause.
4
Examine the third argument (common factor)
When two things are concomitant, their essence and properties must be examined. Concomitance is a common factor between true causality and mere coincidence, so it cannot specifically indicate causality.
This argument emphasizes that additional analysis is needed to identify causal relationships beyond mere correlation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجهين :
الأول : إن حصول ذلك الفعل لا يدل على التصديق . وبيانه : أن القدر المعلوم هو أنه حصل ذلك الفعل ، مقارناً لذلك الطلب ، إلا أن حصول الشيء مع الشيء ، لا يدل على العلية ، لا قطعاً ولا ظاهراً .
ونحن نبين هذا القول تارة بطريق الإجمال، وتارة بطريق التفصيل .
أما طريق الإجمال ، فمن وجوه :
الأول : إن علم الله تعالى بالشيء المعين ، واجب الحصول مع حصول ذلك الشيء المعين ، لأنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى يجب كونه عالماً بجميع المعلومات ، فعلى أي وجه يقع ذلك المعلوم ، يكون العلم متعلقاً به ، كما هو عليه في نفسه . ثم إن العلم يمتنع أن يكون علة للمعلوم وبالعكس . أما أن العلم يمتنع أن يكون علة للمعلوم ، فلأن العلم بالشيء ، تابع لذلك المعلوم ، فلو كان المعلوم معللاً به ، لزم كون المعلوم تابعاً للعلم . وهو دور ، والدور محال . وأما أن المعلوم يمتنع أن يكون علة للعلم ، فهو ظاهر . فقد ثبت أن الدوران حاصل قطعاً مع علم الله تعالى بجميع المعلومات ، مع أنه يمتنع أن يكون واحد منهما علة للآخر .
الثاني : إن المغلول قد يكون مساوياً للعلة ، وقد يكون أعم منها . أما الأول فمثل قولنا : طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار ، ووجود النهار أيضاً لا يحصل إلا عند طلوع الشمس . فههنا العلة والمعلول متساويان . وأما الثاني . فمثل الحمى . فإنه قد يكون لعفونة الخلط ، وقد تكون لسبب آخر . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : العلة والمعلول إذا كانا متساويين في العموم والخصوص ، فكل واحد منهما دائر مع الآخر وجوداً ، وعدماً . والعلية والمعلولية غير مشتركة من الجانبين . فثبت أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية .
الثالث : إن الشيء الذي يدور مع غيره وجوداً وعدماً ، فإنه لا بد وأن فصله المقوم لماهيته، ومع خاصيته المساوية لماهيته ، فالدوران قدر مشترك بين العلة وبين غيرها ، فيمتنع كونه دليلاً على العلية بعينها .
And the objection against it is from two aspects: The first: That the occurrence of that act does not indicate affirmation. And its explanation: That the known extent is that that act occurred concurrent with that request, but the occurrence of a thing with a thing does not indicate causality, neither definitively nor apparently. And we will explain this statement sometimes by the summary method and sometimes by the detailed method. As for the summary method, it is from several aspects: First: Indeed, the knowledge of God Most High of a particular thing is necessarily concurrent with the occurrence of that particular thing, because when it is established that He Most High must be knowing of all known things, then in whatever manner that known thing occurs, knowledge is related to it as it is in itself. Then knowledge cannot be a cause for the known, and vice versa. As for knowledge cannot be a cause for the known, because knowledge of a thing is consequent upon that known, so if the known were caused by it, the known would be consequent upon knowledge, which is a circle, and a circle is impossible. And as for the known cannot be a cause for knowledge, it is obvious. Thus it is established that concomitance occurs definitively with God's knowledge of all known things, even though neither can be a cause for the other. Second: Indeed, the effect may be equal to the cause or more general than it. As for the first, like our saying: The rising of the sun is the cause for the existence of daytime, and the existence of daytime also does not occur except at the rising of the sun. Here the cause and effect are equal. As for the second, like fever: it may be due to putrefaction of the humor or due to another cause. And when this is established, we say: The cause and effect, if they are equal in generality and specificity, then each of them is concomitant with the other in existence and non-existence. And causality and effect-hood are not shared from both sides. So it is established that concomitance does not indicate causality. Third: Indeed, the thing that is concomitant with another in existence and non-existence, its constitutive differentia and its property equal to its essence must be [considered], so concomitance is a common factor between the cause and other than it, so it is impossible for it to be a specific indication of causality.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first presents an objection: the fact that a miracle occurs at the time of a claim does not prove that the miracle is an affirmation of the claim, because mere concomitance (dawaran) does not prove causality. The author then begins a refutation, first via the summary method (tariq al-ijmal), with three arguments. The first argument uses the example of God's knowledge: God's knowledge of a thing necessarily accompanies that thing, but knowledge is not a cause of the thing nor the thing a cause of knowledge, even though they are concomitant. So concomitance does not entail causality. The second argument considers two cases: cause and effect may be equal in scope (like sunrise and daytime) or the effect may be more general (like fever which can have multiple causes). If they are equal, they are concomitant but causality is not reciprocal (the effect is not a cause of the cause). If the effect is more general, then the claimed cause is just one possible cause, so the miracle could be due to something else. Thus, concomitance does not prove causality. The third argument states that if two things are concomitant, their essence and properties must be examined; concomitance is a common factor between true causality and mere coincidence, so it cannot specifically indicate causality. These three arguments undermine the objection's assumption that the miracle's concurrence with the claim proves its causal relationship (affirmation).

Key Terms
الاعتراض
الدوران
العلية
العلم
المعلول
العلة
الإجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things always occur together, one must cause the other.
Correction: The text shows that perfect concomitance can exist without causality, as in the case of God's knowledge and the known, or in the example of sunrise and daytime.

Checkpoint
In the first summary argument, why does the example of God's knowledge prove that concomitance does not indicate causality?
A) Because knowledge and the known are never concomitant
B) Because they are concomitant but neither causes the other
C) Because knowledge causes the known but not vice versa
D) Because God's knowledge is eternal

Key Takeaway
Concomitance (dawaran) does not entail causality (al-`illiyya); additional logical or empirical evidence is required to establish a causal relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed that because two events happened together, one caused the other. How might this lesson correct that assumption, and what would it mean for you to apply this logical caution in your daily judgments about people, circumstances, or even your own life?
?
The passage uses God’s eternal knowledge to show that something can be perfectly concurrent without being a cause. How does this affect the way you understand God’s relationship to your actions? Are you willing to stop assuming that your actions 'cause' God’s response in a simple, mechanical way?
?
If mere concomitance cannot prove causality, then what does that say about how you have interpreted certain events in your life as 'signs' from God? Will you now examine those events more critically, and how can this deepen—rather than weaken—your trust in God’s wisdom?

The Fourth Doubt: Weakness of Rational Judgment
The author criticizes the basis of the claim that God cannot affirm a liar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt in the debate about God affirming a liar, and how the author critiques its foundation on rational judgment of good and evil.

Core Idea
The opponents' argument is only as strong as its weak foundation in rational ethics.

This passage presents the fourth doubt in the series, targeting the claim that God cannot affirm a liar. The author identifies the core of that argument—the ugliness of lying—and then challenges its basis in rational judgment of good and evil. He dismisses this basis as weak persuasive speech, thereby undermining the entire objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that this is the fourth doubt, and the author is critiquing an argument.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the opponents' core claim
Find the rule that lying is ugly, which leads to the conclusion that God cannot affirm a liar. Understand that this is the foundation the author attacks.
The opponents rely on moral reasoning.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author says this rule is based on the doctrine of rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ), which he dismisses as weak persuasive speech. Recognize that he is attacking the epistemology, not the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why attacking the foundation is a powerful move in theological debate. The conclusion may still be true, but the argument for it is weak.
This is a meta-critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : مدار كلام القائلين بأن تصديق الكاذب محال على الله . على أن الكذب قبيح [ وهو من الله محال (۱) ] إلا أنا بينا : أن هذه القاعدة مبنية على القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه . وقد عرفت أنه كلام إقناعي ضعيف جداً. فكان المبني عليه أيضاً ضعيفاً .
The fourth doubt: The core of the statement of those who say that affirming a liar is impossible for God is that lying is ugly [and it is impossible from God (1)], except that we have shown that this rule is based on the doctrine of rational judgment of good and evil. And you have known that it is a very weak persuasive speech. Therefore, what is built upon it is also weak.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt, which criticizes the foundation of the claim that God cannot affirm a liar. The author identifies the core of the opponents' argument: lying is ugly, and therefore impossible for God. He then points out that this rule is based on the doctrine of rational judgment of good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqlī). The author asserts that this doctrine has already been shown to be a very weak persuasive argument. Consequently, any argument built upon it is also weak. This is a meta-critique: attacking the epistemological foundation of the opposing view.

Key Terms
تصديق الكاذب
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
كلام إقناعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the opponents' argument is weak, then their conclusion (that God cannot affirm a liar) is false.
Correction: The author only attacks the foundation, not the conclusion itself. The conclusion might still be true for other reasons.

Checkpoint
On what does the author say the opponents' argument is based?
A) Divine revelation
B) Rational judgment of good and evil
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Empirical observation

Key Takeaway
The fourth doubt reveals that the opponents' claim relies on a weak foundation—rational judgment of good and evil—and thus their argument is not strong.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so certain of a belief that you built your entire argument on a single assumption, without checking whether that assumption was truly solid? How would you feel if that foundation turned out to be 'very weak persuasive speech'? What will you do to test your foundations today?
?
The author calls rational judgment of good and evil 'weak persuasive speech.' Is there a moral judgment you hold that you have never questioned whether it comes from reason or revelation? What would it mean for you if that judgment had no stronger basis than an argument you could talk someone into?
?
If the strength of your entire case for a truth depends on one pillar, and that pillar is weak, your case collapses. What is one truth you believe about God that you base on a human argument rather than on God's own words? Are you willing to rebuild that belief on a stronger foundation?

Refutation of Lying's Impossibility from Rational Good/Evil
Author refutes claim that lying is impossible from God based on weak rationalist principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the claim that lying is impossible for God to be based on a weak rationalist principle.

Core Idea
Claims based on rational good/evil are weak persuasive speech, not firm proofs.

In this passage, the author directly addresses a common theological argument: that lying is inherently ugly, so it cannot be attributed to God. He immediately points out the flaw in this reasoning—it depends on the contested idea that human reason can independently determine moral value. This lesson unpacks his refutation and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and literal translation carefully. Notice the author's structure: he states the claim, then objects by identifying its foundation, then dismisses that foundation as weak.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms 'qabih', 'tahsin al-aql wa taqbih', and 'kalam iqna'i'. Understand what each means in this context.
3
Reflect on the refutation logic
Consider why the author dismisses the rational good/evil doctrine as merely persuasive. How does this affect the conclusion about lying?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن الكذب قبيح [ وهو من الله محال (۱) ] إلا أنا بينا : أن هذه القاعدة مبنية على القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه . وقد عرفت أنه كلام إقناعي ضعيف جداً. فكان المبني عليه أيضاً ضعيفاً .
That lying is ugly [and it is impossible from God (1)], except that we have shown: that this principle is based on the doctrine of the intellect's (عقل) making good and evil (تحسين وتقبيح). And you have known that it is a very weak persuasive speech. So what is built upon it is also weak.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author addresses the claim that lying is ugly and therefore impossible for God. He notes that this claim rests on the principle that the intellect can independently determine good and evil (tahsin al-aql wa taqbih). The author dismisses this principle as merely persuasive speech (kalam iqna'i), not demonstrative, and therefore weak. Consequently, any conclusion built on it—such as the impossibility of lying from God—is also weak. This guards against overreliance on rationalist ethics in theology.

Key Terms

تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
كلام إقناعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of lying from God is an established rational truth independent of any principle.
Correction: The author argues that this claim depends on the doctrine of rational good/evil, which he considers weak persuasive speech, so the claim itself is not firmly established.

Checkpoint

A) Divine revelation
B) The doctrine of rational good and evil (tahsin al-aql wa taqbih)
C) Consensus of the early Muslims
D) Logical necessity without any premise

Key Takeaway
The claim that lying is impossible for God because it is ugly relies on the weak doctrine of rational good and evil, which the author dismisses as merely persuasive speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Second Principle: Certainty Despite Possibility
Introduces the second of three principles for establishing prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second principle of establishing prophethood: that something can be rationally possible yet we can have definite certainty that it did not occur.

Core Idea
Possibility does not preclude certainty; some possible events are known not to have happened.

This lesson introduces the second of three foundational principles for proving prophethood. The author explains that even if something is logically possible, we can still be absolutely certain that it never occurred. A simple sensory example illustrates how certainty and possibility can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a general statement followed by the example of seeing Zayd.
2
Identify the principle
The principle states that something may be known as permissible and possible yet we have definite certainty that it did not exist. Focus on the terms جواز (permissibility), إمكان (possibility), جزم (certainty), and قطع (definitiveness).
3
Analyze the example
The example of seeing Zayd twice demonstrates that even though it is possible for two different people to look alike, we know with necessity that the second sighting is the same person. This shows how certainty can coexist with possibility.
4
Reflect on relevance
This principle is crucial for prophethood: miracles from false prophets are theoretically possible, but we can be certain they did not occur due to the context and evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني من الأصول التي عليها مدار إثبات النبوات : أنه لا أن يكون الشيء معلوم الجواز والإمكان، ومع ذلك فإنه يكون الجزم والقطع حاصلاً بأنه لم يوجد، ولم يحصل. وبيانه : أني إذا رأيت زيداً، ثم غمضت العين، ثم إني نظرت إليه في المرة الثانية، فإني أعلم بالضرورة أن هذا الذي أراه ثانياً، عين الذي رأيته.
The second principle of the principles upon which the establishment of prophethood revolves is that it is possible for a thing to be known as permissible and possible, yet there is definite certainty that it has not existed and has not occurred. Its explanation: When I see Zayd, then I close my eye, then I look at him a second time, I know by necessity that this which I see a second time is the same as the one I saw.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage introduces the second of three principles (al-aṣl al-thānī) necessary for establishing prophethood. It states that a thing may be known as possible in itself yet we have definite certainty that it has not happened. The example: If you see Zayd, close your eyes, then see him again, you know necessarily that the second sighting is the same person you saw initially. This illustrates that something possible (like Zayd's existence) is known with certainty to have occurred, and similarly, we can be certain that certain possible things (like miracles from false prophets) did not occur. The passage is cut off but sets the stage for the principle.

Key Terms
الأصل الثاني
الجواز
الإمكان
الجزم
القطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is logically possible, we cannot be certain whether it happened or not.
Correction: This principle shows that logical possibility does not prevent certainty. As in the example, we know with necessity that the second sighting is the same Zayd, even though it is possible that two different people could look identical.

Checkpoint
In the example, what does the person know by necessity when seeing Zayd the second time?
A) The person seen the second time is definitely different from the first.
B) The person seen the second time is the same as the first.
C) The person seen the second time might be the same or different.
D) The person seen the second time is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The second principle asserts that rational possibility does not prevent us from having definitive certainty about whether something occurred.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you doubted something you were once certain of because someone argued it was 'possible' it wasn't true. How does this principle strengthen your conviction about that belief? What will you do differently next time such doubt arises?

In your daily life, are there matters you treat as uncertain simply because they are not logically impossible? What would change if you applied this principle — accepting certainty despite mere possibility — to your relationship with Allah and His Messenger? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to act on that certainty.

Certainty Despite Possibility
The author provides an example of necessary knowledge of personal identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how necessary knowledge of personal identity is compatible with the theoretical possibility of replacement.

Core Idea
Certainty can coexist with possibility.

In this passage, the author presents a concrete example to show that we can be certain about something even though there is a theoretical possibility of its opposite. This sets the stage for his broader argument about the limitation of rational possibility in undermining certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author starts with 'then indeed, while we know of this possibility'.
The author refers to the possibility of annihilation and replacement discussed earlier.
2
Identify the example
The author uses the example of seeing a person at two different times. He asserts we know by necessity that it is the same person, despite the theoretical possibility that the original was replaced.
This example is meant to be intuitive and widely applicable.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: how does this example support the idea that certainty is not undermined by mere logical possibility? Why does the author think this is a powerful argument?
This connects to the broader rejection of skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا مع علمنا بهذا التجويز، نعلم بالضرورة أن هذا الشخص الذي أراه في هذه اللحظة هو عين الشخص الذي رأيته قبل ذلك بلحظة أو بيوم أو بشهر. واعلم أنك إذا عرفت حقيقة الحال في هذا المثال، أمكنك معرفة أمثلة خارجة عن الحد والإحصاء، موافقة لهذا المطلوب. وأما الأصل الثالث: فهو أن تعلم أن تحسين العقل وتقبيحه باطل، لا عبرة به، ولا التفات إليه، في أفعال الله تعالى، وفي أحكامه.
Then indeed, while we know of this possibility, we know by necessity that this person whom I see at this moment is exactly the person whom I saw before that by a moment or by a day or by a month. And know that if you know the reality of the situation in this example, you can know examples beyond limit and count, agreeing with this desired [conclusion]. And as for the third principle: it is that you know that the intellect's deeming good and evil are invalid, no regard is given to them, nor attention paid to them, in the actions of God Most High and in His judgments.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage makes two key points. First, despite the theoretical possibility that a person could be annihilated and replaced by an identical person in a subtle moment, we still have necessary knowledge that the person we see now is the same person we saw before. This illustrates that the possibility of alternatives does not necessarily undermine certainty. The author then states that this example can be generalized to many similar cases. Second, he introduces the third principle of his argument: that rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥuhu) has no validity in divine actions and rulings. This is a rejection of the Muʿtazilite position that good and evil are known by reason independently of revelation, asserting instead that God's actions are not subject to rational evaluation.

Key Terms
التجويز
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mere existence of a logical possibility necessarily undermines certainty.
Correction: The author shows that despite a logical possibility (of replacement), we still have necessary certainty about the identity of persons.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the possibility of replacement and our knowledge of personal identity?
A) The possibility of replacement completely destroys certainty.
B) Despite the possibility, we have necessary knowledge of identity.
C) The possibility is merely theoretical and never occurs.
D) Only divine knowledge can overcome such possibility.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge remains intact even when logical possibilities point to alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When has a theoretical doubt ever shaken your certainty in a matter of faith? What would it take for you to hold that faith certainty with the same force as your knowledge of your own identity?
?
If you truly internalized that certainty can coexist with logical possibility, how would that change the way you respond to intellectual doubts about Allah's existence? Are you willing to affirm your belief as certain even while acknowledging you can conceive of alternatives? What would it cost you to make that commitment today?
?
The author says this example can be generalized. Think of one specific Islamic conviction that feels fragile or uncertain to you. What would it mean to treat that conviction as necessary knowledge, despite the logical possibility of error? How would that shift your daily practice and emotional state?

Invalidity of Rational Good and Evil
The author states the third principle rejecting rational good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third principle of the author's argument: that rational judgments of good and evil do not apply to God's actions.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond rational moral evaluation.

The author now introduces the third foundational principle of his argument: the rejection of rational good and evil. This principle is crucial for defending the Ash'ari view that God's actions are not subject to human rational assessment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the strong wording: 'invalid, no regard is given to them, nor attention paid to them'.
This shows the author's definitive stance.
2
Identify the scope
The author specifies that this applies to 'the actions of God Most High and in His judgments.' This limits the denial to divine matters, not necessarily human moral reasoning in other contexts.
The author is likely responding to Mu'tazilite rational ethics.
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this principle matters for the overall argument. It means that we cannot use human reason to deem something good or evil in relation to God; instead, we rely on revelation.
This principle supports divine command theory.

Then indeed, while we know of this possibility, we know by necessity that this person whom I see at this moment is exactly the person whom I saw before that by a moment or by a day or by a month. And know that if you know the reality of the situation in this example, you can know examples beyond limit and count, agreeing with this desired [conclusion]. And as for the third principle: it is that you know that the intellect's deeming good and evil are invalid, no regard is given to them, nor attention paid to them, in the actions of God Most High and in His judgments.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage makes two key points. First, despite the theoretical possibility that a person could be annihilated and replaced by an identical person in a subtle moment, we still have necessary knowledge that the person we see now is the same person we saw before. This illustrates that the possibility of alternatives does not necessarily undermine certainty. The author then states that this example can be generalized to many similar cases. Second, he introduces the third principle of his argument: that rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥuhu) has no validity in divine actions and rulings. This is a rejection of the Muʿtazilite position that good and evil are known by reason independently of revelation, asserting instead that God's actions are not subject to rational evaluation.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect can independently determine moral good and evil even for God's actions.
Correction: The author asserts that rational deeming of good and evil is invalid in relation to God's actions and judgments.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the intellect's deeming of good and evil in relation to God's actions?
A) It is valid but secondary to revelation.
B) It is invalid and should be completely ignored.
C) It is valid only for human actions.
D) It is a useful but not decisive tool.

Key Takeaway
Rational moral judgments cannot be applied to God's actions; only divine revelation defines good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's actions are beyond rational moral evaluation, how does that confront your own tendency to question God's wisdom when you face suffering? Will you surrender that question to revelation, or will you cling to your intellect's judgment?
?
Consider an action you consider 'bad' that God commands or does. Can you submit to it without your intellect approving? If not, what does that reveal about your reliance on reason over divine command?
?
The text calls rational deeming of good and evil 'invalid' in God's actions. Does this challenge your comfort in using moral reasoning to judge the actions of others? How will you change your perspective so that your moral judgments are always subordinate to divine revelation?

The Third Principle: Denial of Rational Good and Evil in God's Actions
The author states the third principle: rational good and evil are invalid in God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī rejection of rational good and evil as applied to God's actions and rulings.

Core Idea
Human reason cannot set the standard for God's actions.

The third principle in this work tackles a foundational debate between Ashʿarīs and Muʿtazilīs: can human reason determine what is good or evil for God? The author states unequivocally that such rational judgments are invalid when it comes to divine actions. This lesson explains that claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms taḥsīn and taqbīḥ, and the assertion of bāṭil.
2
Identify the doctrinal target
Recognize that this statement rejects the Muʿtazilī position that reason can independently judge good and evil. The author insists such judgments are irrelevant for God.
3
Reflect on a common misunderstanding
Consider that rejecting rational good and evil does not reject all ethics; it only denies that human reason can obligate God or judge His actions.
This principle upholds divine sovereignty while maintaining morality within revealed commands.

Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage states the third principle (al-aṣl al-thālith) of the author's argument. It is a doctrinal definition: the idea that human reason (al-ʿaql) can independently determine that an action is good (taḥsīn) or bad (taqbīḥ) is declared false (bāṭil). The author asserts that such rational judgments carry no weight (lā ʿibra bihi) and are not to be heeded (lā iltifāta ilayhi) when it comes to God's actions (afʿāl Allāh) and His rulings (aḥkāmuhu). This is a foundational Ashʿarī position that rejects the Muʿtazilī doctrine of objective good and evil known by reason, affirming instead that God's will is not bound by human moral categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The third principle only denies that human reason can obligate God or judge His actions. Morality in the Ashʿarī framework is defined by divine commands, not independent rational standards.

Key Takeaway
The third principle asserts that rational assessments of good and evil have no bearing on God's actions or judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a time when you were angry at God for something that seemed unjust according to your reason. In light of this third principle, what do you need to repent from? What should you say to God instead?

First Principle: Visionary Images Are Not External
Sets out the first principle and defends it via objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first principle regarding visionary images and how the author defends it against the charge of leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Visionary images are not external; denying this leads to universal doubt and sophistry.

The first principle underlying the study of prophetic visions states that the images seen by prophets (and others) are not external objects. This seems counterintuitive, so the author immediately defends it by showing that the alternative leads to even greater absurdities. This short passage contains a clear objection-and-reply structure that is essential for understanding the Islamic stance on the reality of visionary experiences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation twice: first for overall meaning, then to identify the logical steps.
Note the objection is embedded in the middle; look for the transition from 'فإنه يقال لهم' or similar.
2
Identify the first principle and its proof
Find the statement of the principle (these images are not external) and the proof: if they were external, everyone would perceive them, which is not the case.
The proof uses a conditional: if they were external, then necessary perception; but perception doesn't occur, so they are not external.
3
Understand the objection and the reply
The objection claims that the author's view leads to the same absurdity (possible non-perception of real things). The reply shows that the opponent's view leads to a worse absurdity: universal doubt (sophistry).
Notice the author distinguishes between 'allowing non-perception of existents' (his view) and 'doubting existence of perceived things' (opponent's view).
4
Contrast the two positions
Compare the author's position (external realities are certain, but we may miss some) with the opponent's position (all perceived things could be imaginary).
The author's view avoids universal doubt and preserves certainty about ordinary perceptions.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence declares the opponent's view 'extremely corrupt'. Reflect on why the author considers that a decisive refutation.
The argument is based on the principle of avoiding greater harm (sophistry).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الأصل في جملة هذه التفاريع أصلان : الأصل الأول : أن يقال : هذه الصور التي يشاهدها الأنبياء والأولياء وغيرهم ليست موجودة في الخارج، لأنها لو كانت موجودة في الخارج ، لوجب أن يدركها كل من كان سليم الحس ، إذ لو جوزنا أن لا يحصل الإدراك مع حصول هذه الشرائط ، لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال ورعود ونحن لا نراها ولا نسمعها ، ذلك يوجب الجهالات [ فيقال لهم : هذه الجهالات ] التي ألزمتموها على هذا القول ، فهي على قولكم ألزم . وذلك لأنا لو جوزنا أن يرى الإنسان صوراً ، ويشاهدها ، ويتكلم معها ، ويسمع أصواتها ، ويرى أشكالها . ثم إنها لا تكون موجودة في الخارج ، جاز أيضاً في كل هذه الأشياء التي نراها ونسمعها من صور الناس والجبال والبحار وأصوات الرعود ، أن لا يكون لشيء منها وجود في الخارج بل يكون محض الخيال ومحض الصور المرتسمة في الحس المشترك . ومعلوم أن القول به محض السفسطة بل نقول : هذا في البعد عن الحق ، والخوض في الجهالة أشد من الأول . لأن على القول الذي نقول نحن به ، جازمون بأن كل ما رأيناه فهو موجود حق ، إلا أنه يلزمنا تجويز أن يكون قد حضر عندنا أشياء ، ونحن ما رأيناها ، وتجويز هذا لا يوجب الشك في وجود كل ما رأيناه وسمعناه ، أما على القول الذي يقولونه فإنه يلزم وقوع الشك في وجود كل صورة رأيناها ، وكل صوت سمعناه ، وذلك هو الجهالة التامة ، والسفسطة الكاملة . فثبت أن القول الذي اخترتموه في غاية الفساد.
Know that the principle underlying all these branches is two principles: The first principle is to say: These images that the prophets, saints, and others see are not existent externally, because if they were existent externally, then it would be necessary that every person with sound sense perceive them. For if we allowed that perception does not occur despite the presence of these conditions, then it would be possible that there are mountains and thunderclaps in our presence while we do not see them or hear them; that leads to absurdities. [So it is said to them: These absurdities] that you have objected upon this statement are more binding upon your statement. Because if we allowed that a person sees images, observes them, speaks with them, hears their voices, and sees their forms, and then they are not existent externally, then it would also be possible in all these things that we see and hear, like images of people, mountains, seas, and sounds of thunder, that none of them have existence externally, but rather be pure imagination and pure images imprinted in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). It is well known that holding this is pure sophistry. Rather we say: This is further from truth and deeper into ignorance than the first. Because according to the statement we hold, we are certain that everything we have seen is truly existent, except that it is necessary for us to allow that things may have been present before us without our seeing them. Allowing this does not necessitate doubt about the existence of everything we have seen and heard. As for the statement they say, it necessitates falling into doubt about the existence of every image we have seen and every sound we have heard, and that is complete ignorance and perfect sophistry. So it is established that the statement you have chosen is extremely corrupt.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage sets out the first of two fundamental principles underlying the previous branches. The principle is stated: the images seen by prophets, saints, and others are not external existents. The proof is: if they were external, then every person with sound senses would perceive them, because if we allow that perception might not occur despite the presence of normal conditions, then it would be possible that mountains and thunder are right in front of us without our seeing or hearing them—this leads to absurdities. An objection is then raised: the same absurdities (i.e., the possibility of missing real objects) apply to your view. But the author replies that these absurdities are actually more binding upon the objector's view. For if we allow that a person can see images, interact with them, and yet they are not externally real, then it becomes possible that everything we normally perceive (people, mountains, seas, sounds) is also pure imagination and mere impressions in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). That is pure sophistry. The author then contrasts: on his view, we are certain that everything we see is truly existent, though we admit that things may be present without our perception—this does not lead to universal doubt. On the objector's view, however, doubt becomes necessary about every perceived image and sound, which is complete ignorance and sophistry. Therefore, the objector's position is extremely corrupt.

Key Terms
الصور
الخارج
الحس المشترك
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies the external reality of everyday objects.
Correction: The author affirms that everyday objects (people, mountains, etc.) are truly external; only the visionary images are not external. Denying this leads to sophistry, which the author explicitly rejects.

Checkpoint
According to the first principle, what conclusion follows from allowing that perceived images might not be external existents?
A) It leads to certainty that all images are real
B) It leads to possible doubt about all perceived things
C) It only applies to prophets
D) It is the view of the author

Key Takeaway
The first principle that visionary images are not external existents is defended by showing that the alternative leads to universal doubt and sophistry, while the author's view preserves certainty about ordinary perceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the phrase 'we are certain that everything we have seen is truly existent,' do you actually live with that certainty? Think of a time you doubted the reality of something you clearly perceived. What caused that doubt, and how does this lesson call you to trust your senses as a gift for certain knowledge?
?
The author warns that allowing vivid experiences to be non-external can lead to wiping out all certainty. In your own life, have you ever allowed a single doubt to snowball into questioning everything? What would it cost you to lose certainty about the reality of your daily experiences?
?
Is there an area of your life where you are acting like a sophist—accepting that what you see and hear might not be real? How can you apply the author's principle to protect the basic certainty that grounds your faith and action?

The First Difficulty: Why Invisibility Does Not Prove Non-Existence
Author argues that invisibility does not imply non-existence, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the invisibility of small celestial bodies does not prove their non-existence, and how the author refutes the objection that such bodies would have no effects.

Core Idea
Invisibility does not equal non-existence—small unseen planets may still exist and have effects.

The author begins by highlighting a fundamental challenge in astronomy: we cannot see everything in the heavens. Using induction, he shows that small objects at great distances are invisible, so our failure to see certain stars does not mean they don't exist. He then addresses a key objection about their lack of influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a formal argumentative style with 'if...then' reasoning.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has three parts: (1) induction on distance and sight, (2) application to fixed stars and Mercury, (3) conclusion. Then an objection and refutation. Trace each step.
3
Understand the refutation
The objection claims small planets would have no effect. The author refutes by citing Mercury (small but effective) and astrologers' imaginary points (no physical size but claimed effects). This shows effect is not solely due to size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أن الوقوف على أصول هذا العلم عسر جدا. اعلم أن صعوبة هذا العلم تظهر من وجوه ثلاثة: الأصل الأول: إنه يعسر علينا معرفة جميع النيرات الفلكية. وبيانه من الأول: إن الاستقراء يدل على أن رؤية الصغير من المسافة البعيدة ممتنعة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن أصغر كوكب من الكواكب الثابتة وهو الذي تمتحن برؤيته القوة الباصرة، مثل الأرض بضع عشرة مرة. فلو قدرنا أن هذه الكواكب حصلت في الفلك الأعظم، لصارت المسافة أعظم، فحينئذ كان يمتنع إبصاره لا محالة. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: إنه يقال: إن عطارد جزء من ثلاثين ألف جزء من كرة الأرض. فهو في غاية الصغر، فلو قدرنا حصول كواكب مساوية لعطارد في الجرم على الفلك الأعظم [أعني الجرم الأول] لكانت رؤيتها ممتنعة قطعاً. فثبت أن عدم إبصارنا لأمثال هذه الكواكب، لا بدل البتة على عدمها. فإن قالوا: لو حصلت هذه الكواكب الصغيرة، لم تكن لها قوة وتأثير أصلاً. لأجل أن صغرها يوجب ضعفها. فنقول: هذا باطل لأن عطارد مع غاية صغره، تقاربه سائر السيارات مع عظم أجرامها. بل نقول: الرأس والذنب نقطتان، وأصحاب الأحكام أثبتوا لهما آثاراً عظيمة بل نقول: السعادة، وسهم الغيب نقطتان وهميتان، والأحكاميون أثبتوا لهما آثاراً قوية. وأيضاً النقط التي تنتهي إليها تسييرات درجة الطالع فقط: وهمية، والقوم أثبتوا لها آثاراً قوية
The second chapter on showing that knowing the principles of this science is very difficult. Know that the difficulty of this science appears from three aspects: The first principle: It is difficult for us to know all the celestial luminaries. Its explanation from the first: Induction indicates that seeing a small object from a great distance is impossible. And when this is established, we say: The smallest of the fixed stars, which tests the power of sight by its visibility, is like the earth ten-odd times. So if we suppose that these stars were in the highest sphere, the distance would become greater, and then its sight would inevitably be impossible. And when this is established, we say: It is said that Mercury is one part in thirty thousand parts of the sphere of the earth. So it is extremely small. If we suppose that there are stars equal to Mercury in size on the highest sphere [meaning the first body], their sight would be absolutely impossible. So it is established that our not seeing such stars does not at all indicate their non-existence. If they say: If these small stars existed, they would have no power and effect at all, because their smallness necessitates their weakness. We say: This is false because Mercury, despite its extreme smallness, is near the rest of the planets despite the greatness of their bodies. Rather we say: The head and tail are two points, and the astrologers have attributed to them great effects. Rather we say: Fortune and the arrow of the unseen are two imaginary points, and the astrologers have attributed to them strong effects. Also, the points at which the projections of the degree of the ascendant end are imaginary, and the people have attributed to them strong effects.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the first difficulty in knowing all celestial bodies: the impossibility of seeing small planets from a great distance. The author begins by stating that knowing all celestial luminaries is difficult, and induction shows that small objects cannot be seen from afar. He then applies this to fixed stars: the smallest fixed star is many times larger than Earth, but if placed in the highest sphere, it would be invisible. Similarly, Mercury is tiny, and if there were planets of its size in the outermost sphere, they would be invisible. Therefore, our failure to see such planets does not prove they do not exist. An objection is raised: such small planets would have no effect, so their existence is pointless. The author refutes this by noting that Mercury, though small, has effects comparable to larger planets. Moreover, astrologers attribute effects to imaginary points (e.g., the head and tail of the dragon, fortune, arrow of the unseen, and projection points). Thus, physical effect does not depend on size alone, and the objection is invalid.

الكواكب الثابتة
عطارد
الرأس والذنب
السعادة
سهم الغيب
التسييرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we cannot see an object, it must not exist.
Correction: The author shows that many real things (like small distant stars) are invisible, and even imaginary points are considered to have effects in astrology.

Checkpoint

A) Small planets are visible if they exist.

Key Takeaway
Invisibility of small celestial bodies does not prove their non-existence, and physical effect does not depend on size alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Second Difficulty: The Unknown Number of Stars
Author shows that the galaxy proves the number of stars is unknowable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence from the galaxy that demonstrates the impossibility of knowing the total number of stars.

Core Idea
The galaxy’s cloudy appearance proves that counting all stars is impossible.

Having explored the first difficulty, the author now presents a second challenge based on observation. The Milky Way – that band of light across the night sky – is not a nebula but a dense cluster of countless small stars. This phenomenon, he argues, shows that complete knowledge of stellar numbers lies beyond human reach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author’s argument directly.
Notice the concise style and the logical connector 'وهو أنه ثبت'.
2
Identify the evidence and conclusion
The author uses the galaxy (المجرة) as evidence. Identify what it is (small stars in the fixed-star sphere) and what it implies (their indistinguishability leads to impossibility of counting).
The key is that they are 'لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض'.
3
Reflect on the argument's force
Ask yourself why the galaxy is a convincing proof. How does its appearance as a cloudy patch demonstrate that complete enumeration is impossible?
This connects to the overall theme: human limits in knowing creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: في بيان أن عدد النيرات الفلكية غير معلومة، وهو أنه ثبت بالدليل أن المجرة، ليست إلا أجراما كوكبية صغيرة الحجم، مرتكزة في فلك الثوابت، إلا أنها لصغرها لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض، فنشاهد جملتها على صورة لطخة سحابية، وهذا يدل على أن الوقوف التام على معرفة أعداد الكواكب: ممتنع الحصول.

This indicates that complete knowledge of the numbers of the stars is impossible to obtain.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the second difficulty: the unknown number of stars. The author points to the Milky Way (المجرة) as evidence. It is actually a vast collection of small stellar bodies located in the sphere of the fixed stars. Because they are small and densely packed, they appear as a single cloudy patch. This shows that we cannot individually count them, and thus a complete census of all stars is impossible. The argument is empirical: we see a phenomenon that implies countless individual stars, but we cannot distinguish them.

The Third Difficulty: Spots on the Moon and Sun
Author argues that spots on the moon and sun indicate hidden bodies, showing human knowledge is limited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how celestial phenomena like spots on the moon and sun reveal the limits of human knowledge regarding the heavens.

Core Idea
What we cannot see may still exist — human intellect is limited.

This lesson presents a concrete example from astronomy to illustrate a deeper epistemological point. The author uses observations of spots on the moon and a black point on the sun to show that there are celestial bodies we may never perceive. This serves as a reminder that our inability to detect something does not mean it is absent, and that human reason has boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the two examples: the moon's spots and the sun's black point.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the argument is complete.
2
Identify the two theories
The author reports two explanations: one for the moon's spots (small stars embedded) and one for the sun's black point (a permanent spot hidden by light). Understand each.
These are not the author's own claims but reported views.
3
Connect to the broader argument
The author concludes that these phenomena show human minds cannot fully know all celestial bodies. Reflect on how this supports the idea that unseen beings (like angels, jinn) may exist.
This ties back to the overall theme of the book about the limits of reason.
4
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: If we cannot directly observe some stars, does that mean they are not real? The passage says no. This is a key methodological lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن المحققين ذهبوا إلى أن المحو، المحسوس في وجه القمر، إنما حصل بسبب أن كواكب صغيرة ارتكزت في وجه القمر. وقال آخرون: إنه حصل في وجه الشمس فوق النقطة التي هي كالمركز لقرص الشمس: نقطة سوداء، كالخال في وجه الشمس. إلا أنها لا تظهر لأجل قوة نور الشمس. وقد تعرض عوارض مخصوصة. تصير تلك النقطة السوداء كالمشاهدة، وكل هذه الاعتبارات تدل على أن العقول البشرية، قاصرة عن الإحاطة بعدد نيرات الأفلاك
The third: The verifiers have held that the dimness (?) perceived on the face of the moon occurs because small stars have become fixed on the moon's face. And others said: On the face of the sun, above the point that is like the center of the sun's disk, there is a black point, like a mole on the sun's face. But it does not appear because of the strength of the sun's light. And particular conditions occur that make that black point observable. All these considerations indicate that human minds are incapable of encompassing the number of the luminaries of the spheres.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the third difficulty: the spots on the moon and sun. The author reports that investigators (المحققين) believe the marks on the moon (likely craters) are caused by small stars embedded in the moon's surface. Others claim there is a permanent black spot on the sun (like a sunspot) near the center, but it is usually hidden by the sun's intense light, only becoming visible under special conditions. These examples show that there are celestial bodies we cannot perceive normally. Thus, human intellects are limited and cannot fully enumerate all celestial luminaries. This reinforces the theme that our ignorance of many celestial objects does not mean they do not exist.

Correction: The passage shows that spots on the moon and a black point on the sun indicate hidden bodies that are not directly observable, demonstrating the limits of human knowledge.

What do the spots on the moon and the black point on the sun imply according to the author?
A) The moon and sun are imperfect.

Key Takeaway
Even phenomena we cannot directly observe can exist, demonstrating the limits of human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that human minds cannot fully know even the number of celestial bodies. What area of your life are you most certain about, yet might be missing something crucial? Will you pause today to reconsider an assumption you hold tightly?
?
The passage says there are hidden realities on the sun and moon that only sometimes become visible. What hidden spiritual realities—like your own faults or God's blessings—might you be ignoring because they aren't immediately obvious? Commit to one specific practice this week to uncover them.
?
If your intellect is truly limited, how does that change how you approach matters of faith, especially those you find hard to accept? Are you willing to submit to revelation even when your reason cannot fully grasp it? What is one step you can take to cultivate intellectual humility starting right now?

The Fourth Difficulty: The Astrologers' 'Al-Kabid'
Author uses astrologers' belief in 'al-kabid' to argue for possibility of other unseen celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the astrologers' belief in an unseen celestial body to argue for the possibility of other unknown celestial bodies, challenging the claim that only observed bodies exist.

Core Idea
If even astrologers can posit unseen bodies, then why not others?

In this passage, the author presents the fourth of several difficulties aimed at refuting the idea that we can know all celestial objects through observation alone. He points out that astrologers themselves believe in an unseen entity called 'al-kabid' and attribute influence to it. If such a belief is accepted, then logically, the existence of other unobserved celestial bodies cannot be denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its concise argument. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow sink in.
The passage is a single sentence with a clear conditional structure.
2
Identify the key players
Note who 'أصحاب الأحكام' (astrologers) are and what they claim: they have established something called 'al-kabid' and assigned it a portion of effect.
Astrologers are not authoritative in Islamic theology, but their practice is used as an example.
3
Understand the logical move
The author uses a fortiori reasoning: if even astrologers can accept an unobserved body, then it is even more reasonable to accept other unobserved bodies. This undermines the claim that we have observed all celestial bodies.
The argument does not endorse astrology; it uses it as a tool to show inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument weakens the position that only observed celestial bodies exist. It shows that the door to unseen bodies cannot be closed just because we haven't seen them.
This is part of a larger debate about the possibility of many unknown celestial bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إن أصحاب الأحكام أثبتوا شيئاً، يسمونه بالكبد، وأثبتوا حظا من التأثير له، وإذا جاز ثبوته، فلم لا يجوز ثبوت غيره ؟
The fourth: The astrologers have established something they call 'al-kabid' (the liver?), and they established a portion of effect for it. If its existence is permissible, then why is the existence of something else not permissible?
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth difficulty: the astrologers (أصحاب الأحكام) claim the existence of a celestial body they call 'al-kabid' (possibly a comet or an imaginary point) and attribute some influence to it. The author uses this as an argument: if astrologers can accept the existence of such an unobserved body, then logically it is permissible to accept the existence of other unobserved celestial bodies. This further undermines the idea that we can know all celestial bodies by direct observation. The argument is a fortiori: if even astrologers, who are not rigorous, can posit unseen bodies, then why should we deny other possible unseen bodies?

Key Terms
أصحاب الأحكام
الكبد
حظ
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is endorsing astrology as a valid source of knowledge.
Correction: The author is not endorsing astrology; he is using the astrologers' own belief as a logical argument to show that unseen bodies can be posited, thereby refuting the claim that only observed bodies exist.

Checkpoint
What is the author's argument in the fourth aspect?
A) Astrology is a reliable science.
B) If astrologers can believe in an unobserved body like 'al-kabid', then other unobserved bodies may also exist.
C) 'Al-kabid' is the only hidden body.
D) Astrologers should not be trusted.

Key Takeaway
The author uses the astrologers' acceptance of the unseen 'al-kabid' to argue that one cannot claim to have observed all celestial bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that astrologers accept an unseen body they cannot observe. Are there beliefs you hold that depend on unseen realities (like angels, the afterlife) that you accept, yet you sometimes dismiss other claims about the unseen simply because they are not verified by your senses? How will you ensure consistency in your acceptance of the unseen based on the method of revelation rather than personal bias?
?
The author uses the opponent's own premise against them. Is there an area in your life where you are inconsistent—where you accept something from one source but reject the same kind of evidence from another? Will you examine your own intellectual consistency today and repent where you have been unfair?
?
If even astrologers can admit that their knowledge is limited and that unseen bodies may exist, why do you sometimes act as if your own observation and reason are sufficient to judge all matters of the unseen? What specific claim about the unseen have you been dismissing without proper evidence, and will you now commit to seeking the truth from revelation?

The Fifth Difficulty: Uniformity of Zodiac Signs
Author argues that if the sphere is simple, all zodiac signs are identical, making stellar effects inexplicable by nature alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if the celestial sphere is a simple substance, then all zodiac signs must be identical in essence, which poses a difficulty for explaining stellar effects.

Core Idea
If the sphere is simple, the zodiac signs cannot differ—so why do planets act differently?

The fifth difficulty targets the astrological claim that planets have different effects in different zodiac signs. The argument is based on the philosophers' view that the celestial sphere is a simple substance. If that is true, then the signs cannot differ in essence, making it hard to attribute natural differences to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the argument is structured: a premise followed by an implication and an incomplete consequence.
The text is a fragment; it cuts off after mentioning 'the effect of the planet'.
2
Understand the premise
Identify the agreed-upon premise: the celestial sphere (الفلك) is a simple substance (جوهر بسيط). Why is this important?
Simplicity means no internal composition.
3
Trace the logical implication
From the premise, the author deduces that all zodiac signs must be equal in complete essence (تمام الماهية). Otherwise, the sphere would be composite.
4
Consider the unfinished argument
The text breaks off after 'تأثير الكوكب'. Think about what difficulty this poses: if the signs are identical, how can planetary effects vary?
This is the core problem for astrologers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: في بيان أن الأمر كما ذكرناه: إن الفلاسفة أطبقوا على أن الفلك جوهر بسيط، وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: يجب أن تكون طبائع البروج متساوية في تمام الماهية، وإلا لزم التركيب، وإذا ثبت هذا، فتأثير الكوكب
The fifth aspect: Explaining that the matter is as we have mentioned: The philosophers have agreed that the sphere is a simple substance. When this is established, we say: The natures of the zodiac signs must be equal in complete essence, otherwise composition would follow. When this is established, then the effect of the planet...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth difficulty, though it is incomplete. The author cites the philosophers' consensus that the celestial sphere (الفلك) is a simple substance (جوهر بسيط). If it is simple, then the twelve zodiac signs (البروج) must all be identical in essence (تمام الماهية); otherwise, the sphere would be composite, contradicting its simplicity. This leads to a problem for astrologers: if all zodiac signs are identical, then it is hard to explain why planets have different effects based on their positions in different signs. The argument suggests that our inability to fully understand the influence of stars is another difficulty in knowing celestial matters. The passage breaks off, but the intended conclusion is likely that stellar effects cannot be fully known due to these complexities.

Key Terms
الفلك
جوهر بسيط
طبائع البروج
تمام الماهية
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zodiac signs have different essences by nature.
Correction: If the sphere is simple, all signs must be identical in essence, so their differences cannot be inherent.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth aspect, what follows if the celestial sphere is a simple substance?
A) The zodiac signs have different natures.
B) The zodiac signs must be equal in essence.
C) The planets have no effect.
D) The sphere is composite.

Key Takeaway
If the celestial sphere is a simple substance, then all zodiac signs must be identical in essence, making it difficult to explain different stellar effects by natural causes alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever attributed different powers to zodiac signs or believed in astrological influences, how does this argument challenge the assumption that the stars have inherent, differing natures? Are you willing to re-examine that belief in light of the necessity of God's simplicity?
?
This argument relies on a premise that philosophers agreed the sphere is simple. Do you accept that premise simply because 'they agreed,' or do you investigate the evidence yourself? Where else in your faith are you trusting human consensus without personal verification?
?
If the sphere were not simple but composed, would that make it easier to explain stellar effects? Yet the philosophers insisted on simplicity. What does this tell you about the limits of human reason in understanding the heavens? How does that affect your trust in God's wisdom over your own?
